The Red Page
Digger Verses
Old Mb. Lark.
Hay! ain’t the lurk a dinky bird,
The way the beggar sings t
Be dde&n't care a tinker’s cuss
For all the stuff Fritz flings.
He lives up in the trenches there,
He doesn't mind the noise;
He’s just a friendly little bloke —
Good cobbers with the boys.
And when the “ Minnies ” tioist and twirl,
And “ rumjars” bob and bust,
While “ five-point-nines” and, other “ fruit”
Stir up the bloomin’ dust,
It’s then I feels Fd like to be
Well up above sich things,
Like Mr. Lark, who kruncs what’s what,
And climbs upstairs and sings!
And when the dawn is rosy pink,
And I am standin’-to,
A-leanin ’ on the sandbags, ’cos
There’s nothinn else to do,
Old Mr. Lark he goes aloft
And trills his little song,
And somehow, while I listens there,
I feels me heart grow strong.
I takes a lesson from that bird:
The trenches ain’t so bad —
I feels I’ve been a thankless cove
To grouse and bite like mad!
I makes me mind up there and then
To take what Fate may bring,
Instead of cussin’ all the day,
Like Mr. Lark, I’ll sing.
C. It. Ayling.
Leave.
I want to stroll down Bond-street —
Lord, what memories it brings!
I want to see shop windows
Full of flimsy, useless things,
Rosy pink and pale blue mysteries —
You know the kind I mean.
(Are boudoir caps still in fashion?
Do they still wear crSpe de chine?)
I long for Piccadilly
And its crowds of lovely girls,
With their neat silk-stockinged ankles,
And their captivating curls,
With their thin, delicious blouses,
Dreams of silk and filmy net.
(Are pink nighties now in fashion?
Or is* it crepe Georgette?)
I dearly want to saunter
Along by Leicester Square,
And wateh with fascination
The many gay sights there.
Maybe I’ll see these visions
When next on leave I go,
And if I do, Old Thing, be sure,
rn write and let you know.
C. Baker.
The Ordnance.
Who are. the boys behind the line
Who get good food, including wine,
Who have no cause to grouse and whine?
The Ordnance!
Who are the men who dole out clothes
When not at work on curios,
And still get in their noonday doze?
The Ordnance!
At whom do neio chums laugh and grin
As they pass by in hats of tin,
Asking which of the leaves we’re in?
The Ordnance!
But don’t forget we fought the Turk,
And did our share in gallant work;
We fought disease, we fought the thirst
Of Anzac days—a thing accurst —
And didn’t come with the Thirty-first!
The Ordnance!
Benobi.
From “New Zealand a lie Front” (Cassell).
.fit
The lordly volume, New Zealand at
the Front, written and illustrated by
men of the M.L. Division (Cassell), deserves
a high place among trench publica-
tions. As illustrator, poet, story-writer and
humorist Harrimac, Billjim’s Maorfiland
brother, can hold his own with the Aussie, as
Harrimac terms Billjim. Owing to the diffi-
culties of publication and transport the
material for this characteristic and delightful
book had to be procured at short notice, pre-
cluding contributions from that considerable
section of crusading Diggers somewhere
around Jerusalem. The Maoriland language
is receiving some remarkable accessions from
this war: a necessary glossary is kindly pro-
vided —chiefly for the English civilian. The
training-ground is the bull-ring; the Dinks
is the term for that part of the M.L. Army
which rather fancies itself—a derivative of
dinkum. A cigarette of the commoner variety
is a gasper. Food, of course, is kai and a
potato reicai. Pakaru means broken,
smashed; poriro means “of unknown origin,
not in Debrett’s. The English form is used
as an expletive or term of endearment, as the
occasion demands.” Then there is the useful
Maori taihoa, “later on,” and the invaluable
plurry. “the great Australian adjective ex-
pressed in the more mellifluous language of
the Maori.” And there is the useful expres-
sion, stopping a blast, which means receiv-
ing a rebuke or something more cyclonic from
your superior in rank. The velocity of the
blast depends upon the state of your
superior’s liver.
There are excellent illustrations in color
by Thompson, E. F. Hiscocks, Finey, W. F.
Bell, and others; while in black-and-white
there is brilliant work by F. H. Cumber-
worth, G. P. Hanna, and a number of Digger
Bairnsfathers; while Finey is to be congratu-
lated for his numerous apt studies of the
Maoriland private. He has drawn the typi-
cal Maorilander without flattering him —a
nuggety bloke, muscular, hefty, phlegmatic,,
well-fed, ingenuous, cheerful and young, with
an obvious preference for his flat-brimmed,
[leaked, four-dgnted Mount Egmont hat.
The steel hat makes him look like any othter
bloke, and the Maorilander prefers to look
like himself, especially when there are any
Aussies about.
The letterpress is blithe and clever, but
hardly audacious; this Digger is only auda-
cious over the top, and even then he takes
his job verra seriously. This page likes the
conclusion of H. T. B. Drew’s yarn about a
Digger who revenged his cobber’s death by
stalking and bayoneting the German sniper.
“ ’Ere’s ’is cap and ’is badges,” he reported
on his return; then added, “’E groaned
lovely!”
What is Poetry ?
Henry Newbolt, in A New Study of English
Poetry (Constable, per Dymock’s Library,
145.), obliges with a definition of poetry.
Some of the preliminary questions he essays
to answer are: Is the essential part of
poetry the subject, the diction, the ver.silica-
tion or some peculiar quality ot emotion .
Is the highest poetry personal or impersonal,
subjective or objective, the product ot expen-
ence or invention .
Let’s begin at the beginning. . .
“Art, poetry, is common to the race ; it is
the natural activity of every man. . . Art is
simply expression: the act of the human
spirit seizing upon a sensation and giving
form to it.” But what is the use of poetry,
what is the pleasure or satisfaction derived
from it? This differs in the case_ of the
maker of poetry and the reader of it. The
poet’s object is expression, and having ex-
pressed himself perfectly he has his reward.
But the public come to the poet for some-
thing that will help them to a fuller life: ho
must excellently express feelings such as
they can understand and value. But the
poet has no end to seTve except his art itself.
(That is probably why poets usually have
to borrow traybits from the public.) But
the divorce is not absolute y for every man
looks beyond his life to a recreating of his
world; there are two worlds to which
every man simultaneously belongs, -his own
and the perfect world he longs for. And it
is here that the public reach over to the
poet: they find in the poet those pictures of
perfection of which they merely dream.
Poetry, then, is “the expression of the
aesthetic or intuitive activity”; the prosaic
is “the expression of the intellectual or logi-
cal activity of the fruman spirit.” “A work
of art in words is always poetry: a work of
science, in whatever form of words, would
always be prose.” So, in Newbolt’s logical
judgment, a good novel may be poetry, for
at bottom it is “a vast and complex intuition
amounting to a vision of a neiv world of
human life created by the novelist’s imagina-
tion.” But strictly poetry is always asso-
ciated with rhythm. And metre is not a
kind of decoration applied to poetry, as it
were,; externally, and to be judged by an
external standard. “The vast majority of
the well-educated have, in the very process
of their education, been impressed with the
belief that metre is an arrangement of lan-
guage which can be judged by the applica-
n a mechanical test, and that the poet
who produces a line which does not answer
to the test is a fit subject for correction by
SK
“ I* than we were - there is a public which
at the reviewer who
dec j ares tlmt a line ‘will not scan’ or that
con t a j n g a ‘trochee’ where it should have
had an < iamb , without considering whether
it wag ever intended to ‘scan,’ or whether
tkere j s an ything in English verse which can
be treated \ s t he absolute equivalent of a
r ,ToUt, ”
reek 01 Jjatm tloctiea -
What is rhythm? The most significant
thing about rhythm is that it seems to be
always present where there is life. If
rhythm is of essence of vitality, if it
pulses through us like our blood, it is not
surprising that it should appear in human
speech, and especially in poetic speech, in
which we live most intensely. Poetry is in
its origin more akin to dancing than to song,
“Rhythm is before song.... The descent of
poetry is this: the rhythmic instinct of life
begot the movement of dancing: dancing
was accompanied and regulated by the beat-
ing of the measure in monotone: and this'
drum-rhythm was then impressed upon lan-
guage.” But a perfectly regular rhythm
could not be impressed upon language", Im-
cause language already had a rhythm of its
own, necessary to its meaning aiid therefore
persistent. In poetry, Newbolt boldly de-
dares, there is no such thing as perfect or
rigid rhythm. “What there has always
been is an antagonism, a balance, a compro-
mise, between the metrical ictus, the drum-
beat which I have imagined, £nd the com-
mon speech-rhythm of the language. Poetic
rhythm is, in short, neither strict mechanical
rhythm nor free speech-rhythm.” It is a
compromise. It is true that Tennyson
revelled in the regular mechanical hoof-beat;
but in more masculine verse it is compara-
tively rare. “Most of the beauty of The lines
and all their variety is gained by the skill
with which the woof of speech-rhythm is
continually thrown athwart the warp of
the metrical type. A master hand like
Swinburne’s will throw the shuttle 100 times
find produce no less than 50 different effects.
All but one of these are departures from the
strict type.”
Then why keep up the pretence? Why not
seek out the most beautiful natural speech-
rhytlim and use that, instead of pretending
to submit it to a metrical type? Newbolt
doesn’t know. His only answer is that there
must be some artificial restraint; though he
comes nearer to an answer when he adds
that without this restraint of verse-metre
the hearer loses the pleasure of rhythm,
“of having a certain expectation satisfied,
though it is never satiated.” The pattern of
regular beats is seldom actually present in
the verse but always existing in the memory
of the reader or hearer. Robert Bridges
asserts that the same thing was character-
istic of Greek and Latin poetry. The “quan-
tity” of which we hear so much from clas-
sical scholars, was, after all, an arbitrary
convention. “For both Greeks and Latins,
though they wrote by quantity, continued to
speak mainly, as we do, b jy stress; and
neither tried to arrange the words in the
line so that the speech-stress should coin-
cide precisely with the metrical ictus.” It
is exactly analogous to the warp-and-woof
effect in English blank verse. And on the
break-up of the Roman Empire the law of
quantity perished: only stress remained, the
stress of common speech.
J*
Rime was introduced into England by
Norman minstrels after the Conquest; but
the Anglo-Saxons have for centuries had a
prosody of their own, stress, with
alliteration added. The struggle between
the two systems lasted for centuries. It was
Coleridge who asked whether the hypocrisy
of pretending to conform strictly with metre
was worth keeping up: why not write solely
in stress prosody? But it was the present
Poet Laureate who first properly used this
principle of stress-verse. Newbolt quotes:—
When men lucre all asleep the snow came
flying
In large ivhite flakes falling on the city
brown;
Stealthily and perpetually settling and
loosely lying,
Hushing the latest traffic of the droiosy
town.
Deadening, muffling, stifling its murmurs
failing;
Lazily . and incessantly floating down and
down;
Silently sifting and veiling road, roof and
railings ;
Hiding difference, making unevenness even,
Into angles and crevices softly drifting and
sailing.
It will be seen that this stanza is built upon
the formula of five stresses —five natural
speech-stres :es—in each line. But stress verse
is not true to its name if it fails to stress
syllables that would be stressed in speech.
And here it is that Bridges fails —and New-
bolt neglects to catch the Laureate out. The
second line quoted above has six stresses, not
five. The stressed syllables are: large,
ivhite, flakes, fall-, cir, brown. So in the
seventh line there are also six stresses: si-, •.
sift-, veil-, road, roof, rail-. Apparently
Bridges decided to omit the stress respect-
ively upon white and roof; but in stress
verse that can’t be done. In other Bridges
examples quoted by Newbolt the same error
is apparent.
Newbolt justly asks for the- recognition of
two principles and their constant application
in judging poetry. Poetic rhythm is not an
applied ornament, the fit subject of a pedan-
tic criticism. And since poetry is a personal
expression, the natural tendency of poetic
rhj thm will be towards perpetual change.
And Newbolt believes that our modern poets
not only are determined to be no longer hin-
dered by old conventions of diction, of “scan-
ning,” of unnatural stress and ungrammati-
cal inversion, but that they p.re right in their
endeavor.
So, after a study of “Poetry and Person-
ality” and “Poetry and Politics”, we reach
this definition of Poetry: “Poetry is the ex-
pression in speech, more or less rhythmical,
of the aesthetic activity of the human spirit,
the creative activity by which the world is
presented to our consciousness. Good poetry
is not merely the expression of our intui-
tions. It is the masterly expression of rare,
complex and difficult states of consciousness;
and great poetry is the expression of our con-
sciousness of this world, tinged with man’s
universal longing for a world more perfect,
nearer to the heart’s desire.” Later on, New-
bolt adds that great poetry is also the mas-
terly expression “of intuitions in which the
highest thought is fused with simple percep-
tions, until both togetner become a new
emotion.” Hm ! Doesn't help us much....
And even then Newbolt makes the addendum
that though the artistic expression is for the
poet an end in itself, the making of a work
of art has a different motive; it implies an
audience. The poet who says: “I don’t care
whether people read my poems or not ; I
write for myself, and not for the public,” is
confusing two acts in one. His emotion vyas
his own, and expressed for his own satisfac-
tion ; but for whom did he write it, print it,
publish it and send it to be reviewed ?
Newbolt is amusing in his description of
the process by which a poet becomes a
classic. Usually he must wait for that
crowning distinction until he is quite dead ;
otherwise he might break out in some unex-
pected poetic direction and upset all the
critics. This Page has long held a similar
opinion. Our recipe for becoming a classic
may thus be expressed : Any fairly persistent
poet will become a major or minor classic
provided lie manages to keep alive long
enough. P>y the time he is 72 lie is for all
practical purposes dead, sufficiently dead,,
anyhow, to settle his place on the card-index
of fame. Of co rse, he mustn’t take any
risks of disappointing or surprising his pub-
lic by breaking, out in some new direction, by
allowing his brain to develop past his first
manner and method and outlook. The public
expects its popular poet to limit the line of
his development by his earliest successful
performance ; lie must continually repeat his.
subject and the treatment by which he made
his reputation. He must never peer out of
his pigeon-hole. If a persistent poet is care-
ful, by the time he is 72, he has become a
sort of literary legend ; he is too dead to have
anything new up his sleeve; he can be de-
pended upon to repeat himsfclf; his worst
verses will have been learnt by heart, and
hated, by every school-child of two genera-
tions; lbs “works” will have appeared in a
collected edition ; he will have got into all
the anthologies and all the Who's Whos: the
Melbourne laterary Cdub will have discussed
and settled him ; his birthday will annually
appear in the monthly paragraph in Tiib
Bulletin ; his obituary will be filed in every
newspaper “morgue”.; people will point out
his bald head at public luncheons of the
Millions Club; Norman Carter will have
painted bis portrait for the Gallery; George
Taylor will have published reminiscences of
him and himself in their bohemian youth;
nobody will read him, but everybody will have
heal’d of him ; and his most frayed quotations-
from his Collected Jobs will be misquoted in
the editorials of The Sydney Morning
Herald. . •
And when he die? —in his pigeon-hole—the
public, after having got over their impression
that he died years before, will proclaim him
a classic ; publishers who had always, and
rightly, shied from printing his unprofitable
poems, will rush to bring out an authorita-
tive edition ; Bertram Stevens will write his
biography, take up a subscription to bury
the old bloke, and insist upon the Federal
Government paying a pension to bis widow.
Anybody can do it if he only lives long
enough and obeys the rules.
J*
A New Study of English Poetry is highly
interesting. Newbolt has much to say about
Chaucer. Shakespeare, Milton, British Bal-
lads and Futurism in poetry; and he says it
brightly and well. :
»
The Nobel Prize for Literature has this
year been awarded to Denmark, apparently
as a sort of consolation prize, on the prin-
ciple that it is one of the few countries in
Europe that have hitherto escaped the honor.
The £BOOO w r as divided between Karl
Gjellerup and Hendik Pontoppidan, but the
latter refused his share, so that Gjellerup
mops up the lot. These two are the most
popular Danish novelists surviving from an
earlier generation. Pontoppidan is the lead-
ing living novelist of Denmark, almost a
local Balzac, but in the judgment of London
Times below the rank of the great names m
European fiction. After an enthusiasm for
English poetry, Gjellerup was seduced by
the seriousness of German Kultur, emigrated
to Saxony, married a German wife and has
been practically a German for the last oO
years. The Times suggests that it was not
his genius that induced the Nobel Com-
mittee to bracket his name with Pontoppidan
-—his warmest admirers would not rank him
with Anatole France or Thomas Hardy, and
there are in Danish literature several names
more eminent —but the fact that he is the
one Danish writer who is profoundly
fibsorbed by the spirit of Germany. If Aus-
tralia was a starter in the race, she was an
also-ran.

| OVEN UNDERWEAR is one
j of the many commodities in every-
-day demand that have been
materially affected by war-time con-
ditions. Prices have already advanced
to such an extent that the tax on
the average purse has become
extremely heavy. And if indications
are not misread the upward tendency
in cost has not reached its limit.
Anthony Horderns’ policy at all
times is to pass on to patrons the
benefits accruing from advantageous
purchases, and not to seek for an
increase in price until higher costs
compel an advance.
The big stocks of Women’s and Child-
ren’s Underwear, in Wool, Wool and
Cotton, and Silk, ordered for last
Winter, but delivered quite recently,
were purchased at prices considerably
below to-day’s values in London,
America, and at the Continental sources
of supply; indeed, some of the makes
are to-day almost unobtainable.
Purchasing now the goods that were
bought under such favourable condition's '
is advice that discriminating people will
not overlook.
We reiterate the statement—
these goods could not be
duplicated to-day.

The Lighting Problem of those who live
away from the big cities has been effec-
tively solved by the Lalley Light, which
makes electric light and power imme-
diately available to anyone, anywhere !
The Lalley Lighting system is a small,
compact light and power plant, con-
sisting of electric generator, directly
driven by a patented, ball-bearing
benzine engine and a set of storage
batteries.
Lalley Light is to day furnishing hun-
dreds of country homes with brilliant,
convenient, safe and economical light.
For Suburban Homes, where munici-
pal or other current is unobtainable, a
“Lalley” Plant will satisfactorily meet
all your requirements, as well as a
dozen domestic conveniences, such as
electric irons, washing machines,
vacuum cleaners, electric fans, etc.
It is effective for all lighting problems,

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Soldier.
He felt the dew upon his brow and wept
Till the salt tears along his cheek-wounds
crept.
He bit his lips and cursed the arching skies
Choked with his crying, but he could not rise.
“More men, more .men !” he heard the ser-
geant yell;
He strained up, moaning, but in torture fell,
And like a child he clawed the clammy earth
Helpless as dust from which lie had his birth.
“More men!” He shrieked, and with a
stinging pride
Felt for his rifle at his bleeding side.
The trigger slipped beneath his dying hand
And a strange mist seemed moving o’er the
land.
Cries of his comrades sounded far away;
The booming guns like billows of a bay
Roared through the murky madness of bis
mind,
A song of hate till all his heart was blind.
“Another man ! Another man !” he heard.
His filmy eyes in stony anguish stirred.
Teetli-clenched he screamed in triumphed
agony,
Rising at last upon his broken knee ;
And, drunk with torture of his Death’s
desire,
Died with his rifle rattling into fire.
N.S.W.
V.C.
Wise!
Only there in the sea of passion
We touched tne edge with our fearing feet,
Dipped and withdrew, and dipped again,
Bewaring joy just as much as pain
And dreading tides too fleet;
Stepped back where the cold grey shoreland
lies,
Then sought the sea with our longing eyes!
For it was so blue in the lilting weather.
And the sun seemed, rocked in it half asleep,
And the ripple voices conspired together
To cry, “Come forth where the tides are
deep.”
But we, alas! we had grown so wise,
We saw the foam where the black rock lies.
Inland now, in the level places,
I hear the wind in the swaying tree
And its minor note in the summer grasses,
I think of a moon-drawn flood that passes—
Till the wfind seems like the sea
And its salt wave touches my aching eyes.
lam sorry now that we were so wise!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The Kiss.
Whenas in fragrance, blossomwise,
Your fingers dream against my lips,
Out of a tired heart the longing slips
For charity into your dear, brown eyes.
There is it hid in faith, buried in sweet,
As in warm cloud the happy angel feet.
And from afar my own eyes watch your
mouth.
Grace o’ the noontide knows your eyes,
Sweet o’ the dusk your shadowed lips.
Bloom o’ the dawn your heart. In faery ships/
Rocked on old seas forgot, dim dreams
arise—
Old woes forgot grow gradually sweet —
Fade in your eyes, as fade bright qngel
feet.
And afar off, athirst, I watch your mouth.
Old midnights red ’neath darker skies
Cold Memory in her poison dips;
And from the tired heart in mercy strips
All the old pain and sin and dear, far lies;
Grey noon and ashen rose, all fades; more
fleet
Than angel smiles —than unseen angel
feet.i. .
All fades in tears, upon your mouth —-
your mouth.
N.S.W.
HILARY LOFTING.
The Mouse.
All Christmas night upon the shelf,
Among the apples yellow-faced,
There played a pretty maiden mouse
Divinely slim and very chaste,
Who, when I held my candle up,
Did twink her little eyes at me....
So mad, so bright, so mischievous,
I thought of you, dear Dorothy!
VICTORIA.
HUGH McCRAE.
Holiday.
I know not if my love be true;
But this I know, the sky is blue;
There’s still a butterfly or two
To coquette with the sun;
The gum-trees blowing greyly green
Against the hoyden clouds are seen,
The day is made for fun.
And oh, I’m merry with the mirth
That shakes the jocund, fruitful earth
And ripples through his Autumn girth
Into the dancing sea ;
The wine-fraught winds toss streaming hair
Across the eyes of haggard Care,
The world wags merrily.
I know not if my love be true —
An were he false why make to do?
Brown eyes speak just as sweet as blue
To every mother’s son;
And laughter and much merriment
By God into the woi’ld were sent —
Let’s drink our pint of fun.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.

THURSDAY, MARCH 28, 1918.
CHANGING HORSES.
“Never change horses while crossing a stream,
especially if there isn’t a horse to change to.” —
Solomon Malaphof’s Booh of Wisdom. .
A hundred and five years ago the Great
Napoleon (not to be confused with the Small
Napoleon who came after him and in no
way resembled his illustrious alleged uncle
save in the shortage of his legs) travelled
to Moscow and back, and lost his army, his
greatness and his empire. Pie never got to
Petrograd or anywhere near it. But in
those days there were no electric telegraphs,
no telephones (there couldn’t be, for P.M.G.
Webster wasn’t born), no tramway sys-
tems, no long-range artillery, machine-guns,
magazine-rifles, dynamite or other high ex-
plosives, no railway trains or motor-cars to
hurry troops and supplies to the Front, no
airships to locate the enemy, no poison-gas
—in fact, everything was slow and primi-
tive. Under those conditions Moscow
became a name of terror, and the vast dis-
tances looked like an impregnable wall of
defence. Now the distances don’t count
for much, and if the Huns could spare
more troops from the French and Italian
Fronts it looks as if the occupation of
Russia’s two capitals would be a mere
promenade. There is some kind of a dis-
organised army in the way, but disorganised
armies count for little these days, especially
in a flat country, and more especially when
the Government behind them is flat and dis-
organised, too.
* * * *
Amid a welter of printed confusion and
a mass of tattered information a few facts
stand out. Russia had a revolution, and
it was a well-meaning upheaval with great
ideas about freedom and the rights of man.
The old autocrats and aristocrats were due
for extinction. Their system was old, cor-
rupt, brutal, ignorant, narrow-minded, short-
sighted, greedy and, as events proved, more
hollow than it looked. Still it was a fight-
ing force not to be despised, and when the
country was struggling for its existence
against a vastly superior enemy, and one
that was even more brutal than the old
tyranny, it- was a hazardous proceeding to
break the ancient machine without having a
new one to put in its place. Still the war
offered the new men what looked like a
chance, and they seized it. Apparently a
section of the “respectable” elements of the
nation gave it a measure of support, having
no idea of what would come after. Also
it seems that there was a rally of cranks
who are numerous in every country—Peace
Society folk, anti-conscriptionists, free sil-
ver men, bi-metallists, single-taxers, anar-
chists, communists, Seventh Day Adventists,
Freetraders, Fifth Monarchy men, believers
in the Beast of Revelation, passive resisters,
unlimited-paper currency men, the I.WAV.:
these or their local equivalents were natu-
rally all on deck. Then there were old
criminals released from gaol, new criminals
just starting business, released political
offenders with bitter grievances to avenge,
people who were thirsty (the Czardom hav-
ing recently cut off the drink supply), people
who were hungry and others. The ill-paid
and illiterate rank and file of the army had
the millennium offered to it in tons, and
the era of peace and brotherly love and pro-
found calm was lying around in .heaps for
anybody to pick up. There was so much
peace and calm that a great part of the
force simply laid down its guns, which the
Germans promptly picked up, and went
home. A great deal more was formally dis-
banded because, in the blessed prospective era
of love and happiness, an army would be
superfluous, and the Germans gathered up its
dropped gun also. The fact that a vast pro-
portion of the old officers were dead, or were
reduced to the ranks, or were fleeing for
their lives, or were in gaol, and that an
obscure ensign was now commander-in-chief
with suddenly elevated ex-privates as his
subordinates, facilitated these movements.
A Czar of the almighty variety might have
suppressed the revolution in its beginnings.
A weak Czar, who was so ill-served that he
was trotting peacefully back to his capital
in blank ignorance of the fact that he no
longer had a capital, had no chance.
* * * *
A feeble autocrat may reign all his days
unless an unprecedented brick strikes him,
and may die at last in his bed and in the
purple. His name, his ancestors, the old
bureaucratic machine and the old habit of
obedience all help to keep him going. A
revolutionary leader who has gone the whole
hog in uprooting the old arrangement of
things must make a complete new machine,
and he should have the brains and courage
of 20 ordinary Czars for the work. Also
when there is bitter war outside, and insur-
rection, mutiny, impecuniosity, disorganisa-
tion and secession within, he must be a man
of few scruples. He requires to be heavy
of hand and swift on his feet. Not having
tradition behind him, or force of ancient
habit, or the brutal and well-organised
bureaucracy, he must have ways of his own
for making it clear that whoever blasphemes
against the republic, or conspires against it,
or tries to secede from it, or is in any way
actively hostile to the brotherhood of man
might as, well have publicly put dynamite
under a Grand Duke in other days. And he
requires also to have ways of preventing
the new order being used too extensively as
a cloak for burglary, arson, private homicide,
forgery, bigamy, blackmail and other old
offences. A very strong man with a large
force of hefty and reliable subordinates of
his own selection may carry the undertaking
through. But with an almost overwhelming
foreign enemy at the gates, there is little
time to spare for mere theory, however
beautiful the theory may be.
* * * *
Apparently the Russian leaders sat too
much in offices, dreamed too many dreams,
and had too much faith in other people’s good
intentions. They had an idea that the German
—the boss tyrant and supreme liar of the
world —would be allowed to sympathise with
them because they had upset the Czar who
made war on Germany. They visioned that
the Hun bureaucracy would, on that same
account, preserve a semblance of kindliness
towards people who were even then calling
on the German masses to go and do like-
wise. The fact that the Chief Huns would
rather see their autocratic enemy back on
the throne than lend countenance to a
movement for dispensing with autocrats was
possibly beyond their well-meaning compre-
hension. Certainly they held out the hand
of fellowship to the Hun, and that person
looked at it with tolerance. He didn’t hasten
to take it, but he seemed to imply that it
wasn’t a bad sdrt of paw and was worthy
of consideration. While he waited, and did
as little as possible to scare the enemy into
a great rally for the national defence, the
Muscovite army to a great extent melted
away; discipline became a back number; the
empire fell to pieces through the secession
of one portion and another, which, amid con-
cealed Hun applause, and at concealed Hun
instigation, set up for themselves; the dis-
order grew apace because the two men who
sat so much in offices were unequal to the
occasion; and famine, which hadn’t been
far off before, stalked through the land
with long legs and seven-league boots,
for few people cared to sow when
there was such little chance of reaping.
The treasury at Petrograd became a blank,
for nobody save robbers collected much in
the way of taxes and there was very little
to collect in the Bolshevik part of Russia.
The public debt was repudiated, which made
small difference to anybody, for it was dead
already; yet formal repudiation was a silly
way of rubbing it in and a needless piece
of conversation. The Government paper be-
came of almost invisible importance. The
corridors of the official building, where Tkot-
sky and Lenin, the two chief theorists, sat
too much in offices, began to be unswept;
the bells got out of order; the office-boy
didn’t come at call; and the untidy Guards
on duty lolled at casual angles and spat into
the coal-scuttle. About this time the Hun put
a lump of ice and a dead serpent into the ex-
tended hand of fellowship and announced his
long-delayed terms. They were practically
the disbanding of the. remnant of the Russian
army; the indefinite —which means perma-
nent—occupation by the, Hun of much Rus-
sian territory ; the occupation of more terri-
tory if the Ilun feels inclined ; and a stu-
peudous indemnity. Then the Hun got a
hustle on and resumed the invasion.
* * * *
It has been rumored that one Russian boss
theorist wanted to accept the Hun terms or
any terms, while the other boss dreamer
didn't; but neither fact seems of much
account. Whether they were bought by Ilun
gold or whether they just happened to do
Hun service by accident matters very little.
All that matters is that Russia, with an ill-
administered and incompetent programme of
all-round friendship, appears to be worse off
than it was under no friendship at all. It is
New Australia on a stupendous scale, with
the handicaps of diverse languages and
nationalities, dense ignorance, a legacy of
evil traditions and a foreign war. Its
probable fate, unless the Allies are able
to save the situation, is the establish-
ment of a new Czar, a Ilun puppet ruling in
name over a mere fragment of the old domin-
ion ; and the restoration of the nefarious
and corrupt and ill-mannered bureaucrats,
with still more evil and uncivil German
bureaucrats over them, the bond between the
two gangs being their common wickedness.
When it was discovered that the defences of
Petrograd itself were crippled, German
agents having prigged the essential parts of
the guns right under the eyes of the kind-
hearted rulers, and despite their Guards and
their new police force, and their programme
and their ofiice-cleaner and their waste-paper
basket —when this was ascertained an addi-
tional gloom fell on the scene. The Trot-
sky crowd had been looking afar off at the
Regeneration of Man, and things had been
happening right between its feet.
WALLS.
Walls are the emblems of despotism.
Never yet has real liberty raised a great
building or erected a city of massive stone.
Such big works as have been created under
so-called democracy have always been carried
out by something within democracy which
was autocratic or despotic in character.
Ancient Home built magnificent palaces in
the days of the Empire and the foundations
of ruins show still more tremendous things
dating from the very early period of the
kings; but the buildings constructed during
the stalwart period of the Republic were
for the most part ordinary or even insig-
nificant. There was a terrific building boom
in Jerusalem under the despotism of Solo-
mon, and a still bigger one under the abso-
lute rule of Herod the Great; but the
comparatively free Israel of the days when
only a High Priest was in power did little
beyond mending the holes in the city wall
and erecting a makeshift temple which was
but a shadow of the magnificent one that
had gone before. The sky-scraper of New
York, too, marks the rise of the despotism
of the trusts; for the real buildings of free
America are the log hut in the forest and
the shack on the mountain slopes of the
West. Not only are walls the emblems of
despotic rule, but the comparative thick-
ness and savage strength of them are an
indication of the extent and nature of the
evil thing. In England the Norman built
tremendous fortresses of stone, into which he
crowded with horse and herd and man-at-
arms and knight and dainty lady, until the
stench of his castle rose in a defiant blast
to Heaven, proclaiming the might of the
splendid noble who could endure all that
reek. In Scotland, on the other hand, a
simple tower was regarded as sufficient for
defence; and the horses and cows and sew-
age and the bulk of the retainers stayed
outside. And Scotland was just as much
freer than England during the Middle Ages
as such a comparison of walls would indi-
cate. When the Paris revolutionary mob
rose in its fury of despair and pulled the
front out of the Bastile, it was led by sure
instinct. The place where the walls were
thickest was exactly the place where des-
potism was most firmly entrenched.
* * * *
As our so-called civilisation has grown
the nature of the'walls has changed. No
longer does the massive fortress of stone
frown over the edge of the precipice to
strike terror into the hearts of the valley
dwellers below; but a multiplicity of walls
has taken the place of the ancient structures,
and the crowded suburbs of endless brick
and the packed and blackened factories
shout against the liberties of man in exactly
the tones of the older castle. It may be
that walls to some extent are necessary;
but even the walls of the cottage in its green
garden speak too much of the despotism of
custom. Where the free air should enter
there is only a small window, and the doors
are §hut tight against the possible in-
truder. It is all a legacy of the past.
The proverb which declared “an English-
man’s house is his castle” carried down the
idea from the day when Plantagenet knights
lived in stone vaults and protected them-
selves when they rode abroad with walls of
steel armor. The alleged freedom of
England has largely been an extension
of the privilege of erecting walls to
an ever-widening circle of self-imprisoned
citizens. Every wall, like every barred
door, stands for distrust, for want of faith
in the inherent decency of the race of man.
Much modern wall-building is, of course,
merely formal and intended by the unimagi-
native builders to be in some way decora-
tive ; but even extensive decoration of the
kind has an imprisoning effect. The street
with its pride of carved stones shuts out the
good sun and casts a shadow of some sort
upon every heart within it. Trees, open
spaces, waving flowers, the fair view to the
sea, large gardens about low walls—these
are the things that speak always of free-
dom. But the city must always have in it
the elements of despotism, and the higher
and closer its buildings the more that des-
potism grips. Even if the buildings soar
to meet the alleged conveniences of trade
and commerce, those conveniences ?-*. them-
selves despotic. The by-law of life which
insists upon a man going up and down in a
lift is as arbitrary as the force of an auto-
cratic empire that compels a man to fight
for a cause he hates. Freedom must mean
personal liberty of action ; and there is no
such liberty when a brick wall is three feet
from the elbow and the nearest green and
growing thing half a mile away.
* * * *
The movements for the liberation of man
mast be movements for the tearing down of
walls. The world’s needs are chiefly air and
sunshine—mental and moral as well as
natural. The Romans, like the Chinese,
built vast walls across great sweeps of coun-
try to keep back the enemy on the other
side; and though, in a physical sense, such
breaks are not used to-daJ% the arbitrary poli-
tical boundaries of States are just as really
walls as if they were of stone. In point of
fact, the dividing walls of Europe are the
cause of the present war. The balance of
power, the greed of territorial expansion and
a hundred other things all intimately con-
nected with such walls have been the excuse
or explanation of most of the blood shed in.
a thousand years of battle. To smash dowri*
such walls and smash them down forever
should be the object of the Allied nations.
Boundaries for administration there must
be. but boundaries for national glory and
imperial pride are not worth the loss of one
human life. Once break down the wall which
purely artificial systems have created and
mankind can breathe freely. A Frenchman
does not reallv hate a German as a man, nor
has a Bulgarian any fixed desire to slaughter
a Roumanian. The national quarrels are
in every case quarrels over boundaries or
legacies from previous quarrels about boun-
daries. Let the peoples be brought face to
face, into kindly touch with one anpther, and
all their antipathy is no more than the
rivalry of adjacent parishes. Behind the
wall the foe seems an unpleasant person, but
met in the open fields —often even in the open
fields of battle —he is a good sort with
laughter on his tongue and an honest desire
to be friendly. And all this liberation of
man from the shadow and curse of walls is a
comparatively easy matter. With good local
government and a loose universal federation
of peoples for police purposes there is no
reason why any nolitical wall which cannot
be easily stepped over by the least agile
citizen should exist at all. An old Scripture
advises us not to despise the day of small
things ; and the future would certainly seem
to be with the territorially small things. But
because those small things may be real things
they are in the best sense the great things.
Empires like the one which has just fallen
in Russia, and like those that are tottering
in Austria and Germany, have never been
great things because they never had a foun-
dation of reality. Let the walls come down.
There will still be sunshine and good air
despite all the graves and the pollution of
the Ion? and bitter war.

What Denison Miller Didn’t Say.
Thus Denison Miller. Governor of the
Commonwealth Bank, giving, as in duty
bound, a leg-up to the War Loan: —
Australia is at present enjoying an exceptionally
prosperous period, as high prices are being received
for our primary products, many of which have,
through the liberality of the British Government,
been paid for though they have not yet been shipped.
The moneys which have been received in this way
percolate through the primary producers to all classes
of the community, with the result that the Aus-
tralian public now have as much, if not more, money
at their disposal than at any time in our history.
Had it not been for the advantageous arrangements
made with the British Government regarding the pay-
ment for our products, the financial position in Aus-
Easter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Blessed the dead who, fighting, die
Victorious in the blood and mire,
In faith that the triumphant cry
Of life new-born comes from the fire:
Life that shall mould the world afresh!
What though tornadoes round them roll
And thunders burst their quaking fiesh!
They die with undivided soul,
Blessed are they and doubly blest!
No troubled dreams shall rend the mind
That sees upon the good world’s breast
Evil in one gross shape confined,
And seeing, strikes, and, striking, falls.
’Twas thus the happy warriors passed,
Believing Hell’s embattled walls
Fell at their final trumpet-blast.
O sacred dead in lands apart!
How can we hope to catch your glow
Who watch with a divided heart
The changing tides of battle flow?
Who see the foe in friendly guise,
And tremble at the flames that seem
Unguessed from secret pits to rise
And waste the cities of our dream?
The sky is dark with sombre wings,
And danger lurks behind, before;
To-day’s half-vanquished evil springs
To life behind to-morrow’s door.
E’en friendship wears a double face!
Only the simple heart made blind
Can turn his back on the loved place,
And front his foe with single mind.
Yet it was well with Him who paid
The price on sad Golgotha’s hill,
Seeing the powers of darkness fade,
And a new world redeemed from ill.
Not less with those who feel, in truth.
Though the red sky rain fiery sleet,
That, through their hour of anguish, youth
Shall walk the world with careless feet.
Those, too, who in a reckless hour
The paths of dark rebellion trod.
No deep disquiet made them cower
That dawn before the firing-squad.
The quicklime eats their valiant dust,
But what shall stone or steel avail
Against their bright, immortal trust
In the white hosts of Innisfail!
BlessM are they who lie in death !
Life has no deeper boon to give
Than faith that sacrificial breath
Shall make the Tree of Freedom live.
Haply afar with hearts made wise
They taste, in truth, the goodly fruit,
Where we but see with troubled eyes
The blasted trunk and blackened root.
VICTORIA.
VANCE PALMER.


tralia to-day would have been very different, as it
would have been most difficult to obtain a market for
our goods at any price at all.
That was what Denison Miller said, being
true to liis salt and anxious to meet the new
loan with a brave countenance. But how if
Denison Miller had said this? —
Every great loan boom, whether it be for war—
provided the war isn’t devastating our own country
—for good public works, for rotten public works or
for sheer robbery and corruption, puts a hectic beer-
flush on the face, and especially the nose, of the
community while it lasts. The Mississippi Scheme
and the South, Sea Bubble did the same in their day.
A vast new currency of notes and bonds keeps things
on the move for a time.
Through sheer mismanagement and incapacity on
the part of our rulers, however, we haven’t had the
share of artificial prosperity that might be expected.
W'e sold enormous quantities of wheat to the
British Government at a low price. For a first-class
sample the farmer averages (in time) about 3s. 6d.
to 3s. 9d. per bushel net. Even the farmer with
capital, good machinery, industry and intelligence is
hardly making a crust at the game. Also, the crust
is in arrears. About 8,000,000 tons of wheat are
decaying on our hands because we can’t get it away,
badly as Britain needs it. Our imbecile Government
refuses even to run up wooden sailing-ships to do
the carriage; it only appoints new officials, builds
silos (with a smell about them), and wrangles with
trades unions.
There is a great fear that the British Govern-
ment will no longer provide our farmer with the dole
on which he barely lives, being tired of decayed
wheat that is out of his reach. In that case, unless
we find ships, the grower may write “Finis” or
“Iciubod” on his fence and quit. A great deal of
the wheat that has been paid for is too far gone for
acceptance in England. Thanks to these varied
alarms the cultivated area is diminishing and agri-
culturists are already getting out.
What makes the wheat-man gnash hardest is the
knowledge that his product is worth over 10s. per
bushel in the United States, if he had any kind of
cheap tub to take it there.
The rabbit promises soon to be a big loss to the
country instead of a profit. The custom has been to
freeze bunny and export him, and the British Govern-
ment has bought large quantities. Now it says that
as it can’t get delivery the animal will have to
remain here and run about the paddocks and multiply.
The production of rare metals has greatly fallen
away, and many mines have been closed. Rare
metals are little used in Australia, and it is difficult
to export them, as they can’t swim. Besides, the ex-
port prices have been fixed by authority and fixed so
low that great bitterness has resulted.
The shipbuilding industry has almost ceased,
despite the extreme need for ships. You could hardly
find a trace of it with a bloodhound.
Manufacturing industries, taken as a whole, find
employment for fewer people than they did some
time ago—this despite the demand for munitions.
Some people say the utterly idiotic tariff is responsi-
ble. Banks are doing well, partly because the
stupendous loan flotations bring a lot of miscel-
laneous business. There is a dreadful row about
waste in the War Department, and people say that
it is the last possibility in the way of an ass, but
even waste keeps loan money in circulation. Strikes
are more numerous than usual. The Government
pays almost all the war expenditure, including waste,
out of loans in order to obviate taxation. A number
of old loans are falling due and being renewed at
very high interest, but that is mostly a matter for
posterity. I would be darned sorry if I were pos-
terity. Any man who is his own son these days is
taking risks.
If the Ministry hadn’t been so fond of touching
the paint to see if it was dry; if it had known when
to keep still; if it hadn’t worn out its singing voice
with so much public eloquence and hadn’t been so
like the old-time Jacobite conspiracy, where one man
in every 12 was a disguised enemy and the other
11 all talked at once—if it had left a few things
alone and shown some brains about a few other
things, affairs would have been better. For instance,
if it had found ships, first and foremost and all the
time, the people could have sold a heap more goods
at much higher prices. Instead, it was so busy
supervising that a cow could hardly lean over a
fence without being properly stamped.
Personally I think that the Federal Cabinet is kin
to the Bourbons —it learns nothing and it forgets
nothing, and it didn’t know much to begin with.
But as it is my employer, in a sense, I wouldn’t
mention this for the world. Often when it comes
back in its motor-car tired and hot after officially
opening a new hen-farm at AVarwick, with oratory and
lunch, I feel inclined to ask it where it expects
everything t.o go to, when the loan boom is over, but
I forbear. I am not one to tread on even the most
incompetent of my fellow-creatures.
That was what Denison Miller didn’t say.
What a joy it would have been if he had
said it! Perhaps he thought itl

Imported Vegetables.
In mentioning the other morning that the
price of tobacco was being increased, Sydney
Telegraph casually remarked that, as local
tobacco growing had not met with much
success, “leaf for making up cut and plug
tobacco and cigarettes has to be imported.”
As all this seemed to accord with its well-
known principles of Freetrade and to imply
that local products, whatever their nature,
are poor stuff; anyhow, the Telegraph pro-
bably felt very gleeful for the rest of the
day. But to The Bulletin the statement
that tobacco “has to be imported” sounds
like rank perjury. The fact that tobacco is
imported is no reason why it should be. The
circumstance is merely a continual reminder
of the gross public extravagance of Aus-
tralia and of what happens in a country that
borrows its way to greatness. The Com-
monwealth is the only country on earth
having sub-tropical territory which despises
its own smokes and fails to make tobacco-
growing a source of national wealth. It is
worse than a scandal that this should be so.
But as long as public policy is devoted to
borrowing, nothing else can happen. There
is evidence that tobacco-leaf of the finest
quality can be grown here. Yet the latest
figures show an importation of 0614 tons of
manufactured and unmanufactured smoking
material, valued preliminarily at £898,8
and. after the tax had been adjusted, at
£2,225,37(>. • Apparently in 1915 101 b. of im-
ported tobacco was used up in the factories
for every lib. of the local product, and the
position now is very much worse. Thus
nearly a million is being sent out of the.
country each year for leaves of a plant that
we can grow ourselves; over two mil-
lions of revenue is taken on it by the (lov-
ernment, which represents wealth created by
growing something else or omitting to pay
some of the bills; and people _ who ought
to be making a living by growing tobacco
are not doing it. There seem to be about 15
tobacconist shops in every street, there are
3547 persons working at 34 factories—and
apparently nobody cares 2d. that the whole
business, instead of being one that brings
the Commonwealth some definite economic
return, is really a national loser. The day
is not very far off when Australia will have
to stop this leak and several others, and get
down to business like other people.
A Railway Commissioner’s Job.
Several highly-recommended candidates for
the job of making the Victorian railways pay
are announced from the U.S.A., so presum-
ably the unfortunate Person Who Speaks
Through His Nose will soon be here with a
shiny grip and a heart full of hope. Then
disillusionment will come thick and fast upon
him. He will find*: —
(1) That he can’t shut up hopelessly unprofitable
branch lines if political influence is in the way, for
it isn’t good business to antagonise the local voter
even if he be poor and scarce.
(2) That he can’t prevent hopeless new lines being
foisted on him to be “made pay,’’ no matter how
clearly he may demonstrate their hopelessness, if
political considerations require them.
(3) That he can’t seriously reduce wages, because
the trades-union vote would be antagonised to the
damage of political institutions.
(4) That he can’t materially raise freights and
fares without antagonising the farming vote, which
would be a political disaster.
Likewise and furthermore :—•
(5) If he points out that with a few short connec-
tions he could open up a far better port than Mel-
bourne, in an almost empty country, and could
thereby give Portland a start that would make it
another Melbourne, and might thus add 20 or 30 per
cent, to his earnings, he will be told that political
principles forbid there being any big place except
Melbourne.
(6) He will And that if a 3* per cent, railway
loan falls due and has to be renewed at 6 per cent.,
he must shoulder the new burden, and mustn't even
swear for fear of disturbing the political system.
(7) If he tries to economise by making his engines
drag a double load, the resulting smash, as in Tait’s
time, will disturb the political calm.
(8) If he asks whether the lines are or are not
a goldarned implement of corruption for buying votes,
he will be informed that the high political traditions
of the State preclude such an assumption.
(9) And if He Who Speaks Through That Which
He Smells With inquires why, in these conditions, he
was brought to Victoria at all, he will be informed
that he was brought as a matter of political policy
to make the railways pay.
If he dies of an apoplectic fit just here
the doctors won’t have the courage to certify
that political confusion killed him. They
will sooner attest that improper feeding in
infancy rushed him to his untimely sepulchre.

“Silence in the Court” —and
Everywhere Else.
An Australian military officer who robbed
the Defence Department of thousands—part
of the scheme was the regular drawing of
pay for a regiment that didn’t exist —and
had a gay time on the proceeds, finished up
with four years’ hard labor. Subsequent in-
quiries disclosed that good reason existed for
supposing that this officer had got away with
considerably more plunder and had, com-
mitted more numerous offences than were
mentioned in the indictment at his trial. This
was clearly within the knowledge of the De-
partment, because it transpires (vide the lat-
est report of the Commission) that the Prime
Minister, “having regard to representations
of the father and the gallant war services
rendered by the brothers of the accused,”
directed that no further proceedings be taken
against him. The Commissioners consider
that this incident is “inexplicable” and point
out that the result of abandoning further
prosecution was to prevent the following-up
of investigations which might have deter-
mined whether grave suspicions concerning
other officers were justifiable. The view of
Solicitor-General Garran is that the prose-
cution went quite far enough in securing one
conviction, as, even if guilt had been proved
on other charges, the punishment would have
been a concurrent sentence. Now, the ques-
tion is not whether a person who had robbed
the people of £67,000 would have been told
to stay in gaol for more than four years if
he had been proved guilty of other crimes.
This paper could derive no satisfaction from
seeing an alleged criminal pursued to the
bitter end. The point is whether the reason
given for not prosecuting is genuine. Or did
the Minister joyfully seize upon the quite
legitimate representations of a hard-hit
father as an excuse for obscuring further
view of the rottenness of the Department?
The trial of this man was horribly damaging
to Pearce. Just about that time, too.
Pearce was asserting that his department
was going like a well-oiled machine and
struggling to gag The Bulletin when it
asserted that, instead, it was floundering
in chaos. Nothing will make this paper now
believe that the fear of further horrors com-
ing to light had not great weight in causing
the Government to temper justice with
mercy. If this forger had been detected
throwing eggs' in Bananaland or lurking in
the vicinity of the I.WAY. it is long odds
that the facts would have been put before
the jury. Justice may be blind, but can
usually pick a shady place in which to sit
and have a sleep.
The Value of Congestion.
The new State Governor of N. S. Wales has
remarked that Balmain is “worth more than
five bob.” It is true that Dr. Balmain
paid almost as much as that for the place
many years ago; but it was then sweet,
clean and wholesome. At present the sub-
urb has grime upon its face and may quite
easily be in the future, as the Governor sug-
gested, “the greatest hive of industry 'south
of the equator.” But that doesn’t prove
that it is worth five bob: nor does the fact
that five bob wouldn’t buy an inch of its
worst uncleanness. Very many things sell
very readily for a lot more than they are
worth. The whole question turns upon the
value to Australia of congesting an ever-
increasing hive of toil at such a spot; and,
on all the indications, to do so would be a
definite loss both morally and economically
to the continent. Wide open spaces, where
the nominal value of the ground is still five
bob, or something like it, are waiting to be-
come industrial centres; and the spread of
population which their development would
cause means prosperity for the Common-
wealth and cash in the pockets of the Aus-
tralian people. It is, of course, quite natu-
ral that a Governor who has been accus-
tomed to associate great industry with black
and reeking horrors like those of Manchester
and Birmingham should take off his hat and
shout when lie sees the grime getting into the
souls of the dwellers in Balmain. To him
that grime looks like progress. But it is
not progress of the sort Australia needs. To
concentrate more factories around a city
that is already suffering so greatly from
congestion that the disease in its secondary
symptoms is affecting even our armies abroad
and our ballot-boxes at home is ruinously
expensive; and far from a Balmain of the
kind indicated / in the Governor’s speech
being worth five bob to us, it would pro-
bably represent a tremendous debit in the
nation’s books. Somebody would make
money out of it, but the loss to the public
would be calamitous.

Motherhood —or What?
Dear Bulletin, —It seems a bit of bad
luck for Professor Berry, the Methodist
Conference and men in general that, after
promising to present the woman’s side of the
case, “Lysistrata” (B. 21/3/T8) forgot
all about the matter. What is her side of
the case? I wish she would get down to
bedrock and show us where the moral dis-
tinction is between the abortion she deplores
and the prevention she encourages, and what
the difference consists of Meanwhile it is
just cant for a woman to try to justify
evasion on her own part by (1) explaining
that a degenerate woman of the century
before last brought a long line of criminal
wastrels into being, and (2) by putting for-
ward the grotesque demurrer that the Com-
monwealth fiver is handed over to “anything
resembling a baby.” It is easy enough to
understand a woman having a quarrel with
existing relations between the sexes. These
are in many ways barbarous and immoral.
But there are not many men outside of a
mental clinic who will quite understand the
significance of a document in which a
woman, believing herself to be talking about
maternity, puts the greatest emphasis upon
something which she considers forbids to
woman the satisfaction of what she regards
as “an indisputably legitimate appetite.” The
maternal instinct is one thing. The appetite
she is thinking about is something else.
Unless the case against motherhood, or in
favor of keeping the maternal instinct within
certain boundaries, turns upon establishing
the high ethical place of concubinage and
the physiological advantages to woman of
unrestricted indulgence in nervous excita-
tion, “Lysistrata” should come to her
point, quickly, and be done with it. She
put an arresting question: “Is there any
other sound reason in the whole wide world
for having children at all except one wants
them?” The certain answer to this is “No” ;
but neither question nor answer has relevant
place in “Lysistrata’s” thesis. The ques-
tion that is really cogent and goes to the
root of the whole business, morally and
physically, is this: “Is there any reason in
the whole wide world why a woman should
mate with a man unless she wants children?”
Of course this question of the size of the
family is in the main a weariness—the sub-
ject of a disputation as disordered as the
bowels of a dyspeptic. It can be answered
in the terms dictated by the environment,
vitality, vanity and moral notions of each
individual, and the sum of these answers is
going to reflect the practices of society, for
good or evil. But behind all this is the cer-
tainty that the practices of society must
profoundly affect the nation. It is issuing
a challenge to the eternal gods to live in the
belief that a nation whose womanhood de-
clines maternity can handle the job in front
of Australians. Women omit the child and
men second the resolution because they do
not want children ? Bubbisli! They do it
because the State penalises father and
mother for every child they produce. It
is not necessary to be a very expert logician
to make out a better case against this than
“Lysistrata” establishes for restraining the
functions of the female organism. Nor need
one be a member of the Methodist Confer-
ence to have at least some doubts about
whether a few people sprinkled over a mighty
continent are going on the right lines when
they reckon children are no use or in the
road or something. “Lysistrata” intro-
duces some inappropriate remarks by Foret.
If she would read what Havelock Ellis
has been saying lately about the subject she
intended to handle, she perhaps might see
that there is a side to it other than the one
she notices, that problems of psychology and
economics are strangely mixed up in it, and
that at least one very competent observer
and philosopher can write about motherhood
without thinking of prostitution. Popular
views about chastity have undergone some
strange convolutions since the Roman
matron lived and died. Ideas about vice
seem to be keeping them company.—Yours,
Quill.
The Attack on Billjim.
“Bill Badd” : London at the-, present time
is the happy hunting-ground of women, and
the profession is open to any old plague-
carrier that hapnens along. Frenchwomen,
barred by their own Government, are given
free rein and allowed to become prolific
sources of infection, and. by the natural pro-
cess of dissemination, non-professionals are
beginning to suffer. The need for control is
urgent. The reported attempt of the Ituns
to introduce germ's of disease by means of
infected sweets produced a horrified gasp;
but the connivance of our own authorities in
the spread of venereal disease doesn’t raise
a protest. That facilities for treatment are
provided after the revival of the trouble is a
poor substitute for keeping it off the premises
altogether. The alternative to Miss Rout’s
suggestion—the license, with compulsory
periodical examination, and effective dis-
couragement of unlicensed practitioners—is
obvious; but if you do that you '‘counte-
nance immorality.” Morality isn’t the average
politician’s long suit; but the wowser vote
and influence is not a thing to be lightly
jeopardised. So the quantity of venereal
disease passed to Billjim becomes merely
“a matter for sincere regret.” It must be a
weird mental habit that induces “right-think-
ing people” to believe that any amount of
misery inflicted on innocent and guilty alike
—on children especially—is preferable to a
remedy that might be construed into appro-
val of “sin.” It isn’t only Billjim abroad
who needs protection. In one house in Carl-
ton (Vic.) there are six women known to be
diseased, and all quite at liberty to entertain
a lonely soldier on leave. When he becomes
a temporary casualty the Government does
its duty; but that is much too late.
Horse Breeding as It Isn’t.
By a man who has grown up in the stock-
raising business: —
One of the things that most make me tired is
the advice reiterated by Government departments and
bucolic newspapers to get busy horse-breeding “for
the good of the country.”
What the advocates of horse-breeding—invariably
people who breed nothing but inkmarks themselves
—have never seen is that the vital condition of
successful horse-breeding is some means by which
£OO per head should be guaranteeable for all horses
fit for military purposes at four years of age. And
with the average of horses at that figure there are a
good many of us who would still prefer to stand off
them.
At the very lowest estimate one horse takes as
much keeping as 12 sheep. In. fact, if you stocked a
clean “new” paddock at that rate exclusively with
horses you would find them .dying out “on a face”
after the third year. Now 12 sheep in many parts
of the country are returning £lB per annum at pre-
sent values; but we will put their returns at £1 per
head per annum. This return usually comes in two
instalments; in other words, we have to wait only
six months for our first return. To get a return
from breeding a horse we have to keep its mother, on
an average, two years and the horse itself four years.
In those six years the 12 sheep would have returned
£72, not to speak of the interest on the first five
yearly returns. From the £6O returned by that
(imaginary) horse we have to deduct at least £5
for the service of its sire. (We never see more than
a 50 per cent, return of horses sold for services
paid for.) To this must be added interest on that
£5 for five years and £l 10s. for breaking in, or, at
compound interest, say, £7 out of our (imaginary)
£6O, leaving an imaginary return of £53 as against
a real return of £72 from our sheep. But in place
of this imaginary £53 put the much more likely
actual average of £2O (and how few get that much
year by year!), you will have a pretty fair idea of
what breeding horses “for the good of the country”
really means.
The only intelligent answer that can be given to
this presentation of the case is: “There is a great
deal of good country unsuited for sheep while highly
suitable for horses.” To that answer there are two
rejoinders: (1) There are classes of sheep suitable for
all classes of country; (2) on good country suitable
for a horse one can put five good weaners (cattle)
purchasable at £6 per head, and saleable at £l2 in
12 months; or, say, £lO, to make the horse’s case
a bit better. Now 5 £4’s £2O; 6 £2o’s = £l2O, as
against an imaginary £53, or an actual £2O of horse-
money. Is the neddy in it?
And.the quickness of the return from sheep, and
even cattle, contrasts just as vividly with the length
of the wait for the dividend from the “nobler”
animal.
In fact, the more angles one looks at the subject
from the more convinced does he become that the
horse can only be bred as an expensive hobby, with
only the most fantastic hope of a profit to lure one on.
The ordinary horse-breeder never sits determinedly
down with a butt of pencil and the top of a kerosene-
case to figure the business out to the very bitter
end; but just goes it blind —usually because he
happens to have mares that were originally too faulty
to sell and are now too old to work.
In the iron times ahead, when things tighten up to
bursting-point—as they will—we shall see the horse
ever more recognised as the unprofitable member, and
relegated to oblivion, in favor of ever-improving
mechanical means of traction, conveyance and culti-
vation.
The Curse of Conscription.
The N. S. Wales section of the Labor
party proposes that the party at its
next whole and entire conference shall
make “No Conscription” the first plank of
the Labor platform. The Bulletin says
“Hooray” and “Hear, hear !” Compulsory and
universal military training is a curse. It is
very expensive, it is unproductive of wealth,
involves exposure to all sorts of weather,
the wearing of a sombre uniform, and
the carrying of heavy and ridiculous weap-
ons of destruction, and if it is conducive to
moralitv or the leading of the higher life
the fact hasn’t been proved. Without a
moment’s hesitation The Bulletin says
that Conscription (as some people call it)
should be put down, even if it takes oui
whole able-bodied male population to do
our bit in the good work. With one or per-
haps two exceptions every country of any
importance wants to put it down. Britain
had compulsory service of a spasmodic and
ill-defined sort for perhaps 2000 years ; but
after the Napoleonic wars all this was aban-
doned till the other day, barring a dead-
letter Act about a compulsory ballot for
militia recruits. The United States had
recourse to compulsion during the Civil
War, but then it dropped the policy till the
other day. Russia cjid remarkable things in
the way of impressing recruits in periods of
stress, but during the Long Peace or Com-
parative Peace it let the old machine rust
till the other day—the other day, of course,
happening when the German menace began
to loom large on the border. France wiped
out Conscription (so-called) after Napo-
leon’s time and only took to it with a rush
when the German wolf was at the door —
just as the German Wolf was recently at
our door. Then it reverted to compulsion
—just the other day, so to speak—but did
it too late, and it was a half-and-half army
still partly raised by voluntaryism which
went under in 1870-71. Italy took to the
expensive fad reluctantly—the other day. as
one might put it—because Austria was a.
nightmare at its gate and still held and
shamefully oppressed much Italian territory.
Conscription (to use the popular word)
ought certainly to be put dohvn. It would

but for the German menace and the Prus-

inen and enough skill and courage and a

Whose the Blame?
The publication of the Defence Commis-
sion’s reports has been followed by an
almost universal yell at Pearce. Upon him
it is sought to heap all the blame for the
chaos and the waste of hundreds of thou-
sands; probably millions, of pounds. Heaven
knows this paper holds no brief for the
Minister. But it says this; Pearce hasn’t
burgled or embezzled the job for which he is
no more fitted than he is to control the
North Sea Fleet; it has been given to him.
And not given to him once, but again and
again—three times at least since the shock-
ing state of the Department was officially
laid bare. For, after all, what this latest-
but-one Defence Commission has reported is
no more in effect than another Commis-
sioner, It.' McC. Anderson, reported on
April 5, 1915, just upon three years ago.
“The Finance Department,” he said, “is in
a disorganised, dissatisfied and unhealthy
condition. Under modern and reasonable
conditions it could be run with fewer men,
working easily within the regulation hours.”
Of the Ordnance branch he reported in
almost the same terms as those the recent
Commission employed. Subsequently there
were other disclosures —some made by the
Committee of Inquiry, a small body of
accountants; others in the Criminal Court.
Yet Pearce was retained in his job. When
the Phewsion was formed there was scarcely
a “Liberal” paper which didn’t say that
Pearce ought to go. But Pearce remained,
because Hughes insisted upon it; and Cook
and his hungry crowd were prepared to
swallow 50 Pearces in order to get into the
tart-shop themselves. Then came the smash
after the Referendum, and again it was said
that Pearce must go; but again Hughes
insisted upon having his man Friday with
him, and again Cook and the rest of the
“Liberals” swallowed man Friday lest they
themselves should have to leave the confec-
tionery. In these circumstances who is to
blame? Certainly not Pearce. He has done
his poor, dull, stodgy best. But without a
day’s business training this very fair car-
penter was put to the biggest business job
in Australia, and after his hopelessness had
been thoroughly proved, the Liberal side of
the Phewsion twice consented to his remain-
ing there rather than miss the chance of
getting a few jobs itself. More than any
man else Joseph Cook deserves censure for
the continued muddle in the Department.
Hughes probably believed in Pearce. Cook’s
party didn’t. Presumably Cook didn’t either.
But it was prepared to sell Australia in re-
turn for a mess of pottage. And it did.
Armageddon Begins Again.
A fortnight ago Bonar Law informed the
British House of Commons that he was
“sceptical” about a German offensive in the
West. Well, it arrived last Thursday, and
the only thing that remains to be sceptical
about is the result. There were a score of
reasons for expecting that as soon as the
German could scrape the mud off his bluchers
he would hit and do it hard. And there are
an equal number of reasons for supposing
that the enemy has planned his attack with
strict attention to detail and that he saw a
reasonable chance of making a success of the
job. At the moment of writing British and
German armies were locked in a tremendous
conflict raging over a front of about 50
miles. Saturday saw the conclusion of “the
first phase of the battle” —a phrase we have
all had mournful reason to be distrustful of
in the past. The Germans claimed to have
occupied the first positions held by their
enemies, to have taken 30,000 prisoners and
to have put a substantial portion of the Brit-
ish forces in the position of being “already
beaten.” Haig’s reports indicated that (1)
his army had suffered heavy losses, (2) the
Germans had been terribly punished, (3) his
force had at various points retired to new
positions, (4) the enemy was bringing up
strong reinforcements, and (5) his own
troops were fighting with the greatest gal-
lantry. The blunt meaning of all this was
that a terrible struggle had commenced and
that in the initial stages thereof the enemy
had gained certain advantages. The measure
of these was not clear at the beginning of the
week, for, though the British line was
said to be holding fast, there were
disturbing indications that the situation to
the north and south of St. Quentin had by
Sunday become very serious and that the
weight of the German thrust between the
Somme and the Oise was hard to bear. Cer-
tainly the German could make a material
advance in this sector and yet not be any
further forward than he was prior to the
battle of the Somme, but it is also certain
that if the defence could not hold the newer
line, the business of hanging on to the old
one will scarcely be easier. Pending further
information of the happenings at that vital
point it can be taken for granted that the
German will press on this attack with
courage, skill and resolution. He has never
failed to do this, and to expect him to flinch
now, with a victory almost in his grasp,
would be plain fatuity. Briton and Frank
have their backs to the wall and it is pro-
bable that the. best they can hope for is
to continue in that position until the
American armies become effective. That
they will fight and die like men we
know. That our own lads will stand
up to the collar is certain. But reflections
of this kind have at the best only slight
force against the inescapable fact that, unless
there are men to fill the gaps, there can ne
only one end to it all. Complete victory may
not be at stake in this mighty battle, but the
question of whether, when it is over, one or
more of the nations involved will be found
going to pieces very clearly is. All sorts of
things were on the knees of the gods when
this week started.

“The Pariaii” : From the manner in which
the war business has been handled, the
public may fairly assume that a mess has
been made of the Home Defence system;
and that is really of the utmost consequence,
because through .this maladministration the
continuance of the system is endan-
gered. Let me give a typical instance
of the sort of thing foi* which the
people pay. In the year a boy turns
12 he is examined by a doctor to test
his physical fitness for the junior cadet
work to be done in the next two years. The
medical-officer gets half a crown per cadet
examined. Last year the M.O. called at
my school, took the junior cadet record and
a seat. I lined the squad up and the M.O.
called the names. Each cadet marched up,
stood at attention, was commanded by the
M.O. to ’bout turn and go to his place. In
this way 25 boys were passed as fit in as
many minutes. One poor kid who is partly
paralysed on one side as the result of in-
fantile paralysis would have been passed as
fit had I not invited the half-crown doctor’s
attention to his halting gait as he returned
to his place. Not one boy had even his coat
off.
