“11. Sec”: Nyngan (N.S.W.), where the
dogs slept in the hospital matron’s bedroom,
is not very far out. Think what may happen
further back ! There many of the matrons
are incompetent, others worse. In the hos-
pital of which I was secretary Matron A.
was incompetent and old-fashioned. She
took “funny turns” and finally had a pro-
vidential vision from Heaven which told her
to resign. Matron B. was incompetent and
peculiar. There were stories of a stand-up
fight with the nursing-staff of one. She
resigned. The next, C., was a competent
matron with a failing. Under the stress of
work she drank the medical comforts, and was
found down and out in the ward next morn-
ing. The delirious patient had gone for a
walk. She left. Matron D. was a recent
arrival with particularly high credentials
and pleasing personality. She re-filled the
dead marine that Matron C. had dropped
and lasted only two months. The climax
came when she wandered round and in-
sisted on all the patients having bread and
treacle. We had an enteric case in at the
time. She left, after some persuasion. All
these nurses presented good testimonials.
Sometimes there was a lapse of a year or two
since the last. The Government insists on a
properly trained and approved matron being
appointed, and it approved, and, as far as I
know now, still approves, of the appointment
of any of these people. Committees in their
ignorance, led astray by testimonials given
by weak or careless hospital committees, are
still liable to employ them. What can they
do? An inspector coming round now and
then could do less, for, after all, the com-
mittee is on the spot, hears what is going on
and can act immediately. All the above
cases were before the war. It must be worse
now. Perhaps if the Government controlled
the appointment of the matrons, fixing the
salaries, and periodically promoting the de-
serving matrons to more important positions,
a better class of woman would accept the
appointment of matron to the Outback hos-
pitals. Just as with school teachers.
It is time the supine Government took
Simonoff, the Russian revolutionary agent
who calls himself Consul-General, by the
collar and ran him on board the first steamer
that is going furthest away from Allied
territory, and warned him not to come back
for 1000 years. In his latest outpouring he
informed a Victorian audience that the best
way to support the Bolsheviks (sis if the
Bolsheviks can’t earn their own living) was
for the Australian people to “obtain the
management of their own social life.” Now,
as Australia manages everything by the
votes of the whole adult population, it can’t
get any more control of its “social life” than
it has.' Even if. it allowed Simonoff’s Bol-
sheviks to abolish Parliament, and set a lot
of towns and cities on fire, and rob the Sav-
ings Banks and all the other banks, and
repudiate all debts, and break up the rail-
ways, and give up washing themselves, we
couldn’t control our “social life” any more
than we do. At present a boy can take
his best girl walking on the shore by moon-
light under the protection of a brutal and
capitalistic Government resting on the prin-
ciple of one-adult-one-vote, and his life .
wouldn’t be much brighter if he was sand-
bagged by. a Dictator’s Ited Guard and woke
up later surrounded by the blessings of free-
dom to find that the girl had disappeared.
Simonoff may not- do much harm, hut lie
ought to be sent away on a charge of braying
out of his turn.

Australia’s only statesman has bungled
kerosene now. Nearly six months ago he
was asked to raise the price Is. a case, and
a promise was made that this would be
done, as from February 1. But, with
another trip to England in view, he probably
wanted to avoid the job of raising the price
of the poor man’s light and the small man’s
engine-fuel. He thought, apparently, that
the matter could wait until he was out of the
country, and then his locmn-tenens would
have to issue the unpopular order. But the
matter couldn't wait. Already a kerosene
famine has started in Sydney, and by the
middle of next month Victoria, S. Aus. and
elsewhere will be crying out for the goods.
The oil kings are no philanthropists, as
Hughes (and Heaven even) knows, and on
the present market value importers would
have to lose more than Is. a case. The rise
of Is. applied for last year will have to be
increased to Is. 6d.. it seems. And pending
the arrival of supplies some thousands of
gas engines will be out of work, and a few
thousand folks on the land will be in the
same position as Moses before the candle
went out.
“0.L.” : Towards the end of last year the
price of Victorian 1915-16 wheat scrip was
depressed to a shade over 2d. a bushel, owing
in part to the absence of published Govern-
ment information and in part to the fatigue
of the man on the land; yet by the day
when Oman, Minister for Wheat and Wee-
vils, read his vital statement in the Assembly
(13th inst.) the price had gradually climbed
to 3 9-16 d. The Minister’s figures showed
that the scrip-holder might reasonably ex-
pect to receive nearly 31d. a bushel after
allowing for expenses, as well as a possible
extra fd. through the increase in weight dur-
ing storage, the whole being subject to a
trifling contingent charge of not more than
one-eighth of a penny. So it is likely that
the actual return wall be somewhere around
4d. a bushel, or nearly 100 p.c. profit fBr
those who bought low. That won’t be all for
the speculators, however. The banks w’ere
happy to finance buyers on a very narrow
margin, with the result that some fortunate
speculators will be able to draw as much as
400 p.c. on the money actually laid out by
them to secure the producers’ wheat-scrip.
Oman’s terribly belated statement, coming
as it did at a moment when the brokers had
practically skinned the market, must leave
the 1915-1916 wheat-grower with a horrible
burr of doubt sticking to the seat of his in-
telligence. ,It smells as though some body
hasn’t been so close-mouthed on the matter
as the Minister himself.

million more like him.

just clinched an amazing bargain. Its build-
ing has been reclaimed. The land cost the
council nothing and the building cost £6OOO.
It gets in return a piece of Crown land, one
of the most dominating sites in the city, and
£14,000 in hard cash. Who put this
through ?
“Ministers welcome criticism of the De-
fence Department,” Hughes was reported to
have said the other day. Yes, they welcome
it with an order to the press under the War
Precautions Regulations making disparaging
criticism an offence.
Harry Sutherland Wightman Lawson,
Vic.’s new Premier, is over six feet long,
with a muscle cultivated on the football
fields of Castlemaine. Like J. W. McCay,
whose seat in the State House he won in
1899 as a boy of 24, Lawson is the son of a
Presbyterian parson, and though he was a
street-corner evangelist his girth and thick-
ness, combined with his popularity as a fair
footballer, barred the description “wowser.”
This is his fourth portfolio, and in an elec-
tion he is regarded as unshiftable. He is a
lawyer now, but was only an articled clerk
when he arrived.
“Voluntary” enlistment isn’t quite dead.
An expert in the business estimates that the
defeat of a hot favorite for any big race is
worth at least 25 stone-broke recruits. The
time may come when the only way to re-
inforce the Australian armv will be to bribe
John Brown and a few others to run stiff.
Walkers of Maryborough (Q.) is an enter-
prising firm in a big way, and, like the
Broken Hill Prop., it wants to build ships
for wheat- and wool- and meat-carrying. But
as the Broken Hill Prop, received warning
from the Hughes Government to leave off,
Walkers is wise in its generation and waits
awhile before trying to fill a long-felt want.
Now, how is it that these private concerns
are anxious to start in such a casual way,
without 24 months’ negotiation with the
unions, while Hughes and Cook are still
busy wasting a ton of paper on protocols
and draft treaties?

“Padjud”: “Arthur It.” confidently pre-
dicts (B. 7/3/T8) that the lofty aerial gear
now used in wireless telegraphy will soon
be a thing of the past. Perhaps. But at
present the tendency is in the direction of
higher towers. About 12 months ago the
Germans added 300 ft. to the mainmast at
Nauen station (near Berlin), giving it a
total height of 900 ft. An idea of the calibre
of the station may be got from the fact that
readable signals from it are commonly re-
ceived in Maoriland and Australia. Those
brief German “official” reports, which are
sent out for the world to intercept, are fre-
quently picked up by stations as far away
as ours. Perhaps by ours. Only Mr. Hughes
can tell you that. Eiffel Tower is gener-
ally conceded to be one of the most efficient
stations on earth. This is solely owing to
the immense height of the mast —985 ft.
Thus the engineers’ dictum, “height for effi-
ciency,” is borne out. Funibashi, the giant
Japanese station recently opened, has 19
masts, a central tower of 650 ft. set in a ring
of 18 others each of 260 ft. All rest on ball
and socket joints, and thus, like Nauen,
depend on steel guy-ropes.
“Cherokee”: Some years ago, when Vic.
Parliament was legislating to prevent “wine-
shop weddings,” cases were dug out in which
the ’groom knew the bride only as Ducky or
something similar. But it has remained for
Collingwood to produce (trust Collingwood!)
a gentleman who did not know his bride’s
first monniker, but only her surname. The
lady, Mary Elizabeth Doyle, otherwise
Toms, aged 59, is now under committal for
trial on a bigamy charge. In 1903, it seems,
James McMurtrie, a South Melbourne
hawker, wedded her, knowing her only as
Mrs. Toms. She said she was a widow.
Some years later she said that if he did not
clear out she would hit him with an axe.
He wouldn’t chance the axe, and the Court
made an order for 7s. 6d. a week, which he
paid until she began to sign the receipts
“Mrs. Doyle.” He took the hint and ceased
payment. On November 28, 1914, she
married William Henry Berryman, but
the parson refused to hand over the
marriage lines because he failed to
get his fee. The lady candidly told a
constable that she had gone to a party and
“got shick.” She regarded the whole thing
as a joke. On the evidence, it teas rather
casual. Beimyman’s sister saw a matri-
monial ad., and sent him to see Mrs. Doyle.
“I’m looking for a wife,” he said. “And
I’m looking for a husband,” said she. They
at once agreed to chance it. After a fort-
night’s matrimony he found his clothes
packed up in the bathroom, and they separ-
ated. Love still can have its dream, even at
59. But in Collingwmod the awakening is
liable to be sudden.

Auditorium “Joan the Woman”
There is one infallible test for successful
melodrama. If the audience frenziedly
applauds when the hero, with the -spotlight
registered upon his face, raises his arm and
declares: “By the sainted memory of my
dead solicitor I swear that an isosceles tri-
angle is equilateral!” that melodrama is in
for a long run. It doesn’t matter what the
hero says; it’s the way the spotlight helps
him to say it. Judged by that test, “The
Woman Thou Gavest Me,” a melodramati-
sation by John Cosgrove of Hall Caine’s in-
terminable novel, produced at the Opera
House on Saturday, will at least equal pre-
vious long-distance records.
* * # #
There is nothing in this play to wound the
soul of the most rabid sectarian, or bring a
blush to the cheek of sweet innocence. In-
deed, a husband at the Opera House will
learn more about the nobility of wives than
he had ever suspected at home; and the
religious element is quite innocuous. The
villainy is plain, but it is polished ; and when
the vampire vamps she vamps with decorum.
The scenic artist excels himself in some
stage-pictures, especially in an Antarctic
snow-scene. Though the blizzard is heard
but does not otherwise make itself evident,
the effect of solitude and cold is impressive.
And it is in this scene that this melodrama
proves true to type. The hero is leading an
exploration party to the South Pole ; he is
only 100 miles away from it; but his men
are unwilling to go further. He appeals to
them to support him in the final dash, and
sends them 'off into the blizzard to get an
extra ration of grog. Then, while an aurora
Australis works overtime in the background,
the hero prays. His prayer is answered by
a vision of the heroine ins'.de the aurora.
She is evidently in trouble. The brave
“THE WOMAN THOU GAVEST ME,"
AT SYDNEY GRAND OPERA HOUSE.
John Cosgrove, author and producer of the
piece, wondering how he can prevent George
Cross perspiring at the South Pole.
•explorer summons his men from the back
premises of the blizzard and announces that
he, too, has decided upon a retreat. The men
welcome this ignoble decision, and give them-
selves three cheers for going home. It is
true that the hero, as an afterthought, re-
quests them to promise to return next year
for another attempt, and they cheer them-
selves some more. Then the curtain descends,
and on Saturday evening the whole vast
audience applauded. Applauded what? The
decision of those intrepid explorers to chuck
their job! The dramatiser evidently knows
his audience.
George Cross is a noble hero, manly and
sonorous ; his foil, Rutland Beckett, provides
a clever and almost subtle study of villainy;
John Cosgrove is an attractive old parish
priest; but Charles Stanford’s conception
of the heroine’s cruel father is rather crude.
Harry McDona and Mabel Lynne capably
afford the usual farcical relief; Elsie Prince
is sufficiently pathetic and pleasant to look
upon as the persecuted heroine; Muriel Dale
vamps with the proper intensity; and Helen
Fergus, as a Rev. Mother, is sufficiently
saintly. The 13 scenes of this elaborate pro-
duction are well contrived, and the support
of a large cast do’es not call for any curses.
On Thursday evening of this week Marie
Tempest will be “Mrs. Dot” for the last
time at Sydney Criterion. On Saturday “A
Pair of Silk Stockings” will show the Eng-
lish star and Graham Browne in what is
claimed to a somewhat different comedy.

crust with his wonderful lamp at Sydney

union wage.

day at the matinee. George Welch and his

show.

ney Playhouse will take place on Thursday
afternoon, including a potted pantomime
burlesque. The English Pierrots are making-
friends with Sydney.
At Sydney Opera House on Bun Friday a

Sydney screenings this week will be inter-
rupted by Good Friday, when all shows will
produce Something Solemn, guaranteed not
to cause archbishops loss of sleep. At the
Crystal Palace, however, a se-rmon on
“Womanhood” will see the week through.
This is another of ’Murka’s “preparedness”
propaganda films, and its moral applies even
more strongly to this empty continent. Doug.
Fairbanks illustrates a “Wild and Woolly”
time at Hoyt’s and the Haymarket in his
usual knock-about, hair-raising way. “The
Garden of Allah,” the film of Robt. Hichens’s
fine novel, survives another week at the
Lyric.
*# * *
Last week has disappeared and Sydney
Hippodrome audie’nces still don’t know when
the unsociable lion that regards the world
with a sour eye intends to bite a meal out
of the trainer. Every night the beast seemed
on the verge of a great offensive and glared
at the whip with intense hatred. This paper
has a strong suspicion that one of these
evenings the brute will provide more thrill
to the proceedings than is mentioned on the
day’s menu. Meanwhile the man in charge
goes on with his job as unconcernedly as if
he was merely running a Defence Depart-
ment. This disregard of calamity and their
complete concentration on the business in
hand is the most cheerful feature of all cir-
cus performers’ work. Whether it is the
elephant standing on its hind legs, or the
lady dancing on an invisible wire, or the
limber gentleman who whirls rapidly through
the atmosphere on to his feet, each is for
the moment Engrossed in that incident to the
exclusion of everything else on earth. And
that is why they get through with a fine
certainty and three cheers from the stout
citizen who only concentrates on refresh-
ments. It being Easter, the writer intends
forwarding a bouquet of buns to the
grotesque imitation of one Chaplin who
waves himself at impossible' angles in the
breeze and falls heavily in a scientific heap
from gi’eat heights. Why he emerges from
the smash without a broken neck is a dark,
inscrutable mystery.

All the Melbourne theatres, save the
King’s, ai-e waiting for a week-end change
of bill. The King’s is still proceeding with

other name.

Gregan McMahon has wisely abandoned
his intention of producing “The .Madras
House” at Melbourne Playhouse this week.
The Repertory Co.’s performance is to hap-
pen on Saturday, April 13, and be repeated
on the Tuesday following.
** * *
Our old friend William Desmond is the
big Irish hero in “The Sudden Gentleman”
at Melbourne Majestic this week. As an im-
promptu millionaire in American surround-
ings, William is the happy hero turning sad-
ness into gladness and warming the woman
in a cold coquette. The second feature, “The
Awakening.” is a case of love in Bohemia,
picturing all the poverty and high vitality
of that curious region. Montagu Love and
Dorothy Kelly register every aspect of human
feeling in the principal parts.
** * *
“Robinson Crusoe” continues to be the
twice-a-day feature at Melbourne Bijou. The
panto, is weak in its remembrance of the
ancient story, and has somehow mislaid the
faithful Friday, who was aide-de-camp and
valet to the original hero. There is a story
to the effect that he is in the Federal Minis-
try. Still there is no lack of Friday’s rela-
tives. In fact, the balance of the week is
obvious, headed by a Coon Queen who seems
lonely without a banjo and a slice of water-
melon. Also the entertainment is strong in
panto, essentials. A fantastic touch is lent
by a weirdly-garbed Demon whose only
human quality is a baritone voice. As far
as the present writer can see, the rest of him
is green limelight and delirium tremens.
Melbourne Auditorium is having a week of
“20.000 Leagues Under the Sea.” which is
a first cousin of Jules Verne’s narrative, not
less startling than its relative while it keeps
to the surface events, and even more thrill-
ingly adventurous when it takes a header.
The production is alleged to have cost half a
million dollars ; doubtless a lot of the,cash was
needed to bribe the submarine photographer
to face the Jim Jam family that pose as fish
in those parts. One would need a shandy-
gaff of rum and absinthe, stirred with a stick
of dynamite, before one could conceive any-
thing approaching the deep sea of this won-
derful film. Next Saturday the camera
swings back several centuries and concen-
trates on Geraldine Farrar in “Joan the
Woman.”
** * *
Douglas. Fairbanks is jumping out of the
gloom at Hoyt’s, Melbourne, this week as a
vivacious tenderfoot in “Wild and Woolly.”
Despite his saccharine manner, he has mus-
tard beneath, and that ingredient comes to
the surface when he encounters a bad man and
a mob of wild Injuns. Redskins also figure
with greater prevalence and tumult in “Her
Own People,” featuring Lenore Ulrich.
Lenore is the bit of color in a white man’s life,
and there are some big scenes where his rela-
tives urge him to change the tint. Hoyt’s
Olympia is also filming the first chapter of a
serial, “Who is Number One?”
Chief Justice Griffith hurried up in a
brutal fashion with lus report that 5400
new troops.per month are required to keep
the Australian forces up to strength. If
he had only been decent and lingered in the
orthodox fashion, Hughes and Cook might
have been on their way to England before
the problem of getting all these men had to
be faced.
The eight ships which the Australian Gov-
ernment was getting built in the U.S. may
probably be written off as a delusion. Uncle
Sam, like everybody else, is taking over un-
finished ships—anybody’s ships—to supply
his own needs. So we bump up once more
against the problem of doing things our-
selves.
As the Northern Territory is under direct
Federal control the Hughes Ministers might
have covered themselves with a spebk of
glory by < settling the meat difficulty there.
The slaying of beeves is a large industry
at Darwin, and commands high wages—in
fact, extra wages are paid in the Territory
for most industries. This year the offer was
that 20 weeks’ work should be guaranteed,
that the industrial award wages should be
paid whatever they might be (a new award
was then expected) with one-third added —
that is to say, the lawful £ should really be
£1 6s. Sd. —and that the employees should
receive their fares to Darwin and back, with
£lO travelling allowance. As a side issue
the beef companies asked for a promise that
there should be no strikes this time. The
terms were refused, whereon the meatworks
decided to let the ox live. This meant leaving
1000 men out of work. The Australian
Government as boss of the territory rose to
the occasion and said nothing. However,
it was at last agreed that there should be
a sort of half-season, which will presumably
employ the men already on the spot. Thus
the bullocks will draw lots and one will be
taken and the other left. And that is how
we develop the Northern Territory, though
even the war is hardly more important.
“Peter” : Mannix is the first Victorian E.C.
prelate to make extensive use of the motor-
car. He is always whizzing about in a smart
single-seater, and is no slower than Hughes
in turning his vehicle into a rostrum. Also
he likes the convenience of a car for rapid
runs to Mass. The other Sunday morning
he skimmed past me with his famous biretta
aloft in an evident hurry to keep an appoint-
ment at St. Pat’s Cathedral. The late Dr.
Carr never showed the same admiration for
modern inventions. A nice languid brougham
was more in his line.
“The Old Soldier”: It never has been
easy in England—and I doubt whether it
ever was easy anywhere else—to raise an
army by voluntary enlistment. For quite a
century the War Office has never paid less
than £1 to the recruiting N.C.O.'s for every
man attested ; and, to my certain knowledge,
during the Crimean and the Mutiny, in
both of which I fought, recruits were paid
£5 to attest.
“Murrabit”: Various Departments, State
as well as Commonwealth, and many private
firms, are reaping the benefit of the policy
of the Vic. Education Department to afford
facilities for its best teachers to procure a
University degree. For a good many years
now 20 of them have been nominated for a
free course which puts them on the track to
an Arts or Science diploma. What the De-
partment has not realised is that there is a
demand at good salaries for qualified men,
and that it does not supply the good salaries.
The consequence is that there is a steady
stream to the private colleges as teachers
and a snapping-up of good billets in labora-
tories by the scientifically qualified. The
Department recently woke up to this fact,
and now requires its nominees to enter into
a bond to serve four years in its schools.
What it wants to realise is that salaries in
keeping with a man’s qualifications ought to
be paid if it really requires such men. If
it does not, then the sooner the taxpayer is
relieved of the burden of the cost the better;
if the national benefits are recognised, then,
let the system be extended so that other'
bright youths, who are not teachers, may
have a chance.

“What do you think of my new silk bathing
costume? It cost five guineas!”
“Well, there's not much to show for your
money.”
“Why, how much more would you show?”
“Copabella”: One of the best workers in
our Red Cross branch is a white-haired old
lady with grandsons at the Front. The
branch packed Christmas-boxes last Septem-
ber, and grandma enclosed in hers a loving
note and signed it “Annie Box and
note reached a lonely boy in Palestine, and
back came a reply brimming with gratitude.
It, too, was lover-like and intimate in its
appreciation of the “dear Annie” it addressed
by name so frankly and so frequently,
Grandma replied as lovingly, admitted white
locks and 70-odd years, and enclosed proof.
Other “dear Annie” letters had followed the
joyous pioneer. Last week came the first reply
since the disillusioning photo, was enclosed.
It contained a picture of a round-faced lad;
but the lover-like motif remained in the
accompanying letter—and so did the “dear
Annie” method of address. Billjim said
beautifully that “Annie” was dearer to him
than ever!
“Gron”: It happened at the 3rd London
General Hospital inquiry counter. A modest
maiden of 40 or thereabouts tripped gush-
ingly up to the counter and blushingly con-
fided that she wanted to see an Australian
sergeant. "What’s his name?” was the not
unnatural query. “Jack,” gurgled the maid.
“Jack who?” "Well, really I don’t know.
You see, I met him the other day and a
friend of his told me that his wound has
broken down and that he’s in bed again.”
“But you must realise that there are 2500
patients in this hospital and about half of them
are Australians. If you can give me any-
thing to go on, I’ll try to find him for you.”
Eve pondered. "Well, he’s tall, and dark,
and handsome; and”—in a burst of confi-
dence—“his father owns a place called Tas-
mania !”
* * * *
“The Old Soldier”: There is a ferocious
old rule of war which hasn’t been strictly
enforced since Magdeburg. Up to a certain
point artillery fire is very effective; but
when the works are levelled it is necessary
to bring up infantry to take possession of
the ground. Artillery kills or wounds com-
paratively few men unless they are standing
exposed in open ground ; but when the works
are beaten flat it can make a desperate
defence against infantry. That was the rea-
son for the old rule that, if a garrison per-
sists in defending itself after a practicable
breach has been made in the works, the be-
siegers may put it to the sword if they take
the place by assault or blockade. We have
got it into our heads that it is only in this
war that humane ideas have been thrown
overboard, but there were old practices at
which we should even now shudder.
“Troopering R.8.R.” : I met my first Ger-
man officer the morning after we captured
Beersheba. He was posing as a Zeppelin,
and was decidedly swollen-headed. I sup-
pose he was the best kind of Prussian you
could wish to see—he was very dead. Later,
however, his manner became more offensive,
so I parted with two piastres to a member
of the Egyptian Labor Corps, and the proud
Hun hit a well-bottom some 20 feet down.
He was wearing a pair of riding-boots,
which he lost some days later owing to a
venturesome Anzac coveting them and, after
resisting the temptation for 24 hours, brav-
ing the multitudinous odors in the pit. The
glorv of war!
* * * *
If the Hawkesbury bridge (N.S.W.) is
blown up or blown down the greatest rail-
way system in Australia will be cut in
two. Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide —that is
to say, nearly half of N. S. Wales, all of
Victoria-, all S. Australia and Westralia—
will be on one side of the break. The rest
of N. S. Wales and all Queensland will be
on the other side. North of the smashed
link will be more than three-quarters of the
Commonwealth’s coal supply. South of it
will be more than three-fourths of the Com-
monwealth’s population, so demand will be
on one side of a river 3000 ft. wide and the
supply on the other. There is no alternative
route to Sydney, except by a detour of 260
miles. The bridge might be rebuilt in two
years, provided it wasn’t blown up again.
The matter is one of supreme importance,
especially if German vessels like the Wolf
are about, carrying bomb-dropping aero-
planes, and more especially if an obliging con-
federate helped with signals. If the oblig-
ing confederate put dynamite under the
bridge bombs might not be wanted. So to
be armed against all hazards the railway
points at Hawkesbury Bridge were lately
put in charge of an unregistered alien with
a German name, and he was last week fined
20s. and costs for non-registration. He was
described as belonging to Switzerland, a
composite land where t\Vo-thirds of the
population is German. When the person
with the Hun name was being fined the
magistrate “expressed surprise.” He seems
a person very easily startled.
* * * *
“Boyd Cable”: I am at present with “the
Night Fliers,” whose job is to bomb Ger-
many. On their strength is an Australian —
an Adelaide man, I think ; Wald, by name —
who recently put up a good show. He was
out to drop the eggs the other night and
when about 10 to 15 miles over the line his
pressure-feed went dud. If that’s too tech-
nical I may explain that petrol is forced to
the engine by pressure and in the savage cold
these nights—round about 30 degrees of frost
in the air—the pump gadget is apt to play
tricks. Wald turned back and when on the
safe side started tinkering round and with-
out landing managed to rectify the trouble.
Trouble of this sort would have quite justi-
fied any man’s coming down and chucking
the show, but Wald turned and went back
to have another whirl at it. When some
distance over he again struck misery; escap-
ing steam blowing in his face and the behavi-

“And always speak the truth. I never told

“When’s a fair time to begin, Dad?”
“Neddie”; I was a kid when John Red-
mond came to Ballarat and stirred Bungaree
to the depths of its Hibernian being with
his eloquence. “What have you done for
Ireland?” was the burden of one deliverance
in the old Alfred Hall. When all was said
the exuberant admirers of Redmond filed
up for a grip of his fist, and Redmond’s
greeting to the more enthusiastic was, “What
have you done for Ireland?” Came O’Neil,
a big red devil of a man. “What havq you
done for Ireland?” said Redmond. “Done
fer Oireland!” cried the O’Neil. “Haven’t
I this night put me brick troo the windy of
Parson Anthrews?” Parson Andrews was a
rampant anti-llome Ruler, who had been
attacking Redmond with great vehemence.
A policeman who overheard O'Neil reached
for him, and next day O’Neil was tried
for the confessed offence, and fined heavily.
As O’Neil left court, a proud boy, another
Irishman, larger, wilder and more fiery,
stepped up and felled him to the pavement.
“Take that for the dirty liar ye are,” said
he, “stealin’ away the glory from a better
man. You know as well as anny man livin’
’twas me troo that brick!”
“R.8.R.”: Pacifists and others who still
doubt Germany’s Eastern dream should see
Beersheba. A railway station about 300
yards long, with excellent stone stores, all
red-tiled, makes the heart of a Sydneyite
thump with the thoughts of red-roofed
Mosman. There is also a fine two-storeyed
hospital over which the Turkish Red Cres-
cent still flew when we entered the town,
an Administrative building of like design,
and an extremely imposing mosque bearing
unmistakable Hunnish footprints, evidently
built with the intention of showing Jacko
chat the great William has Mahomet as
well as Gott in the partnership. All build-
ings are floored with marble slabs, very
cool in hot weather and easily cleansed.
Before the fateful August of ’l4 not one stone
of these reared its head above the dusty
plain. And this is not all. Even under
the pressure of war a magnificent park has
been laid out in the centre of the town and
planted with Australian eucalypts and with
groves of wattle, all now with about three
years’ growth. It is under these trees of
their native land that the heroic dead from
Australia are buried.
FLOWER OF WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The soundless planes of trance
Had whirled me past a hundred sleeping years,
And dimly, far below, through haze of tears,
I saw the Wound of France.
From little hill to town,
From road to river silver-string it ran—
An endless hurt: and, like a swooning man,
I drifted slowly down.
bo, all the growing things
Were steeped in red, and red, and red again,
As though dead years still fanned their blooms of pain
With unforgetting wings.
Here swayed red violets;
There grass was red, and marched with crimson
plumes
To where tall scarlet lilies spilt perfumes
Above red mignonettes.
A daisy bowed its head
Vainly to hide its shapeless, bloodied spots
From eyes of jubilant forget-me-nots—
Forget-me-nots of red.
But, checkered on the sod
Among the flowers, stood rows of crosses white.
They wore no red : they flamed with living light—
The glory-gold of God.
MAOIIILAND.
O. N. GILLESPIE.

“Jacko”: Just to pile a little more on to
the big military-boot bungle disclosed by
the Defeuee Commission’s report, allow me
to explain the system of hoofing the Com-
monwealth’s defenders. For the past three
years, parades and other regular work for
the Citizen Forces have been suspended, the
equipment recalled and
the issue of clothing-
cut right out. But for
some reason the boot
issue has remained.
And this is the way'
the leather is handed
out. The various
units are called into
camp for eight days’
training. After sev-
eral days have gone,
word comes round for
a boot parade, where-
upon each trainee is
obliged to display the
condition of his foot-
wear to the O.C. If
the officer thinks that
a new pair would come
in handy for any par-
ticular man, down
goes the name for the
tans on the strength
of appearances. The
other fellows, who
might have happened
to don a decent pair
of uppers, go without,
even though everyone
is on the same work.
In a recent camp at
Liverpool (N.S.W.)
I saw college students,
doctors’ sons and other
well-to-do lads pre-
sented with boots by
kind Mr, Pear ce.
Others, on eight bob a
day in ordinary life,
were compelled to use their own property
simply because it happened to' be usable.

“0.K.” : John Madden ought to be remem-
bered by the Melbourne Presbyterian
Ladies’ College for his excellent advice to
its young ladies. In his Supreme Court
capacity Madden was merely a strict moral-
ist who judged, the ill-regulated by his own
powers of self-restraint, and talked foolishly ;
but as a paternal adviser of growing gen-
tility lie was a Madden who had carefully
studied the ways of young womanhood in
his polite family circle. What he didn’t
know about the orthodox tricks of the femi-
nine trade was not worth knowing; pro-
bably because he watched the tricks being
practised from the beginning and therefore
saw- through them so easily, he begged the
dear, clever girls to moderate their fond
belief in their own cleverness. “Young ladies,”
said the benign orator, “give themselves a
lot of undeserved credit for being able to
’get round’ men and ‘tw-ist them round their
little finger,’ and so forth. What they don’t
understand is that men. as a rule, are not
being deceived at all. They see you getting
round them, and because the process is very-
pleasant they are apt to make it last a long
time.” Perhaps John Madden over-esti-
mated the number of the men who are not
actually fooled by Gwendoline, but his esti-
mate of the amount of intellect required for
fooling the fooler was not a bit too small.
Probably for every woman who deludes a
man there are five men who can delude a
woman; so if she be half as clever as she
thinks herself, the guile of the male must be
immense.

intrigue. It never occurs to them that such things
could in the circumstances happen otherwise. God
forgive me, I lie and say it is so.

* * #• *
From France :
We still have 11 of the old horses which we
brought from Australia in October, 1914, the best
and hardiest prads I’ve seen. Throughout last winter
they never had a bit of shelter excepting a rug, and
they were in mud and snow up to their hocks. I’ve
seen their tails and fetlocks frozen as hard as a rock,
and to water them the drivers had to break
through 2ft. of ice. All the feed they got was,
approximately, 91b. of oats and 51b. of hay per day—
nothing more, though sometimes they were working
right round the clock.
Al! the good Aussies don’t do their bit on
two legs.
* * * *
The name of the German raider Wolf was
a grievance in itself, for it reminded thou-
sands of people of the hackneyed saying
about the wolf in sheep’s clothing, and they
repeated it till it became a misery. The
name had no points about it, anyhow. Some
centuries ago, before the drill-sergeant had
kicked all. the humor out of the German, that
person did actually perpetrate a jest on the
high seas. It was in the days when the
Hanseatic League was in its glory, and the
Victualling Brethren were doing wholesale
piracy on the .waters. The League grew wild
at the plunder of its goods and chattels, and
organised a fleet to argue with the Brethren.
Its admiral was a mayor in his spare time
and a sort of universal provider in business
hours, lie called his flagship the Brindle
Cow, to imply thiit he was a man of peace,
and only went to war as a matter of neces-
sity. The admiral of the Brethren called his
flagship the Mad Dog, to explain that he was
a holy terror by choice and a blood-drinker
by preference. The moral is that there is no
one so terrible as a universal provider suffer-
ing from oppression, unless it be, as in the
case of Spinola, the Italian, a banker who
has taken up arms to recover his overdraft.
The Brindle Cow and its consorts swept the
Brethren off the seas,, and the Hanseatic
admiral went home without knowing that
there was anything comical about his
arrangements.
BULGED OUT OF HER CLASS.
“I thought she said she’d accept him if he
enlisted.”
“Yes; but he’s developed into such a fine big

“Pork and Beans”: These English learn
slowly, and there is great danger that
they won’t learn quickly enough to
save themselves and the Empire. On
leave in Blighty, with another N.C.O.
I strolled into the Coal Hole in the
Strand. Noticing “Private Bar” on a door
in the rear, we walked in and found three
civilians drinking. The Hebe behind the
counter told us that “only officers and gentle-
men” were served there. I boiled over and
asked to see the manager. Up bustled a
dame with: “I’m the manageress and it’s
quite right.” I told her in our plain Aus-
tralian language what I thought of her rules
and the probable future of a country that
tolerated such things. Put me in mufti, and
this bar will serve me. Put me in khaki, as
proof that I’m doing my duty to my country,
and this bar. like hundreds more, will turn
me down. Could there be a greater insult
to the uniform? Yet the thing is quite
typical. The uniform is dirt. Do these
people really deserve to be saved?

“Ellae”: Re “J.l.F.’s” comforting p'ar
(B. 14/2/’18) about the, Lost Soldiers’ De-
partment at the Vatican. We, whose last
authentic record of one of our soldier boys
is a “Missing and believed prisoner’ of war”
some 10 months old, rushed the Bulletin
round to the house of a kindly returned
Priest in our neighborhood to ask for fur-
ther particulars. After courteously giving
us every possible help in the matter, he told
us that he has repeatedly tried to have men-
tion made of this matter in the daily papers,
but has been unable to get it. How’s that
for vicious sectarianism in war-time, when
thousands like ourselves are frantically seek-
ing any means to supplement the dreadful
"No further information available” which
threatens to shadow the rest of our lives?

“4 x 2”: Here’s my experience of the
“help” the returned man gets. I enlisted in
November, 1914, and went away with the
second contingent. After a spell in Gallipoli
I was invalided and discharged. Having been
a pearler before enlisting, I naturally tried
to return to the work. A small pearling
lease, insufficient to support a man, came
up for tender, and after protracted negotia-
tions I was granted.it on very favorable
terms. Later a larger lease came up. I put
in in the ordinary way, and before the result
was announced was informed by
the Colonial Secretary (whose
decision it rested upon) that the
matter was between myself and
a civilian whose tender was 2U
pounds higher than mine. I
offered to make up my tender to
the same amount, but, finally, the
lease was granted to the civilian
on the grounds that he had six
small children. I have since
found out that his children are
two boys over the age of 18 years,
two girls over 20, one boy over
16, the others over 12. But,
better than children, he had
several members of Parliament to push his
barrow. 1 think I had better go back to the
was as a Y.M.C.A. reinforcement. They have
refused me in any other capacity.
“Tito” : Australian daily newspapers were
permitted to grossly libel the Italians when
the llun fell upon them, and they have done
practically nothing to correct their misinfor-
mation. Their story about the Germans
weakening the Italian fighting spirit with a
few showers of pamphlets is still widely
prevalent in suburban circles, though the
fable received no publicity in leading Italian
journals. Aosta’s Third Army showed bril-
liance in dodging punishment on the way back
from the Carso, and Bersaglieri and Alpini
exacted two corpses for every one against
three or four times their own strength in
ferocious in-fighting. The Italians also con-
trived unbelievable ingenuities in the lagoons
about Venice ; they invented floating islands
that poured out annihilation from camou-
flaged guns, but apparently there was no
Australian pen to paint the achievement.
That the retreat was a great performance is
proved bv the fact that the Italians are #till
holding the Piave line, though the British
and French reinforcements commenced to dig
in on the much stronger Adige line, believ-
ing it impossible that they could hold the
enemy pack from that river. The Italians
altogether showed a character for doggedness
in a terrible situation that merited the loud-
est publicity. The shameful thing is that
Australian papers imputed Boloism to Italy
at the very moment the Commonwealth was
voting down Conscription.
COMPLETE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The German offensive plans are being completed.”
The plans are quite complete. The gathered hosts
Shudder a little at the shell-torn rear.
There are the usual raids, the usual boasts
To tell that murder’s triumph-hour is near;
And up and down the line the cannon roar,
While seas of flame from hidden throats outpour.
Though wilted Russia play the Kaiser’s game,
Releasing armies that are ripe to die,
Though Krupp with restless wheels feed flame to
flame
And tanks crawl down the mud with burning eye,
Things can’t be worse than those that made last year
The worst that ever plagued the hemisphere.
Guns, steel and men, the plans are quite complete;
Yet with strange confidence the nations stand
That shock of shocks with valiant heart to meet
And tear the triumph from the despot’s hand.
They stood before; and they will stand again
Victorious in the drenching fiery rain.
And see, they come! Dense wave on wave they break
Against the rocky front of freedom’s pride.
In agony the hills asd valleys shake
At the vast swirl of the outgoing tide.
And then, across, the wreckage backward hurled,
Comes in slow light the morning of the world.
The plans are quite complete. The word “Advance”
Makes a bold headline on the printed sheet;
And, with the whip of scorn, from tortured France
' Begins at last the Teuton's red retreat. .
No pause, no rest; but, in a storm of steel,
Hot terror presses at his flying heel!
May these things be? Hope ever stirs arid prods
The ’ heart to take the optimistic view ;
But counting fairly all the risks and odds,
Our crowd looks good enough to see it through.
And while the challenge through the darkness peals
The Kaiser’s wondering how hanging feels.
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.

The great Griffith had his Reinforcements
report rgady within a week. It has been
Griffith’s plan t® sit at a job until he has
finished it. He never put a case away to
tackle it fresh in the morning. He always
preferred to follow it into the morning.
Gone west at 25, Adrian Consett Stephen,
lieutenant of field artillery in France.
Young Stephen came of the well-known Syd-
ney legal family, and was himself about to
start at the N. S. Wales Bar when the
world wqs turned upside down in l:u4. He
got a commission with British artillery, and
in three muddy years in Flanders he had
figured tAvice in despatches and annexed the
French Croix de Guerre as well as the Mili-
tary Cross. At Sydney Uni. a few years ago
Stephen was about the brightest spark in the
rather dull Hermes, the ’Varsity magazine;
and his letters from the Front to its pages
in later years Avere always worth reading.
He promised to be a brilliant playwright,
and some of his essays in that direction have
been staged by the University Dramatic
Society and the Sydney Stage Club.
In the old days Hughes Avas very fond of
a game of euchre. He always insisted on
having the joker in. The habit seems to
have stuck.
WAR.
JONES: “Bali ! EVERY man should go."
“M.C.”: The death of Col. George Lane-
Mullins revives memories of the Dabor
Bazaar, conducted by Messrs. Mullins and
Maher, in Ritt-street, Sydney, just about
where City Tatt.’s raises its blatant voice.
The original Mullins Avas coachman to Arch-
bishop Bolding, if a Bulletin paragraphist
of many years ago was correct. This par.
said: —
How proud Avould Mick Mullins, when he was
driving the carriage for Archbishop Polding, have
been if he could have seen his two sons going off to
Oxford. Sydney colleges are not good enough for
Sydney natives.
George Mullins did not go to Oxford, how-
ever, but to Dublin. Before he matriculated
he went from the Grammar to St. Kilda
House, newly opened by the Jesuit Fathers.
This was just after the awful turn-up at
Bathurst, when Mr. Kenna was refused
burial in the R.C. portion of the cemetery
because he wanted to send his boy to another
school. This so frightened the recalcitrants
that St. Kilda House had a great influx, in-
cluded in which were the scions of the
houses of Mullins, Maher and Hughes.
George Mullins was at this school for some
months and learned more Greek than he had
dreamed of previously. After matriculation
here, he went to Dublin, and so on to liand-
wick No. 4. If the authorities had given him
a square deal, he would have been there still.
Archbish. Mannix is keeping strange com-
pany at Kew (Vic.). Across the river from
his palace is another large, building which
the Vic. Government proposes to turn into
a Venereal Diseases Hospital. As the man-
sion next door is the home of John Wren,
who will probably object to the existence of
such a neighbor, there is every likelihood
that the Government will change its mind.
Apart from the ethics of the question, no
Government should have an opinion contrary
to ex-Citizen Wren.
SUNSET.
The Rev. John Saunders, father of Dr.
Renwick’s widow—the estimable lady died
in Sydney the other day—was an absent-
minded hardshell. When he finally left Aus-
tralia for England in IS4B he forgot all
about £3OO in the Barrack-street Savings
Bank ; and probably it would have lain there
until this day had not somebody taken pity
on the little man when disaster struck him
at the time the land-boom smashed. Renwick
was able to prove his wife’s claim, and the
accumulations came in very handy indeed.
John Saunders, by the way, was lawyer as
well as parson. He was the first shepherd
of the first Baptist flock in Australia, and
opened a tabernacle behind St. Andrew’s
Cathedral in 1835.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” : The Mayor of
Balmain told Governor Davidson at a func-
tion last week that Governor Hunter sold
Balmain to Dr. Balmain for five shillings.
Wrong. The site of the now populous
suburb was a free grant to Balmain; and
when the latter w r as going back to England
he thought so little of it that lie sold it to a
medical friend in India for 10 shillings. The
friend did not hold it, either, but cut it up
and sold it in lumps.
Daniel Harvey Hogan, of Brunswick
(Vic.), who came in 1852 as an A.B. on the
Fantome and has just died a Sunday-school
superintendent at 84, was one of the blue-
jackets who marched from Melbourne to
Ballarat to suppress the Eureka rising.
Adam Forsyth, the cheerful money-spinner
who had been looking after the odd ha’penny
all his life, and got most of his joy of life
out of it —for many years past he was one of
the chiefs of Burns- Philp’s—left £311,000
behind him. An old bachelor, most of his
wealth goes to nephews and nieces in Scot-
land ; but he scattered Australian bequests
liberally-—gave £5OO apiece to most of the
working heads, in B.P.’s and to numerous
friends, £lOOO to Colonel Burns to do what
he likes with, enough for a new cottage at
the Burnside Homes, which he had already
helped, though in his lifetime it was almost
the unpardonable sin to say that he had
opened his purse for anything.
Billjim in Egypt lost a good and remark-
able friend when Baroness Wentworth, better
known as Lady Blunt, Byron’s grand-
daughter, died a few weeks ago in Cairo.
She learnt drawing from Ruskin, excelled in
rapid sketching, was a mathematician and
linguist and a distinguished Arabic scholar.
She married Wilfred Blunt in ’69, and being
a great horsewoman accompanied lnm
through the wildest part of the Mesopo-
tamian and Arabian deserts. She inherited
the Wentworth barony, descending from her
grandmother, Lady Byron.
Robert Algie, 55, killed the other day by a
tram on St. Kilda-road, was one of Colonel
Tom Price’s instructors during the years
when the Vic. Government was training its
Mounted Riflemen. In those times Algie
\Jas in charge at Hamilton, where he
straightened the backbone of many a gay
young squatter-spark and nailed in the right
idea of duty. The news of his passing will
be received with sorrow by many a soldier
in far fields.

A noted Westralian goldfields man, Bob
Alderson, was killed in France lately. Pri-
vate Alderson was a few years ago the prin-
cipal owner of the rich Neta mine, at Edju-
dina, and carried out in his spare time a
multitude of public duties, being, among
other things, the dentist of the waybaek camp.
The dentist's chair was a sturdy mulga,
in the fork of which the victims rested their
necks, a bolt being fixed across to prevent
withdrawal while Bob went to work with
instruments made on the anvil of the mine
smithy. Later, he acquired a modern kit
and put many a job through with the finish
of an expert. Ultimately, he was made a
J.P., and the first offender dealt with was
an old mate. The conviction was summary,
for the evidence of the prosecuting constable
—a black eye and sundry other disruptions
—was beyond explaining away. “I’ll fine
you a quid,” said his Worship, sternly.
“Blime, Bob, ain’t you a bit strong?” the
culprit wailed. “Hang it all, Billy,” ex-
claimed the presiding SoTon, “you don t
think the blanky dough is for me ; it’s for
the Guv-ment.” Bob, in the soothing atmo-
sphere of the adjacent pub, afterwards
refunded the quid and there was harmony
again. A great-hearted man was Bob Aider-
son.

of them, are so good that, wanting more,

In a recent list of Westralians killed in
France was the name of one whom insiders
knew as the daring spirit responsible for
the big gold “lift” from the Bullfinch mine
in the height of the boom. With the whole
surface on the qui vive, some £4OOO worth
of metal (the monthly clean-up) suddenly
vanished, and the plant was never located.
It was in the adjacent mulga and was dis-
posed of much later on when the jaded
sleuths withdrew from the hunt. The sus-
pected marauder, as brave a man as ever
lived, died leading his men on, for he had
won his way to officer rank and also held a
medal for valor.
They are hardy old chaps in the country
where the Federal capital should be. At the
Bombala Show recently, Edward Irill, a
Snowy River youth of 73, with a wonderful
seat in the saddle, competed in the high
jumps.
“0.K.”: Lord Brassey’s habit of drinking
“colonial” beer in out-of-the-way pubs was
doubtless the outcome of his inborn disposi-
tion to mix with “common people.” There
is a tradition that his father, Tom Brassey,
began life as a navvy, but as a matter of
fact he sprang from ancient English yeoman
stock, and started his career in a surveyor's
office. Thus he came to be a railway con-
tractor when railways were, new to the
world. Ultimately he and his partner em-
ployed 75,000 navvies on contracts amount-
ing to £35,000,000, and he was in close and
sympathetic touch with the working-man
and his beer, and was full of appreciation
of the value of both, when the future Lord
Brassey was born.
Young I). T. Moore, commanding the 3rd
N. S. Wales Battalion somewhere in France,
is now T only one step off a brigadier’s crossed
swords, and wears the French Croix de Guerre
next to his British D.S.O. and Belgian Ordre
de la Couronne. This unusual youth, who
is not yet 2G, is one of Colonel Bob Owen’s
bright pupils, and succeeded to the charge
of the 3rd when its previous infant com-
mander, Lieut.-Colonel II al Price, went West.
From the neighborhood of Petrograd: —
Enter Madame Kollontay, the first of her sex to
hold Cabinet rank. She is a Bolshevik and any
other old thing that assures Liberty even at the
expense of Equality and Fraternity, and her coif-
fured head is crowded with languages, economic
statistics, and various arts and sciences. It is
said that she entered the Bolshevik Cabinet at the
invitation of Lenin, but to assume that Lenin was
alone responsible would be showing scant respect
for her character and charms. She is a rather
pretty woman, who has employed a corsetiere to give
every advantage to her flowing lines. She has also
big, dark' eyes that have room for other expressions
beside indignation with the bourgeoisie. The sight
of this perfectly-shod and manicured lady in the
phantom frocks and frillies of a pre-war mannequin
would appear rather grotesque at a Soviet discus-
sion, but her comrades found her lusciousness com-
patible with the highest revolutionary aspirations.
For some years she did good work for her party in
Switzerland, and out-distanced the most advanced
by headlining marriage among her list of things
anathema. The lady, who is a little over 30, divorced
her own husband some time ago. Before she became
a Bolshevik she was a school-teacher in a village
not far from where Mrs. Sukhomlinoff, wife of the
notorious ex-Minister of War, started putting her
beauty to use.
EXTENUATING CIRCUMSTANCES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“In some countries charges of corruption have been
laid at the door of the Army administration. Hap-
pily, nothing of the kind in regard to Australia is
mooted in these reports.”— G. F. Pearce in reply to
charges of shameful bungling and mismanagement in
the Defence Department.
Of waste and gross extravagance
Let carping critics prate ;
But that I ever robbed a church
Or sought a girl’s fair fame to smirch
' Are things they dare not state !
Of coin misspent and pay-sheets lost
Talk on, if you’re inclined ;
But recollect I have not yet
Been gaoled for larceny or debt,
Or been for “language” fined.
If you should mention Ilowell-Price
I’ll answer: “History
Tells us of Ministers whose lives
Were spent with other persons’ wives—
You can’t say that of met”
In common fairness, cease to howl
Of blundering and loss,
And of my virtues sometimes speak—■
I’ve never been before the beak
For playing pitch and toss!
VICTORIA.

This is a holding co., its assets consisting
almost exclusively of the shares in the British
Australasian Tobacco Co. (which combines
the Cameron and part of the Dixson busi-
nesses), the States Tobacco Co. (which in-
cludes the tobacco and cigar businesses of
Sutton and Co. and Jacobs, Hart and Co. and
the other part of the Dixson business) and
W. D. and 11. O. Wills (Australia). The
amalgamation was effected in 1903; but it
was not until the last few years that Aus-
tralian investors came freely into the huge
concern, which has a (i p.c. cumulative" pref.
capital of £1,484,727 and an ordinary capital
of £4,000,000, of which £3,750,300 is issued
—all £1 shares. The cos. whose shares this
co. holds have an independent existence; but
they don’t publish their proceedings to the
world, or even to the shareholders in the
holding co. The latter doesn’t even say pre-
cisely what the aggregate dividends received
amount to: it exhibits all its income in one
leap—“ Direct net profits and dividends,”
which for last year were £555,314.
This, it will be noticed, is £15,771
less than the profits shown in the
table. That amount hasn’t gone in
management exes, (these are not disclosed,
being apparently deducted from the profits).
but is money used by the directors in
purchasing shares for the benefit of em-
ployees. During the past four years the
additions to capital in consequence of these
purchases have been :
1914 .. ..£33,536 I 1916 .. ..£32,285
1915 . . . . 38,708 | 1917 . . . . 15,771
This makes a total of £120,300 in the four
years, and means an addition of nearly
£15,000 a year to the wages of the participa-
ting employees. The reduction in the amount
handed to the trustees for the past year is
apparently the result of reduced net earnings
by the co'. The “direct net profits and divi-
dends” have moved back from £577,239 in
the year ended January, 1910, to £509,259 in
the following year, to £555,314 in the year
just passed. This is an English co., and
probably the falling away is due to heavier
taxation. At any rate, the fact that the
employees got only about half the usual
bonus is likely to take some of the sharp
edge off the cry that is bound to go up over-
last week’s rise in the price of tobacco. True,
the co. doesn’t guarantee any profit-sharing
distribution: tlier thing is purely experimen-
tal. and is, presumably, only to be continued
so long as the management finds that it pays.
In most cases an employee can benefit after
two years’ service—provided lie (or she)
doesn’t draw a salary of £250 or more a year.
After five years he can sell the shares, if he
wants tobut as he is getting 12 p.c. he
mostly doesn’t want to. After eight years
the shares, which until then are held, by
trustees, go on to the register in the name of
the employee: they are his absolutely. Tak-
ing the present value of shares the employees’
interest in the business is now worth very
nearly a quarter of a million, all accumu-
lated in. say. five years; and a holding of
that size ought to make the employees feel
personally interested. Also it is certainly not
going to make the Labor politician any more
eager to nationalise the business.

The record harvest for the past year has
been gathered out of a fool's paradise. The
oldest inhabitant cannot recall a- time when
the community seemed more prosperous. The
fact that we are on a gorgeous burst with a
p.n. apparently worries nobody—least of all
the politicians. The average citizen might
better realise the position if one bright morn-
ing he awoke and found this under his
door: —
Mu. Cornstalk.
Dr. to Cohen Unlimited.
Debt contracted on your behalf by X. S.
Wales politicians .. .. . . £7O 0 0

Federal politicians .. . . . . 20 13 5
Advanced to you to help win the war . . 30 0 0
£l2O 19 5
P.S. —Note that each of your dependants owes a
similar amount, and that you are responsible for and
have to meet their obligations as well as your own.
P.P.S. —A settlement of portion of above amount
will be necessary at an early date.
That was something like the actual position
of every Cornstalk as at Dec. SI last. And
to pay for our part in the war another £1
goes on the bill every five weeks.
Because of the splashing of loan money,
the magnificent prices we are getting for
many of our products, and the consequent
high wages, Gordon’s, who run a big cash-
order business in conjunction with a number of
retailers, as well as a cash and t.p. furnishing
business of their own. have naturally been get-
ting fat. The cash-order business is a lucrative
one; Gordon’s will lend you up to a fiver
to purchase goods for “cash” in other shops.
The co. receives Is. in the £ for 20 weeks’
accommodation. That charge would be equal
to 13 p.c. p.a.. even if there were no repay-
ments by instalments. With weekly repay-
ments it is nearer 30 p.c.; but out of that the
co. has to pay collecting and book-keeping
exes. Also it has to cover bad debts, which,
however, are surprisingly few. Neverthe-
less, the co, makes provision for the bad
marks by reserving a proportion of the
profits:—

But look at the foot of the first table and
you will see that the co. is about to dis-

also that it is only three years since £40,000
was ladled out in the same way. That means

for all contingencies in the past, and that
they have been able to give shareholders the
benefit of things having turned out right.

The first-column figures now represent this
conglomerate: “Leasehold property, war
loan, debentures, mortgages, shares in other
cos., cash in hand and on deposit at short
call, stock on hand, fittings, plant, etc.”
The co.’s holding of property is big, and
altogether they are a healthy-looking lot of
assets. About the “sundry debtors” item,
The Bulletin hesitates to make compari-
sons with earlier years in the matter of
advances to customers, as it is not unlikely
that there was a certain amount of cash
formerly included in the item. Anyhow,
five years ago, when £24,423 of profit was
made, sundry debtors owed £103,431 ; last
year an increased profit of £6OOO was made
with the sundry debtors item reduced by
over £20,000. Undoubtedly the co. lias built
up a very fine business. Whether it can
maintain with safety the present turnover
in the dull days ahead is very doubtful. But
if it can do half as well it will still show
very excellent figures.

The additions to the Insurance Reserve may
not be all true profit, because the co. carries
its own Workmen’s Compensation risks: but
it is pretty obvious that the amount credited
to Dividend Equalisation Reserve is regarded
as good honest lay-by. In addition something
of unknown dimensions is written off against
depreciation. No idea of this sum can
be got by comparing the mine capital
account, because during the past two years
the co. lias been spending a lot of money in
opening up the Pelton mine at Maitland and
supplying it with a railway. The latter is
now completed. At December, ’l5, just after
the co. had Bought this freehold, assets other
than mine, buildings, plant and rolling stock
were £36,435 net. Notwithstanding the
surplus profit earned meantime and the
money derived from the sale of surface
lands the corresponding figures are now
£17,323. This means that the funds have
been cut into by less than £20,000 to provide
the new mine ; but a good deal of money will
still have to he spent there. Considering
th(> bumps that the coal business has had
since the war started, the Wallsend share-
holders have nothing to feel very unhappy
about.

Owing to better results from the residential
side of the business the directors managed
to squeeze out a profit despite bigger bills for
provisions and wages. But the figures are
still disappointing. When the co. set out to
establish a first-class residential pub—-the
Pacific-—on the Manly beach, everything
seemed rosy. The promoters, working on
1010 figures, reckoned on an annual profit of
about £7400 after allowing for £2OOO or so
for depreciation and smashages. The net
profit for the past five years has not aver-
aged a sixth of the estimate. Just when the
Pacific was becoming known to the right
Class of customers the war intervened ; then
six o’clock closing followed and knocked holes
in bar receipts: and all the time the spectre
of increasing costs played grab with the bed
and tucker profits. All the shareholders
have got so far has been one 5 p.c. div. Mean-
time the cash position has been moving this
way:—

As written down the fixed assets are about
£6OO less than they were five years ago; so
if the allowance for wear and tear, renewals,
etc., has been ample the co. is about £5OOO
better off than it was as at December, 1912.
Capital is £35,000, in fully paid £1 shares;
there are debentures totalling, with interest,
£20,202 ; and the co. is leaning on the bank
for £5594. After the others have been fed,
there is only a rare crumb left for share-
holders under present trading conditions.

This co, sold its business for an interest in
the Combine when the latter was formed some
years ago, and its brewery and malthouse pro-
perty at Abbotsford has ever since been on
its hands. Since the close of the financial
year, however, the directors say, they have
reopened negotiations for a sale and expect
that one will he effected, and they would
hardly uncork that much if the business wasn’t
almost as good as clinched. Apart from that
asset, which has been written down from
£40.000 to £16,000, the co. owns shares with
a face value of £70.1-5 in the Combine and
£8094 in cash, hotel property (£2609) and
sundries. The war hasn’t done the Com-
bine any good, mainly because of the early
closing for which it was made the pretext.

undivided net profit, a good deal of it is;
and that is an astonishing performance on a
live share capital of only £1462.

out of that fund to the undertaking have to
pay interest to it. The sinking fund is
unsatisfactory in that it is not of a general
character, but applies only to one loan.
No new line was opened during the year, but
an extension was in progress. There was on
tin* old lines some extra traffic, unfortu-
nately at an increased cost which almost ate
up the increased revenue;—

Last year’s was the heaviest bill per mile
since the Trust began. But circumstances
are quite abnormal. Apart from the wages
bill, which may or may not be cut down
when the cost of living becomes normal, there
have been extraordinary increases in the
prices of many of the Trust’s necessary com-
modities. Engineer Dix quotes a few (com-
parisons are witli 1914) : Steel tyres. 191
p.c.; car lamps, 172 p.c.; bar iron. 153 p.c.;
copper wire, 100 p.c.; white lead, 84 p.c.;
oil and grease, 04 p.c. ; cotton waste, 00 p.c.
—and so on. In all the circumstances the
surplus may be regarded as satisfactory. It
ought to be twice as large, of course, in
order to make proper provision for the future
and for the repayment of capital; but as the
Trust, isn’t in the kind of business in which
extra costs can be passed on, it is doing as
well as could reasonably be expected. But
there shouldn’t be any talk of concessions
until it does a great deal better.
From a trustworthy man in the business :
The N. S. Wales coal-miner is in a rebellious mood,
and it is foolish not to recognise it. AVe shall be
in trouble if we don’t. Mostly it is on the question
of the owners’ refusal to re-employ a certain num-
ber of alleged leaders after'the recent big disturbance.
Some of these men, exercising something akin to
hypnotic influence over their mates, exulted in lay-
ing a mine idle over some trivial matter that a
level-headed man would have adjusted in five minutes.
These men are out for sabotage and such-like madness.
No sane community can have any sympathy for these
dangerous fools. But it is equally true that some
of the men are now out of work for no other reason
than that they kept the managers up to the mark in
observing awards—in the payment of full com-
pensation to men working in deficient places, for
instance. As a result of the removal of these spokes-
men, miners in some pits have to take whatever the
bosses offer them, and if they protest they are
reminded that the alternative is 14 daj's’ notice.
The pendulum of domination has swung from one
side to the other. This was exemplified at the
recent conference, when Baddeley (for the em-
ployees) objected to the stand-and-deliver attitude
of the. proprietors, and Morgan (for the employers)
reminded him that it was not so long since he
(Baddeley) was in that position. The one and only
solution of these recurring snarls is the annihilation
of the contract system and the substitution of an
adequate daily wage. A man would then be able
to look his mate in thc#6ye without mentally calcu-
lating the tonnage he was filling or how he fared
with the boss in the matter of compensation.
Baker’s Creek, Hillgrove (N.S.W.), is in
the market again after a liquidation and
years of travail. A float was pulled off in
Melbourne per broker Moore to test a small
reef got by the tributors at 1700 ft.. some-
where west of the run of the old Baker’s
Creek reef. That was a narrow shoot of
stone out of which Adelaide snicked £250.000
in divs. in the sweet long ago. Latest bul-
letins from No. 17 level read like old times:
winze down on soz. stone, stope on 2]oz.,
No. 16 level winze on 3oz. But it takes a
lot of Bin. stone to make a ton ; nevertheless
the mine is so well opened up that it may
have another brief lease of life.
* * * *
Welcome to the Vie. Mines Department,
Sam Barnes, of Walhalla. The Hon. Sam
is a lean slab of a mountaineer, who, after
Dawson chucked up mine-managing the
Long Tunnel, got the job. Besides being
a good miner Barnes is a good fellah, with
as many yarns at call as ever had old L. L.
Smith. Of course, coming from Walhalla,
they may not be equally nutty, but if one
can last till 3 a.m. with Barnes, one will
learn that they have a flavor of their own.
Barnes’s electorate has the M’orwell brown
coal open-cut in it, so that long-suffering
industry may at last get a move on. That
the London-owned Melbourne Electric Co.
has put up. the costs of lighting and power
10 p.c. to users should hearten Barnes to
the job of turning Morwell to account.
*** • *
There isn't anything in the half-year’s re-
ports of Duke Extended, Ararat (Vic.), to
suggest that divs. are any closer than they
were six months ago. Receipts amounted to
only £OO2O, and of that £2409 came from
calls. The 931-joz. won from 530
fathoms was dug from the run of
wash west *of Nos. 1 and 2 rises
into which panellers were put early this year.
Of .course, dirt of that grad® will soon clean
up debts; but east of the main crosscut the
ground is patchy and mostly on the poor side.
There has been a bit of a hustle to get work
done to the west by pushing out the inter-
mediate from No. 2 rise, but the gutter has
split or there is a blimp in the reef from
150 ft. to 357 ft. Beyond that point is 60ft. of
wash which isn't worth taking out. though
it is said to be “strong, well water worn and
of. good appearance.” In all the western
drives the wash has gone underfoot, so a
branch reef drive is being rushed ahead to
get under the western ground. There a rise
will soon tell the flavor of the dirt. The
cash sunk by the co. so far amounts to
£80,843, without a cent of return to share-
holders. Bold won has brought only £40,451,
leaving £35,632 to be forked out by investors
for calls. This is the way all alluvial mining
goes in the Cabbage Garden these days.
“Dodger”: The idea of George Bryant and
Jimmy Maekay to shut down on extraction
work at the Langi Logan (Vic.) isn’t shap-
ing well. Judd and Little, of the A.M.A.,
saw that it meant scrapping a lot of alluvial
workers, and fired a writ into Maekay invit-
ing him to attend the Federal Arbitration
Court. Little’s stiff job is to prove that the
co. has been responsible for a lock-out. Both
New and South Langi have been sinks for
cash, and a number of the wise-heads unload
shares every time prices spurt. Should the
Court agree with Little, what is to happen to
the no-liability cos. that are on the call
list and probably have plant in pawn? Any-
how, every mining tyro knows that the only
chance the Langi shows have is to drain the
gutter and work on cheap lines with a big
staff. If Little could produce 150 efficients
he would earn a vote of thanks.
Cardross copper-field (Q.), where the
Mammoth Co. unfolded its wings just before
the war, is very quiet now. The pumps were
withdrawn from the co.’s main mine, the
Chieftain, before Christmas, and a few men
put on to work the small lensical Caledonia.
Bunches of nice ore are obtained, but if
this co. intends to become a producer it
must concentrate on the Chieftain. Except
for a 48 hours’ smelt, the blast furnace has
not been worked since the war started, and
the bit of good grade ore gouged out of the
oxidised zone of the Chieftain was sold to
southern smelters. To allow water to rise
in a mine and have to pump it out every
time operations are restarted makes a big
hole in profits.
The old Vulcan, Irvinebank (Q.), reckons
it is on the scent of something good, a bit of
nice stone being located in the vicinity of
the 500 ft. level. Otherwise the outlook is
very sick. Deep prospecting yielded blanks
mostly, so operations have been transferred
to the upper levels.
Mt. Petersen, at Irvinebank (Q.), is being
cleaned out by old John Moffat’s co., which is
going to give the big lode a trial. Its
backers reckon it will go It p.c., and with
tin at its present high price that will pay.
An aerial ropeway has been installed to pro-
vide cheap transport to the mill. Irvinebank
needs nourishment of some sort. It is very
weak.
* * * *
Stannary Hills (Q.) comes to light again
as a result of the high price of tin. Its mill
at Rocky Bluffs is being put into order for
crushing, and once more the co. "will be on
the list of producers. Development work on
the Arbouiu mine has revealed big bodies
worth about 4 p.c.—enough, it is reckoned,
to keep the mill going for three years.
Arsenic mines are becoming the fashion in
the Chillagoe district (Q.). One syndicate
is pegging out wholesale. The other day
there was a flutter up Koorboora wav, and
some shows were taken up which showed
nearly 50 p.c. on assay. The blows are near
the railway and could be easily handled
But nobody comes to light with a plant for
treatment purposes. Yet the State Mines
Department is working ore much poorer as
regards arsenical contents and reckons to
make big profits out of it.
* * * *
From D. Mackey, late manager:—
Wild Oat par. (B. 28/2/’18), commenting on New
tommy Burns (N.Q.), says “the No. 3 tunnel was
continued 125 ft., but it only proved that the tin
ore sunk on in the deep winze did not live to
depth/’ This tunnel (555 ft. in length) was driven
from the eastern side of the hill and would have to
go another 4Oft. to be plumb under the winze, which,
by the way, was not in ore of any importance for
the last 40 or 50 feet. The undoubted trend of the
Burns main ore-body was westward, and the tunnel
would require to go at least a further 200 or 300 feet
to prove anything much about it. Present pro-
gramme is to connect winze with tunnel for pass and
ventilation and try for the continuation of the ore-
body nearer to where it was lost.
As at December 31 the co. owed £07!) to
sundry creditors and £558 on account of
Government subsidy; on the other side,
apart from mine, plant and a trifle of stores,
it had under £—-)—not much more than was
Paid in diva, per share on the old issue.
Three or four years ago about £3OOO worth
of insured gold was stolen by masked men
from under the nose of the Bullfinch man-
agement. The insurance co. declined to pay
out, and the Board went to Jaw. The case
was heard in London, where the co. lately
won a verdict for £2780 and costs.
After paying £140.000 in dividends. Golden
Ridge (W.A.) has decided to give up. The
Board has been authorised to dispose of the
assets, and voluntary liquidation is to fol-
low.
Tyrconnell North (W.A.) has taken op-
tions on several has-beens at the north end
of the Kalgoorlie field, and is now consider-
ing what it will do with them. Included in
the number is the Hidden Secret, which once
had a rich shoot of telluride; the Fair Play,
from which several small rich crushings were
taken years ago; and the North End, which
once enriched a couple of prospectors but
afterwards ruined a eo. The Tyrconnell
Crowd is also busily developing a formation
exposed some time ago in a cutting on the
East-West line, near Kalgoorlie, and has
already begun to blow about prospects.
With both eyes on the market Princess
Royal has installed a plant on its* Happy
Jack option at Comet Vale (W.A.) and
Adelaide is being advised to get in early.
But it should look before it leaps. It wouid
he interesting to know why the last half-
yearly report contains no report from the
manager, and also whether the option was
exercised against the advice of the local
director, Herbert Moss, who controls the
adjacent Sand Queen mine and should know
something about the field.
* * * *
Great Boulder’s ore reserves are now esti-
mated at 387.511 tons containing 270.731
fine ounces. This is a big drop compared with
the previous year’s figures— s6o.647 tons
containing 416.2650 Z. To date the co. has
paid over 51 millions sterling in dividends,
and will probably distribute another million
before the blind is pulled down.
The cash position of the Mararoa ( W.A.)
is stronger than its ore reserves. Below No.
7 level things look pretty sick, though there
are blocks of stone north and south at No. 8
to be. beaten out. This has to he sweetened
np with what quartz can be got in the far
north workings of Nos. 5 and 6 levels and
the No. 7 intermediate. Here manager Hen-
derson has prodded round till he has satisfied
all and sundry that the shoot of stone
lives only a few feet above No. 6 and below
No. 7. level. So he has only about 60ft. of
material to stope. which means hard graft to
keep going. Under the circs, he won’t
chance estimating the reserves. Still it looks
as if he has Is. 6d. available, and as there is
2s. cash in hand apart from the plant the
risk of holding shares need not fluster any-
one.
* * * *
The Tasmanian Government proposes to
snend about half a million in damming th.'
King River, and developing power. Mount
By ell has guaranteed to become a big cus-
tomer. But before undertaking the work,
the Government is, with unusual caution,
spending a few hundreds on deviating the
stream to'discover whether the bed is too
permeable to admit of the project being suc-
cessful. Generally this sort of question isn't
asked until ail the money has been spent.
* * * *
If Mount Stewart (Tas.), which the
Mount Jasper Co. has visions of floating off,
doesn t do any better for the new people
than it. has done for the old, nobody will get
tiit Picked samples have given from 40oz. to
•).)oz. silver, trace to 6gr. gold, 9 to 22 p.c.
lead and 10 to 17 p.c. zinc. But in the last
working, the south drive which was extended
150 ft. from the shaft at 200 ft., the values
taken every sft. were 240 z. silver, trace to
ogr. gold, 1 to 7 p.c. lead and 5 to 11 p.c.
zinc. The lode was 43in. wide in one place;
but what the new people ought to make sure
about is whether width doesn’t mean lean-
ness. There are some very fine streaks of
galena : and it will be important to ascer-
tain what the silver values are outside the
streaks.

the price of a solitary Diamond in
our enormous stock, notwithstanding
that Diamonds have risen fully 33

When war broke out all jewels
slumped, and we bought enormously,
and so have two years’ normal supply.
We could, of course, increase our
prices, and profit by war conditions,
but we prefer to increase our cus-
tomers rather than profiteer. It will
pay us better eventually.
For the higher grades of Diamonds
our prices are far below those of
London.
