A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, March 25, 1918.
My Dear Akjni,—
The Fullerton Memorial Hall in Crown-
street housed the crowd which heard the
annual report of the Women’s Hospital over
the road. Bunting fluttered from pillar to
post, and the platform was packed with hos-
pital officials, Judge Backhouse in the midst
of them as chairman. Governor Davidson
talked a little, while his lady nursed the
committee’s presentation posy. Dr. Mac-
kellar warmed up to his favorite theme, the
saving of child life, and plucked statis-
tics from a sheaf of papers. David Storey
and others took up the cudgels for hospitals,
David pausing to throw a pebble at the
Goliaths who accept the baby bonus. Nellie
Leach, who organised the big matinee for. the
Crown-street haven, got a word of thanks.
She crept under the shade of her hat. Her
neighbor, Mrs. Kelly, who had only a toque
to hide her blushes, had a similar compli-
ment paid her.
The carnival to help the Alexandra Hos-
pital for Children was quite off the track.
For this the committee has to thank the
theatricals who provided th& meat of the
show. Enormous kewpies, with humans
perspiring inside, strutted across the sunlit
Sydney Cricket Ground. “The Bing Boys”
played cricket with fancy teams from the
National and Tivoli. A huge crowd, with (I
guessed this) millions of children, filled the
grandstand and piled up against the fences
round the oval. Organiser Clarence Moss,
secretary of the Hospital, can shake hands
with himself.
The concert arranged by the Town Hall
staff to help the Mayor’s Patriotic Fund
didn’t get enough publicity. Good works
alone won’t draw the multitude, and the
attendance was not large. The sub-regal
party, which is going hard, came along, and
surrounded the leather-chair reserve in
good order. Captain Glossop and his bride
were in this selection. The usually dismal
organ platform was a bower of tall palms
from which, to start off with, the excellent
Metropolitan Band gave a fine performance.
Charles Smythe, Carrie Lanceley (in rose
pink and white lace), John Lemmone, A. E.
Benham, and a rich-voiced contralto, Lilian
Gibson, were in a programme that was as
long as this day week.
Nancy Chisholm, good Red Cross worker,
was married last week in All Saints’, Wool-
lahra. The hero was Ivan Young, of Nliill
(Vic.) and the Flying Corps. Youthful Mar-
garet and Val. Stephen walked sedately to
the altar behind Aunt Nancy. Sister Joan
“attended” the dainty bride, who was frocked
in silky-white georgette, with a Limerick lace
veil over all. Bishop Langley, aged 80-
something, came over the border to help at
the knot-tyinsr of his pet niece. The Sendalls
from Goulburn, and some of the Youngs of
Nhill, were at the party. The flying man
gave Nancy a badge of his corps, the wings
of diamonds and the letters “A.F.C.” in
enamel across the body. Other presents
included a carved oak-tray and a basket
woven with the ’groom’s colors from the Red
Cross spinning depot.
Next to Dr. Morrison, W. 11. Donald, a
one-time Victorian pressman of charming
personality, is the best-known Australian in
China and one of the most influential white
men in the big straggly republic. He edits a
paper and a very authoritative magazine, and
is correspondent for several big English and
American journals. “Donald,” says a recently
returned wanderer, “is still young and fresh-
looking, considering his years in the land
where the pace is usually swift. Not
always, of course, and the Victorian says he
can’t remember ever having tasted alcohol.”
Died at Krawaree a few weeks ago, Mrs.
B. Mackey, aged 85 —33 years, almost to a
day, after the death of her husband. Born
in Ireland, Bridget Nicholson came to Syd-
ney in ’54, and spent 10 years here as pri-
vate teacher. Married in ’64 to the late
Daniel Mackey, she went with him to a selec-
tion at Krawaree, 32 miles from Braid-
wood. The struggle in that bushland was
enlivened by visits from bushrangers led by
the Clarks; but they took nothing, perhaps
because there was so little to take. The
selector-pedagogue—Dan studied for his
schoolmaster’s job by the light of a bramble
fire, with a blanket wrapped round in the
bitter winter nights of those highlands—was
only bailed up once. That was when he was
travelling to Araluen. The bushrangers
emerged suddenly from their hiding-place,
but only to ask news of the world they had
left behind. The only eligible in the Mackey
family is a grandson who is for the present
helping at the Records Office in London.
Our girls are capturing an occasional
Sammy. In December at Norfolk, Virginia,
Lieut. Elisha Converse. National Naval
Volunteers < Boston), laid his worldly goods
at the feet of Lilian, a daughter of the late
W. Buckland Taylor, of Queensland.
Mrs. Moyes. wife of Major Moyes—she
was Nina Edmond, eldest daughter of James,
The Bulletin’s old chief, a very much less-
sick man in latter days, by the way—has
reached London and her wounded husband
safely. The Australian was twice damaged
in Flanders, the second wound involving the
loss of a foot. Meanwhile the Edmond
family, after getting progress cables of the
daughter’s voyage as far as St. John’s, had
an anguished bout of silence. Finally news
was received, through the Government ser-
vice, of the wanderer’s arrival. This took
only two days on the journey. On March
18 came a message which had started on
February 1 from London. It was a very
lame cable on landing, by reason of 45 days’
hard travelling. As it hadn’t come by the
Government route, one can’t throw even half
a brick at Mr. Webster this time.
,Though he’d had many months of indif-
ferent health, the death last week of I)r.
George Lane Mullins, for many years a
colonel in the Army Medical Service, and
formerly of the Garrison Hospital, was unex-
pected. There’s warm and wide sympathy
for the devoted wife, who lias nursed a sick

tains.
There has been a buzz at the Royal Naval
House to farewell Miss Henderson, who has
been the pivot of the Sailors’ Wives Friendly
Union. Excellency Helen addressed the
company, and on its behalf handled the
heroine a silk umbrella, travelling clock, suit-
case and a sheaf of good wishes. The clock
will point the hour on the coming pilgrim-
age the lady takes as travelling sec. of
the Australian Board of Missions. “But I
daresay,” said her Ex., “Miss Henderson
will occasionally forget the heathen to come
and have a look at us.” Frantic applause
frqm the little things our sailors left behind
them. Mrs. Dunn and her helpers saw that
the tea-urn hissed at 4 o’clock.
Chaw. H. Bertie,
City Librarian, is
really the storied
glad dog with the two
tails. Both appen-
dages are so new
that he waves them
joyously before the
eyes of all comers.
One is the just-
opened Municipal
Library for Adults
in Queen Victoria
Building—it sheds
the “Markets” forth-
with. The other is
an airy adjacent
Children’s Library
—a long - deferred
Bertie dream come
true. The kiddies
heard about it, of course —their very own —
and surged round the doors while Mayor and
aldermen were puffing through the heavy-
weight function of introducing the adults
to their bookbindings. In the youngsters’
den the shelves are not quite full. “On the
water” is the ship that brings the full-and-
plenty to feed the juvenile mind. Two lady
librarians, specially chosen for the job, are
the custodians of this Mecca of youth.
“For every book in the Municipal
Library,” says the librarian, “there is a
reader. The same thing applies to the
youngsters. Some, of course, are athirst
for fiction —plentifully supplied—but many
of them ask for solid informative literature.
They come from both State and private
schools, eager to dip into the new treasure
trove.” The tall man with the slight stoop
—he has the bookworm shoulders—and the
true librarian’s love of his hoard, sits atop
of all the readers when he climbs into his
sanctum. This commands an unrivalled
view of city chimney-pots, with a distant
glitter when the western sun shines on the
shores of Watsoh’s Bay. “I was really born
in 1910,” there muses Chas. Bertie, “when
Syd Smith and I became the prophets of a
neglected god called ‘Old Sydney.’ The edition
quickly sold out. We tried the phblic again
with ‘Stories of Old Sydney.’ Since then
I have edited an Encyclopaedia of Australian
History. This is in pickle, waiting for the
end of the war. Edited ‘Aboriginalities’
from The Bulletin —and a discerning pub-
lic bought them. So with a ‘Story of Vau-
cluse House,’ which I put together last year.
Sold out.” City Librarian since 1909. Pre-
sident of Australian Historical Society,
1914. Went to Europe in 1913, making a
pilgrimage in search of remains of Governoi
Macquarie at Mull. Found a large hotel on
the island. Wondered how it paid. G°t tne
bill next day. Left Off wondering. Judith
Fletcher is responsible for the photo, of the
man himself.
The smile on this face rarely comes off.
Being merely general manager of the big
Broken Hill I’rop.,
which has built the
great iron and steel
works at Newcastle
and wanted to build
ships as soon as it
saw that Australia
needed them, he
hasn’t, of course,
any worries. G. D.
Delprat was born in
Holland ; but he left
early, and was in
Scottish engineering
works and then at
mines in Spain and
America before he
came to Australia
nearly 20 years ago.
I suppose he is
worried with about
the biggest salary paid in Australia;
but be wasn’t content to let his girls grow
up to be butterflies. Every one of them has
a profession—mostly medicine or music.
Carmen, the violinist, who “finished off” in
Europe, is one; another met Mawson, the
Arctic explorer, at Adelaide University and
married him later on ; a third, a doctor, is
just back from America. If Mr. Delprat
were asked what Australian women lack, lie
would first tell you suavely that they don’t
lack anything : if you pressed him he might
allow that higher education wouldn’t hurt
the °irls. For a little light recreation he
would as soon be set a problem in mathema-
tics as anything. They made him a some-
thing or other in the new Order of the
British Empire the other day. No, not a
knight. He is still plain Guillaume.
Governor Davidson declared the liorty
Society’s Autumn blooms well and truly
open, amidst an almost stifling incense from

crop—waved over the proceedings; and
Maiden, of the Botanic Gardens, guided his
Ex. round St. James’s Hall.

John Madden was connected with the great
Sydney legal family of Stephen. This way.
The original John Stephen arrived in Sydney
in 1824 as Solicitor-General. He became a
Judge ; his son Alfred became Chief Justice,
and. his grandson, Mathew Henry (still
living), Judge and acting Chief Justice.
John I. had five sons and three daughters.
The eldest son, Sydney Stephen, became a
Judge in Maoriland ; his son, Francis John
Stephen, was a well-known Melbourne
solicitor, and one of his daughters John
Madden married.
One of the dailies the other day printed a
photo, of 85-year-old Captain William
Moseley, who at the moment was busy knit-
ting socks for soldiers. Moseley is an old
Army officer, Captain in the 60th Rifles,
with which he served in India. Getting out
of uniform he tried squatting in N. S. Wales
with a high percentage of poor luck. Having
married the daughter of a well-known Syd-
ney solicitor, Captain Moseley settled down
to study law, and in 1888, at the age of 55,
he was admitted to practice. For some
years thereafter the firm was Billyard,
Moseley and Andrews.
Another decorated young warrior stepped
ashore unannounced the other day. He is
Captain Stanley Calderwood, M.C., of an
infantrv battalion that has had a record of
tough work since Gallipoli. Well known in
the Galston district, lie was surveying when
the call came; and before embarking he
got special leave to sign up with May Tay-
lor, of a well-known Bathurst family. His
cantaincy and decoration were won in a hot
corner at Paschendaele. Sickness soon after-
wards added his name to the casualty lists.
Word comes in that Captain Brian Laid-
ley Dowling, of the Flying Cori.s, son of
solicitor Arthur Dowling, is engaged to an
English heiress. Mr. Dowling’s three sons
went to the Front. One was killed in
France; the other two are still plugging
away.

It is not many years ago since ads. de-
signed in Australia were so bad that they
were used as examples of what not to do by
an advertising expert in the TJ.S.A. llow
far we have moved during the last decade
may be seen at the Commercial Artists’
show opened this week in the rooms of the
Ad. Men’s Club in Culwulla Chambers. The
exhibits comprise original drawings and
prints of newspaper ads., book illustrations,
catalogues, etc., from the studios of Walter
Jardine, Smith and Julius, W. Lassie, The
States Studio, Tom Ferry, Virgil Reilly and
others. The artistic standard of these draw-
ings is high, for most of them are the work
of men who have made good in what is for
the sake of distinction called fine art.
Surgeon-Major Donald, recently home from
active service, puts a circlet on the finger of
Dora Christoe this month. She’s a Hunter’s
Hill girl.
Some time last year (that ought to be
indefinite enough) a boat that passed through
the Panama Canal took a consignment of
Billjims from Australia, and this is how
Driver Jack Coffee, of Chatswood, describes
that part of the journey:—
As we pulled in the sides of the lock were lined
with American soldiers and canal officers and their
wives and daughters. They looked good to us: the
soldiers would do credit to any nation —trim and
neat in uniforms that fitted like gloves. There was
much cheering and exchanging of pieces of money,
badges and buttons.
At Trinidad, where “the people fed us with
cigarettes and fruits and expected to see us
black,” young Coffee remembered that Mr.
Baker, formerly Consul in Australia, was
located there; and that meant a re-union and
more pleasantness.

Sydney’s remarkable young accompanist
Aimde Puech is going to be married later on.
It’s a recent engagement. For the last few
years this young French-Australian pianist
has been a constant “at the piano” item.
She rarely opens a music score—-even when
the melodious voice of the Belgian Goossens
delights an audience, and Aimee Puech sup-
ports him in a repertoire that runs through
grand opera to the latest ballads.

T \ THE SUPREME COURT OF NEW ZEALAND.
J- WELLINGTON DISTRICT.
IN DIVORCE, No. 1548.
BETWEEN ALBERT GOODMAN. Petitioner, and
MARTHA GOODMAN, Respondent.
TO MARTHA GOODMAN formerly of Lower Hutt
in New Zealand and Sydney in. New South IVales
but now of an address unknown wife of Albert
Goodman of Trentham in New Zealand Horse Trainer.
WHEREAS a marriage was solemnised between
the parties hereto: AND WHEREAS a petition for
the dissolution thereof has been filed in the Supreme
Court of New Zealand at Wellington in New Zealand
wherein the petitioner alleges that you have been
guilty of desertion : AND WHEREAS it was ordered
that service of the Citation extracted with a sealed
copy of the petition herein be effected by publish-
ing an advertisement once in the Sydney Bulletin
containing an abstract of the said Citation and
petition. NOW THEREFORE TAKE NOTICE that
unless within thirty-five days from the appearance
of the said advertisement you do attend at the Regis-
trar’s Office Supreme Court Wellington aforesaid
and file an answer thereto the cause will be dealt
with in vour absence. A copy of the said Citation
and petition will be supplied to you <on your apply-
ing for the same at the office of this Court at Wel-
lington aforesaid. - '
Dated at Wellington this eighth day of March,
1918.
(L.s.) A. H. CUTLER,
Deputy Registrar.
Humphrey Francis O’Leary is Solicitor for the
Petitioner whose address for service is at the office
of the said Humphrey Francis O’Leary, 200 Lamb-
ton Quay, Wellington. Any person who may know
the address of the respondent is requested to send
this notice to her.
PURSUANT to an Order of the Court of Chancery
of the County Palatine of Lancaster dated the
27tli day of November 1917 made in an action by
the Public Trustee as the Trustee of the Will of
ELIZABETH FALKNER BLAIR deceased late of 33
Hoghton Street Southport in the County of Lancaster
Spinster who died on the Ist day of June 1880 as
Plaintiff against FRANCIS WILLIAM GREEN and
other as Defendants whereby enquiry was directed
“Whether Joseph Stephen Blair ’Frank Reginald
Blair and Hugh Gordon Blair the three nephews of
the said Elizabeth Falkner Blair deceased named in
her said Will are respectively living or dead and
if any of them be dead when they respectively died
and whether they respectively left any and what
children and if so when such children were respec-
tively born and whether they respectively attained
the age of 21 years or being daughters married
under that age and'whether such of them as attained
the age of 21 years or being daughters married
under that age are living or dead and if any of
them be dead when they respectively died and who
are their respective legal personal representatives”
the persons claiming to be any of the persons re-
ferred to in the said enquiry are by their Solicitors on
or before the 27th day of May 1918 to come in
and prove their claims at the Chambers of the Regis-
trar of the Manchester District of the said Court
situate at Duchy Chambers Clarence Street Man-
chester England or in default thereof they will be
peremptorily excluded from the benefit of the said
Order. Tuesday the 18th day of June 1918 at 11
o’clock in the forenoon at the said Chambers is
appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
Dated the 7th day of January 1918.
HUBERT WINSTANLEY
Registrar.

Melbourne, March 25, 1918.
My Dear Y aerie, —
The garden party at Government House
was charming. Autumn weather, a lovely
garden, clumps of very companionable people
and a good band—what more could you want?
Lady Stanley was 'tailored in tan, wore a
big black velvet liat tied under her chin, and
carried one of those huge decorated bags.
His Ex. wore a comfy grey lounge suit. (The
invitations curtly requested “sac suits.")
The decorative W. .T. T. Clarkes were there
—ltoma in black velvet. Dame Miller came
along with her newly-knighted Edward and
wore royal blue satin. Mrs. Reading and
Mrs. Macdonald from Sydney arrived with
the Norton Grimwades. The solemn (laud
Mayoral pair attended, Mrs. Stapley in her
black tailored silk.
Melbourne is over-run with delegates to
the fifteenth annual conference of the
Chambers of Commerce, hast week, before
the Melbourne taverns had cleared their
record batch of race visitors, some of the
delegates sat on the steps of Menzies’ and
refused to budge until a corner was found,
for them. The delegates have been meeting
and discussing ponderously in intervals of
protracted feeding. Dinners, lunches, sup-
pers and parties have been flung round to
them and their wives. The Stanleys gave a
garden party; there was a big soiree at the
Masonic and a crowd motored the lovely
beach road to tea at Frankston.
The show at the Masonic was an old
fogeys’ affair which recalled a country meet-
ing at “the Mechanics” or in the woolshed.
Tables were set on the floor of the big hall,
while a concert was hurled at the banqueters’
heads from the platform where Di Gilio had
his band. Mayor Stapley was a serious
guest, and his Mayoress reserved her musical
ear for the platform noises. Norton
Grimwade was another serious figure; his
wife was fluffed about with purple and laven-
der. Tom Murdoch, of the Tas. Marine
Board, brought his vivacious little wife. She
wore a black and white gown and a vivid
Egyptian-looking head-dress. Mrs. Meares,
from Sydney, caught the eye. And big Hugh
Y. McKay was there receiving congratu-
lations on his selection as one of the three
business men who are to keep an eye or two
on the Defence Department.
John Madden’s widow is leaving the
Cliveden flat to settle for a time in the
Frankston humpy. ' Daughter Lesley, being
tied to town, will step into a boarding-house
in South Yarra.
General Sellheim and his wife gave a fare-
well lunch party at Menzies’ to General
Johnston before his departure to New Guinea
and his new job of Administrator. Colonel
Kenneth Mackay came along for a handshake;
and Mrs. Johnston was there* in cool grey
toggery. She’s to be left behind,
Mrs. Willie Sharp is spending a quiet
time at Medlow trying to recover from the
shock of her husband’s death. He was head-
partner in the big timber firm and died sud-
denly from some heart trouble just after
finishing a game of golf.
Mrs. Alex. Gillespie, wife of the flour-
miller, has gone into hospital with a nervous
breakdown. Melbourne sassiety misses the
lady; she was an indefatigable hostess whose
“little dinners” were poems to the favored
Naicest.
Mrs. Masseran is back in Melbourne for a
brief visit. She has been in France and
enveloped in war work.
Mrs. Rogers Thomson gave a party to
introduce the Langham, the new swaggah
tea-and-bunnery run by the widow of Dr.
Kelly, of Ballarat. It’s all lavender and
lace, and Mrs. Thomson swamped her cor-
ner in roses.
Adela Pankhurst Walsh is a very subdued
young woman nowadays. With her able-
bodied seaman and his two daughters, who
adore their new stepma. she lives in a cot-
tage at Coburg, near the gaol, taken when
she was “doing time,” so that the family
might visit her as frequently and easily as
possible. Adela is an excellent cook, and, as
has frequently been noted with surprise, a
good-looker except when she has her mouth
wide 'open to shriek something foolish.
Domesticity seems to agree with her, for she
is a smiling, rosy-cheeked young wife.
Aide Conant has been holidaying in Tassy
with Captain Clifford. They fished at Lake
Leake.
The Desert Cold Lowrys of Maoriland
have fled from Melbourne to Sydney ; thence
they go home.
Mrs. George Higgins had a party at the
Quambv Club to meet an Adelaide visitor.
Mrs. Fisher, the guest of honor, runs the
Red Cross Record there. She is an old-
time Melbourne girl, a daughter of the late
Robert Sellar.
Vance Palmer having enlisted, his clever
wife Nettie (she used to be Nettie Higgins)
is going to give lessons and do coaching work
in French literature. Nettie is a ’Varsity
girl and was trained in France and Germany
for coaching work in languages. The lessons
will be arranged at her home in Sorrett-
avenue, Malvern, as the coach has two small
children to keep an eye upon. A phone
message will open up arrangements.
The Canadian Trade Commissioner has
quite a shopful of Red Indian goods left over
from Our Roys’ Day. He has them spread
out alluringly in his office —Indian baskets,
clothes, fur boots and beads, marked down to
cost price.
The Charlie Taits are due back this week
from America. Mrs. Charlie found the
weather conveniently freezing at the finish
of her visit and hubby came to the rescue
with some Alaskan furs. Brother Ted re-
turned a boat sooner, and is, established at
his Sydney post.
The whole Riverina Falkiner clan, off to
Sydney for the race season, are anticipating
a breathlessly gay time. The Easter frivols
will rage just as if the Hun were not taking
toll of the finest Australian manhood. But
we are sending across quite a consignment
of the other sort.
Cousin Adelaide drops in :
Red Cross workers are well to the fore in the
awards of the Order of the British Empire in this
State. Kathleen lvyffin Thomas, who was for some
time assistant-secretary and is now joint sec. with
Major Connor, is an officer,- while Mrs. George Dean
is a member. Other members are W. J. Isbister, for
a time hon. Commissioner in Egypt, and H. C. Cave,
who toiled as R.C.C. on Salisbury, Plains.
The cinematograph plant that the hotelkeepers
have presented to tire V.A.D.’s at the Outer Harbor
had its first show on Mohday night. The pictures
unrolled themselves to music from a piano for which
the necessary cash had been raised by the Rev.
Frank Lade, one of our keenest prohibitionists. It
makes good shandy-gaff.
To the ranks of Adelaide verse-writers add Maud
R. Liston, who has just published a collection under
the title of “St. George for England.” Sothe of
the' verses have already appeared in the dailies.
A. E. Nott, general manager of the S. Australian
Brewing Co., who has just relinquished his job after
28 years’ service, was farewelled and given a pre-
sentation by the staff the other* daj'. He isn’t much
more than middle-aged yet; but he is well-to-do arid
so is his wife, and he wants more leisure to enjoy
his rheumatism.
The late Lieut. R. Q. Scott left £20,000 to his
widow and small son. He was vine-growing here
when war broke out and was killed in action in
France about a year ago.
Major Cavanagh-Mainwaring, who has been with
the A.I.F. ever since August, 1914, has cbme back
to South Aus. on duty. He left here as
Captain of the Field Ambulance of the 3rd Light
Horse, and since then has done hospital work on
Gallipoli and in Egypt and Macedonia. The Serbian
White Eagle is the Order that has come to him.
Ruthven li. Forbes, who represents the Suzuki
Co., has been entertaining Shigetaro Kawasaki, chief
geologist to the Japanese Government in Korea. The
little brown men, as Dr. Morrison told us lately, are
developing Korea with remarkable success; and the
geologist is come to pick up wrinkles from our
great smelting-works at Port Pirie and Wallaroo.
Beatrice Henty, one of the few Melbourne
war-workers to receive a Commandership in
the Order of the British Empire, is grand-
daughter of the pioneering James Ilenty, of
Portland, and daughter of the late Henry,
of pious memory. Early in the war. when
she started as superintendent of the All-tor-
Sokliers’ League, energetic Beatrice took "No
Nonsense Here” as her unwritten motto. Get-
ting results has been her one aim, with the
consequence that the social-butterfly type
of war-fiddler soon found herself fading right
out of the picture. Some of em made spite-
ful remarks as they went, but the willing
majority kept the comforts-supplymg
machine grinding steadily ahead, with both
feet on the pedals and not a hand idle. Ihe
figures to date tell a magnificent tale of per-
sistent toil.
Hilda Bull that was, who became Mrs.
Louis Esson, and is now with him in
Blighty, is working for the War Office on a
travelling Medical Board. Her job is to
examine girl recruits for various depart-
ments of army service, and slie is making an
interesting tour of it.
Captain Ronald Cumming. who went to
the Front as orderly to General McNicolt
on that scarred soldier’s return to France,
has been handed the Croix de Guerre. Last
October, after Passchendaele, Cumming went
through much the same life-and-deatli strug-
gle with wounds as did his General on Gal-
lipoli. Now he is booked to go on H.Q.
staff of the 10th. after a term of easy work
in France.
Geoffrey Shillinglaw, who gave up his man-
ager’s job with the National Bank in Mel-
bourne to plunge into war work, has had
some chastening experiences while working
for the Anzac Buffet in London. The man
who used to sit in his office and condescend
to see a client about an overdraft finds him-
self bossed by women workers. In the midst
of intricate figure-work an agitated dame
rushes in to mention that milk-o charged for
half a pint too much that morning, and it’s
Shillinglaw’s job to see that such a catas-
trophe doesn’t occur again.
One of the three airmen who came home
last week was young Cecil James, a son of
Major G. 11. James, cadet trainer and rifle
coach, and brisk little Britomarte James,
presswoman and war-worker, now in
Blighty. Cecil was flying at 16,000* feet
when a Boche shell burst near him, and
though lie brought his ’plane down safely
shell-shock forced him to quit the unpleasant
land of France.
Back to Australia, “Big” Bailli'eu’s
nephew, Flight-Lieutenant Reggie, M.C., an
old Melbourne Grammar boy, whose stunt
practically duplicated that for which F. H.
Macmaniara, another Victorian airman, won
the V.C. With his observer, Lieutenant It.
M. Smith, M.C., Reggie lobbed on the
Egyptian sands to rescue a pilot who had
been forced to land and had fired his machine.
Smith stood-off Abdul with his revolver until
Baillieu was ready, and then all three left
in Bailliou’s ’plane, which, being heavily
laden, could not climb above 500 feet, and
was consequently under heavy fire. But it
took them 60 miles to safety.
Sergeant-major Flora Sandes, of the Ser-
bian Army, has been heard of in London.
She wears her man’s uniform as befits a
woman who has campaigned with one of the
bravest little armies in Europe. She was
wounded at Monastir and decorated.
Expected back from Blighty this month.
Lieut.-Colonel J. W. Springthorpe. who for
a year has been in charge of shell-shock Cases
in a hospital of 1200 beds, llis experience
should be of immense value here. He has
visited the leading hospitals for the treat-
ment of such cases in England and I* ranee,
especially those institutions near the Front.
He has also given some study to the pension
question. When he returned from Egypt
earlier in the war he took the opportunity to
get married, but it was not long before he
rejoined the A.M.C. and sailed in anew to
help battered Billjim.
Genthelp Glanville, of the Newdigate staff,
fell in love with the very young niece of the
Governor. Miss Winifred Newdigate re-
sponded with all the fervor of romantic li,
so her vice-regal uncle packed them off to
England'—on different boats —so that they
may ask the consent of the head of Wini-
fred’s house to an engagement. (The Head
of the House is her brother, aged about -M.)
Winifred is a rich young person in her own
right. She is even richer in proportion than
the Governor Newdigates, who keep the
wolf from the door by the careful manipula-
tion of about £40.000 a year. One Booth
is on his way out to fill the genthelp gap
left by Glanville. In the meantime all the
social jobbing is being done by Captain
Fitzgerald (he has just become engaged to
Pauline Curran, daughter of Curran ot
Tatt.’s). and Browne, the permanent local
aide of great distinction.
The Toorak Moore widow had hardly fin-
ished unpacking in the Rutter Clarkes
Dutch-gardened castle when daughter Nell
gave notice that she and Larry Heath, or
Gaunt’s, had agreed to be made one in the
immediate future.
From “Rosna,” in London —weddings, of
course
Roma May, a little Australian who has done some
music-hall work in this isle and for three years has
worked like a clock entertaining Tommy Anzac and
Pte. Billjim, was married to a Billjim of her own
on January 16. He is Lieut. J. W. Stabback, M. 0.,
and a brigade or two of Anzac Buffet folk, headed
by chairman John McCall, went to wish them luck.
Lorna, daughter of Mrs. Purves-Smith, of St. Hilda,
is now O.C. Cant. McAlister Moffat-Pender. The
new husband is a Scotch international footballer and
a relative of the Moffats of the Western District of
Victoria. The bride and her mother have been
mighty war-workers, first in Paris and latelj in
London. One uncle gave her away and another tied
her up.
The best confetti is to flutter on April 10,,
when the medical McAdams’ chick Beryl
pairs off with the George Kelly widow s
younger son at Melbourne Grammar ( hapel.
At the same hour violinist Carmen Delprat

Ada Armytage. of the Como family, is
rousing Beaconsfield and Berwick to a big
Easter effort for Our Boys. There are special
prizes for the most useful article made by a
man and the prettiest article made by a
woman out of waste material. And a Coun-
try Fair, with prizes for the biggest pumpkin
and other weird exhibits.
There is more glad news about the
Equestrian Club girls’ approaching side-show
at Bendigo. The Easter celebration up north
is a “Back to Bendigo” affair, hence bed-’n’-
board is very precious ; so the local Benevo-
lent Asylum has come forward and offered
its extensive stabling for the use of those
equestrians who can’t make other arrange-
ments, aud has gallantly volunteered the use
of a number of spare beds for removal to
the horse-lines. The offer speaks well for
Bendigo’s prosperity. The trouble in most
cities where they have “benevolent” asylums
is that there isn’t enough bed to go round
among the weary foot-travellers.
To aid the French Red Cross and Societe
Maternelle, Mrs. The.vre Weigall is arrang-
ing a series of “Talks at the Piano” —not
trivial society-entertainer stuff, but good
highbrow music, as the Weigall lady is a
musician of some standing. “Aida,”
“Otello,” “Louise” and other grand operas
are down for dissection. .Jack Sanderson
has lent his Toorak home for tin* outburst,
and the Naicest are getting their bibs and
tuckers ready.
Esther Mitchell, now playing a lead in
“Turn to the llight,” made a lot of money
on Our Boys’ Day by driving round in an
ordinary cart (I think it was a milk-o’s)
and collecting from the crowd. Sin* was a
very fetching cadger. Esther is a Sydney
girl who has had only live years of stage
work. She appeared here before in “Mr.
Wu,” but was only one of the fake Chinese
maidens who got badgered because they did
not wear enough make-up.
Elsie Mackay, daughter of Sam, is playing
lead at a Chicago theatre in “Gipsy Trail,”
described as “a 1917 comedy-romance.”
Chicago papers call Elsie “that dazzling
Australian charmer.” Tree introduced her to
the American boards and she is, by all
accounts, a brilliant success.
It’s a sign of the homeless times that John
Madden. Lieut.-Governor and Chief Justice,
was buried from a glorified apartment house
—the big brick barracks that baronet S’Wil-
liam Gjiarke built for himself and his descend-
ants just beyond Fitzroy Gardens. The
place had another such a day of gloomy
splendor years ago, when young Clive
Clarke’s coffin was borne up the street to
Trinity Church by eight sweating gunners of
the gorgeous Kupertswood Battery, origi-
nated and maintained by the first baronet.
Tours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

STRENGTH AND ENERGY CREATED
BY PHOSPHATE.
NO TASK TOO HARD; NO STRAIN
TOO GREAT.
There is no longer any reason why the
nervous, hesitant man or woman should
envy those who are strong, resourceful and
calm, for, according to an eminent specialist,
such strength is within the easy reach of all
who desire it. Men and women are strong
mentally and physically in proportion to the
strength of their nerves, and nerves are
strong or weak according to the amount
of phosphate they absorb. Ordinarily
the nerves derive phosphate from di-
gested food, ( but owing to faulty metabo-
lism or to abnormal strain, the phosphate
thus taken up often proves insufficient, and
in consequence the nerves become weak and
exhausted. To remedy such a condition it is
necessary that the nerves should be provided
with more phosphate, and the use of the
concentrated bitro-phosphate, which most
chemists now stock in the form of five-grain
compressed tablets, is highly recommended.
Phosphate in this form is rapidly absorbed,
and a decided improvement in the general
health is soon noticeable. Nerves become
steady and strong, and the brain acts
quickly and clearly. Eyes become bright,
and the cheeks glow with healthy color. No
task seems too hard, no strain too great.
Abundant energy displaces lassitude, and
great strength reigns in the place of weak-
ness. — Advt.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Jimsey”: Here’s another notable case of
the homing instinct. My dad took delivery of 1.500
cattle from a station near the junction of the Mit-
chell and Palmer rivers (North Bananaland) and
steered them to a station in Gippsland. The second
night out on the home journey he lost his horse, and
although a day was spent in hunting for him, he
couldn’t be found. About two months after the old
man had reached home (a small station on the
Monaro) the wanderer turned up, quite well but
terribly thin. He was an iron-grey gelding, rising
seven, boasted of a grand old Australian pedigree
(Furious Riding out of the Bush), and was roped on
the Monaro as a two-year-old.
* # * *
“Barcoo” : When a shearer is working for
a tally the kicking sheep is his nark, and it re-
mained for “Grass-seed” Collins, a Bananaland
Dreadnought, to discover how to anchor the protest-
ing jumbuck. With a buckle strap, the fore and
hind feet, crossed fore and aft, just above the hoofs,
are tightly hitched together. On the “whipping”
side (the finishing side) the adjusting takes just one
second when the sheep has been removed from thq
“catching” pen to the shearing floor; the removal
of the hobble-strap takes less. Rams and crossbreds
are particularly bad kickers. With this device
they take their gruel as gently as a tame curate in
the chair of a manicurist.
VERY LAX.
SOAK: “Put it down on the slate, unit yer?”
BARMAN : “ ’Ere; tee don’t keep no slate ’ere.”
SOAK (getting it down hurriedly) : “Well, don’t blame me if yer forget all about it."
: I don’t know how long a snake
can stay under water, but I’ve kept one submerged
for a full 20 minutes by the watch. It was a
brown fellow and in full view the whole time.
Finally I raked him out and gave him the woodener
I’d been saving up for him. The winter-sleep of
snakes, by the way, is a delusion. Those I have
unearthed have had all their wits about them. I
think they hibernate simply because during winter
their natural food is unprocurable. They constantly
sun themselves outside their dormitories on bright
days in winter, and are quite ready for battle,
although not so savage as when the mating instinct
stirs in them in late spring or early summer. , I
know deadly snakes mate in a temperate climate as
early as October 10. On the Monaro (N.S.W.) high-
lands above the coast-range the honeymoon begins
later, and I have there, by the way, seen a black and
a brown snake engaged in serpentine coquetry. Are
there hybrids among snakes?
* * * #
“I. Say”: If “T.F.M.” (B. 21/2/TS) is
correct as to the date of settlement' at Mallacoota
(1842), it seems possible that Port Albert was as
early or .earlier. A party of eight left Melbourne
in the Singapore, 300 tons, in 1842, for Corner Inlet.
They proceeded to and named Port Albert, where
they built a two-roomed cottage. They were looking
for a harbor near Gippsland country, and for a good
stock route. Two boats from the wrecked Clonmel
were found on the .beach opposite Port Albert, and
one of them was used. The Albert and the Tara
rivulets were named (the latter after an abo., Charley
Tara) ; also the Latrobe River arid Lake Wellington.
The overlanding party struck civilisation at Western-
port, but failed to find a decent stock route.
■ :\ * * *
“Try Fluke”: It’s a rare sight these days,
the tattooed dial of a brown MIL. citizen. Of the
hundreds I used to know when I was a youngster, not
more than a dozen or so are still above the soil. That’s
males; there are still plenty of chin-decorated ladies
making lively the up-country townships, and now and
again blocking the traffic in Wellington or Auckland
while they discuss Desert Gold or some other famous
moke. There are a few practitioners of the moko
art still on the job, and the usual charge is 30s. for
the feminine lips and chin. Almost as rare as the
blue-mofco-ed face is the'tattooed nether part of Hone
Tikera. The old-timers had their anatomy under the
shawl-kilt adorned with vast skill and pains, so that
from hips to knee or thereabouts the warrior was a
work of art in blue scrolls, chevrons and diamonds,
after the manner of the black-wiofco-ed Samoan
warrior, only more so. I know one big chief (half-
caste) —he drives his swell motor-car in the King
Country,—who was accustomed to divert his friends
and astonish the new-comers by exhibiting a choice
piece of vertu in the form of a tattooed back, etc.
But he’s more reserved now that he’s got a big bank
account. He was about the last man tattooed by the
old tohungas. The daisy of them all, though, was
my venerable acquaintance Miti Kakau, of Hokianga.
Miti had the unexampled honor of displaying his
tattooed breech to royalty, no less. At the great
gathering of the clans at Rotorua to meet the present
King and Queen my unregenerate friend was sort of
premiere danseur in the Ngapuhis’ war-dance before
the royal stand. At a fortuitous moment he charged
to the front and with a right-about 1 whirl and a
flick-up of his kilt lie presented a bosker view to the
distinguished company—as a mark of high respect.
Missionaries and Cabinet Ministers and other shocked
dignitaries expostulated with the ancient athlete
when the show was over, but Miti’s rejoinder was to
the effect that if royalty hadn’t seen a well-tattooed
g'entleman before it was time it did, wherefore he
obliged on behalf of the tribe. If Mary of York saw
Miti and his art-gallery she, like a lady, never let on.

“K.R.”: The fact that the heart of the
gohanna or lace lizard will beat for hours after the
reptile has been killed is by no means unparalleled
in the reptile world. The heart of the fresh-water
turtle will jog on for at least 10 hours. I have
observed it still beating at that length of time after
demise, and but little diminished in vigor. The
stump-tailed lizard of W.A. has a record but little
inferior to that of ; brother turtle. It would seem,
from observation, that the reptilian heart-beat lasts
longer after death than that of any other vertebrate
animal. The common Australian frog keeps its clock
ticking for two or three hours after lights-out.

MICKIE MOLLYNOO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’re marchin’ out, we dunno where, to meet we
dunno who;
But here we lights eventual, ’n’ sighs ’n’ slips the
kit,
’N’, ’struth, the first to take us on is Mickie Mollynoo!
A copper of the Port he was, when ’istory was writ.
Sez I: “We’re sent to face the foe, ’n’ selp me, this
is It.”
A shine John Hop is Mollynoo. A mix-up with the
push
Is all his joy. One evenin’ when his baton’s flyin’
free
I takes a baby brick, ’n’ drives it hard agin the cush,
’N’ Privit Mick is scattered out fer all the world
to see,
But not afore indelible he’s put his mark on me.
I got the signs Masonic all inlaid along me lug
Where Molly, P.C., swiped me in them ’appy, care-
less days.
He’s sargin’ now, a vet’ran; I’m a newchum and a
mug,
’N’ when he sorter fixes me there’s somethin’ in
his gaze
That’s pensive like. “Move on!” sez he. “Keep
movin’ there! ” he says.
If after this I dreams of scraps promiscuous and
crool,
The mills in Butcher’s Alley when the watch is on
the wine,
Those nights he raided Wylie’s shed to break the two-
up School,
I takes a screw at Molly. With a grin that ain’t
divine
He’s toyin’ with a scar of old I reckernise as mine.
’N’ so I’m layin’ for it, ’n’ I’m wonderin how ’n’
what.
We’re signed on with the Germans ’n’ there ain’t
a vacant date;
But sure it’s coinin’ to me, ’n’ it’s cornin’ ’ard ’n’ ’ot.
Me lurk is patient waitin’, but I’m trimmin’ while
I wait
A brick to jab or swing with, in a willin’ tater-
tate.
Oh, judge me wonder! There’s a scrim that follers on
a raid.
I’m roughin’ it all-in with Hans. He socks me
such a bat
I slides on somethin’ narsty, hi’ me little grave is
made ;
But Molly butts my Hun, ’n’ leaves no face be-
neath his hat,
’N’, “ ’Scuse me, Mister Heir,” sez he, “I have a
lien on that!”
He helps me under cover, ’iv’ he ’ands me somethin’
wet
(I’ve got a lick or tw'o that leaves me feelin’ pretty
sick).
“Lor love yeh, ole John Hop,” sez I, “yiv buried me
in debt.”
“Don’t mintion ut at all,” he sez, ’n’ eyes me
arf-a-tick.
’N’ back there in the trench I sits, V trims
another brick.
EXCLUSIVE.
“Hullo, been in for your swim?”
“Ah — no. Some other Johnny was using the baths.”
’Tis all this now a month or more; then Mollynoo
sez he:
“Come aisy, Jumtn, yeh loafer, little hell ’n’ all
to view.
A job most illegant is on, cut out fer you ’n’ me.
The damnedest, dirtiest fighter on the Continent is
you,
Bar one, yeh gougin’ thafe, • ’n’ that is Sargin’
Mollynoo !”
I take, with knife ’n’ pistol, arf a brick to line me
shirt.
We creeps a thousan’ yards or so to jigger up a gun
Which seven Huns is workin’ on the Irish like a
squirt.
We gets across them, me ’n’ him. I pots the extra
one;
Mick chokes his third in comfort, ’n’, be’old, the
thing is done!
He stands above me, rakin’ sweat from off his gleam-
in’ nut.
“Me dipper’s leakin’, Mick,” sez I; “me leg is bit
in two.”
Sez he: “Bleed there in comfort, I’m for bringin’
help, ye scut.”
He’s back in twenty minutes, with a dillied Ger-
man crew.
“Three’ll carry in the gun,” sez he, “the rest will
carry you.”
I dunno how he got ’em, but he made them barrer me.
They lugged the gun before him, ’n’ he yarded
them like geese.
Then Mickie s’lutes the Major. “They’re in custody,”
sez he,
“Fer conduc’ calculated to provoke a breach iv peace,
A-tearin’ iv me uniform, ’n’ ’saultin’ the po-lice.”
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

“Bung”: “Jimmie Pannikin” (8.14/2/’18)
complains that the cultivation of native fruits and
flowers has received very little attention in our
country schools. Well, why 'does not the Depart-
ment give the teachers a bit of encouragement? In
1916 every kid-whacker throughout N. S. Wales
was ordered to pull off his coat, build fences and
plant trees for protection against westerly winds,
whether they blew in the locality or 'not. A
friend of mine applied for seedlings of that beauti-
ful South Coast native, the boobyaloe. He wrote
voluminously regarding his experience with the
tiee, and explained its hardiness, rapid growth to
maturity, and other considerations that made it an
ideal playground shade-tree and ornament. In reply
he received a consignment of the sorrowful pin us
msrgnis and Canadian giant elms!
* * * *
“Jackdaw”: I flayed a wombat once. I
shudder when I think of it. It took two hours’ hard
knifing to cut off the rind, 11 minutes’ hard nailing
to peg it on a tree, the sun six weeks’ hard flaming
to dry the thing, me two hours-and-a-bit’s hard
rasping to get the slivers off, and the henchman three
minutes’ hard sawing to trim the edges. But a
healthy old wombat cover makes an everlasting door-
mat, so the time wasn’t wasted.
* * * *
‘Dug-out”: For the kill-’em-quick stakes
I’ll put.my bit on a little grey insect about an inch
long, covered with grey hairs, or bristles, and com-
monly known in the southern districts of N. S. Wales
as fifty-legs.. I saw a fox-terrier that was tunnelling
after a rabbit suddenly go through a series of circu-
lar movements accompanied by frantic howls and
desperate attempts to clear something from his nose.
Upon investigation I found that a fifty-legs had
fastened on to foxie. When freed of the hanger-on
the dog developed tired feeling and reclined in the
shade of a near-by gum. About 40 minutes later he
was dead. AVhat’s the name and pedigree of this
murderous insect or reptile?

“li.A.Y.” ; There’s another champion axe-
man, named Gardiner, cutting fuel for Clarence River
(N.S.W.) butter factories. He offers to back himself
to fall, chop and split a cord of firewood (green oak)
in an hour. It looks a tall order; but so far no one
who has seen him use the chopper has reached for
his dough.

“Know Nothing”: Consider the bullocky
on the North Coast (N.S.W.). Always he is pur-
sued by a most business-like Stock De-
partment, with a. threat to cancel his per-
mit to work unless he dips his bullocks
every 21 days in a mixture that would
scald the rings off a maul. With the
certainty of 10 or 12 days’ lost time, the
horror of a maddened team, the possi-
bility of some of them dying (and of re-
ceiving no compensation if they do), is it
any wonder that the jangled man embalms
the atmosphere at times? If, as some-
times happens, he gets in a full working
month and draws his timber any old
distance up to 20 miles, he probably
finds the bar bad, no boats coming in and
no timber going out —unless on a flood. To
make a real bullock-driver, secure a young
church-going man; sell him a team of 20
for £460; charge him £1 ss. per week
for grass, and for white hide about Ss.
per inch; license 2s. 6d. per month, and
royalty Is. per 100. Lead him to a patch
of pine; explain that generally he has to
fall and work it himself and cut his
own roads. By the time that is done
he will be in a good frame of mind to
lose the 10 or 12 days for dipping. You
needn’t remind him that it may rain
throughout the days that intervene
between dippings. May Peter in his
mercy pass in the ox-cohductor without
going into details!
“Wandi”: The animal which “Crosscut”
inquires about (B. 21/2/’18) is probably the banded
ant-eater, or myrmecobius, a graceful and active
little creature with long ears and a long, bushy tail,
somewhat like a squirrel when running about. The
upper part of the / body is reddish, darkening towards
the tail and crossed with white or yellow bands. The
under surface is light yellow. The beast is diurnal
in habit, retiring at night into a hollow log or a
hole in the ground, where it also breeds, producing
half a dozen, and occasionally as many as nine, young

“L.A.G.” : Ever tasted fresh pig which has
been fed during the early autumn on the refuse frofn
the wine-press? It has a fragrant flavor of its own,
and is most tender. The refuse, stalks and skins, is
merely stacked in the pig yard, and the swine eat
into it as cattle eat into a straw-stack. A good feed
makes ’em very groggy. ' Fowls, too, take to the
grape tucker, which is very fattening.
‘‘Loco”: Our little loco.-shed stables One
engine only. The other morning, when I went on
to the footplate to get ready for a trip, I noticed
some stray ends of grass and. rubbish sticking out
from the recess between the double roof of the cab.
A couple of sparrows had begun to build. I ran the
train, taking the nest along. Next morning more
progress and more protest from the builders as we
moved out. This went on for several days. Then
the cheeky pair began to get careless "and drop
things all over the footplate; so I removed the
nest before complications arose.
* * * *
“Kahbionba”: I have never struck a dis-
trict where goitre did not occur. There are, I
think, at least two very distinct forms of the dis-
ease, one especially incidental to high-strung, over-
active brain-workers, perhaps usually when nearing
their climacteric, and perfectly irrespective of
whether they live on a citied plain or on mountain-
tops. The other form is endemic, and seems to be
caused mainly, if not entirely, by soil-secretions—
so far as I can learn, magnesian. Women who
drink nothing but tank-water turned into tea seem
even more liable to the complaint than those who
are spring-bibbers. The primary absorption in
these cases is done by the cattle whose flesh and
milk are consumed by the patients. I would like to
hear from anyone who has had experience of goitral
eases where there are no known magnesian elements
in the soil of the neighborhood.
“Breeza”: Talking about rabbit poisoning,
I have tried miscellaneous mixtures, and found
thistle roots with jam and a little whisky (say, a
small nobbier to a 21b. tin of jam) the best thing
to carry the poison. Much ahead of a eucalyptus
decoy. With whisky and sugar on thistle roots I
have had better success than with any advertised
decoy; ditto with roots,, sugar pnd a little nutmeg.
But I look for success with these only when grass is
dry and feed generally scarce.
*** * A
'“Bouragi”: Twenty-five years of rigid
suppression and missionary effort in Papua have
utterly failed to suppress the “magician” who
flourishes with a power and prestige unknown even
among the ju-ju men of West Africa. Even the
most carefully brought-up of native teachers nurses
in his heart a deadly fear pf the pourri-pourri man,
and retains a belief in his powers which is a good
deal more definite than his ideas on, say, the doc-
trine of the Trinitj'. Among that excellent force
the Armed Native Constabulary, many pourri-pourri
men are to be found, though they are daily accus-
tomed to the arrest and degradation of supposedly
all-powerful magicians, and are more in contact than
any other Papuans with the practical and logical
side of the white man’s beliefs. One of the best
native n.c.o.’s that I ever knew was a famous pourri-
pourri man, though he did not practice openly. He
had been reared from childhood among the Thursday
Island pearlers, and spoke English with an ease and
fluency that put many of his white contemporaries
in the shade. His manners and habits, and even his
clothing:, when he was out of uniform, were those of
what we consider a superior civilisation. He
delighted greatly in the evenings to dress in white
ducks and yellow boots and parade the. station like a
European, and his opinions of his fellow natives, on
the drill ground or in court, were in quite the white
mans vein. Aet with it all, and his sterling
honesty and reliability, he was a professed operator
of black magic and believed in it as thoroughly as
any of the savages about him. The Papuan medicine*
men move with the times, too. The Anglican Mission
not so long ago found the work of years undone bv
one of their converts, who set up a religion of his
own, curiously combined of Christianity and pourri-
pourri. When I last heard of it, the cult was going
strong.
* * ♦ *
‘W.M.S.”: The “policeman fly” inquired
about by “Steve Hart” (B. 28/2/’18) is, a wasp, and
there are several species in Australia. Digger-wasps
are largely represented in the country, but they do
not all prey on the same insects. The flycatchers
usually store their captures in cells dug in the
ground. In the west and south-west of N. S.
Wales these John Hops may be seen arresting flies
from the backs of the sheep. One species has a black
head, and yellow face, with a black spot in the
centre; another is generally black in tint, varied
with reddish brown; a third is of black and gold.
The prisoners are stored in the cells as food for the
larva of the wasps. Many members of the family
paralyse the insects they gaol by stinging them in
the vital nerve centres, so that the tucker will be
fresh when the eggs are hatched out. It remains
alive, but is unable to move or to escape when
attacked by the voracious grubs. A larger species of
wasp, commonly known as the house-hornet, preys
wholly on spiders. These are paralysed in the same
manner and packed into the mud cells, which the
wasps build and seal up after the eggs have been
laid. There is nothing more marvellous in nature
than the unerring in which the female wasp
proceeds to reduce her prey to a state of inertia
without otherwise injuring it.

“Drofsed” : Is a white porcupine ( echidna)
common or is it a freak? I found one the other day
and' took him home. . But the trouble was to keep
him. After building several houses for him, and dis-
covering that they would not hold him, I transferred
him to a barrel with top Off and about 18in. of fresh
earth at the bottom. That diddled him. He is about
a foot long and covered with white hair and spines,
and looks like a damper with spikes when he hides
his head. His country residence was near Toora,
South Gippsland.

“Ibis” : Expound this riddle. Last season,
as has been their custom for years, a pair of welcome
or house swallows built their home in my laundry.
They duly hatched out a family of five. When the
chicks were about a week old the proud parents were
visited by hundreds upon hundreds of swallows. The
air was thick with them, though usually the welcome
swallow is not plentiful in our neighborhood. They
were in a state of wild excitement, twittering and
fluttering incessantly, all trying to get into the
laundry. The visitation lasted two days, ceasing as
abruptly as it commenced. Is it an extraordinary
event for a swallow to hatch five chicks, and had all
swallowdom come to pay respects to such success,
or what was the game? Later in the season pa and
ma had another try, but hatched only two chicks.

“Copabella”: It is on March 14 that a
paternal Victorian Government collects a
small fee from shopkeepers who register their
premises, or a large one (£10) from those
who forget to do it. As might be expected
from a legislature of half-measures it doesn’t
bother at all what sort of old humpy it
registers. For the benefit of the very fre-
quent house-manager who doesn’t think about
the old man’s dinner until about 4.55 p.m.,
most of the little hucksters who decorate
street corners in residential' areas, with
their windows littered, with everything
from liquorice-straps and fireworks to haber-
dashery and potatoes, keep a lump of cooked
corned-beef and a piece of “german sausage.”
A Pure Food regulation says such eatables
must be kept away from flies, where and
'how not specified. I lately heard an inquiry for
“sixpenno’th of corned*” and the dainty was
produced from a drawer which also stocked
bootlaces and I don't know what else. When
the next ptomaine catastrophe finds its way
into the newspapers, it is to be hoped some-
body will remember to ask whether the cause
Of it was kept in a gin-case under the- bed or
in a biscuit-tin in the fowlhouse. The
collection of a registration fee should either
be big enough to carry some privilege or
responsibility, or it should be. abolished.

“Cyril S.” : “Arthur R.’s” experiment with
a low aerial (B. 7/3/’IS) proves nothing,
because he only received on it and did not
send. It. is easy to receive without aerials,
but you cannot send without them. The “lines
of electro-motive force” of oscillating waves
bend downwards until they reach the earth.
Naturally the higher you, start those waves
the longer they take to reach the earth and
the more stations they include. In the field
sets, where weight and speed in erecting are
important considerations, the telescopic pole
is still part of the necessary equipment. It
would be promptly dropped if it were useless.
Every man from his own standpoint.
Wyndham Hayes, sanitary contractor, giv-
ing evidence before the Sutherland water-
supply select committee (N.S.W. Parlia-
ment), in answer to a question about the
population, said it varied between 500 pans
per week in winter and nearly 2000 at
('hristmas, but in the slackest times it never
got below 500. ‘.‘The council books,” he
said, “will show that last year I did between
40,000 and 50,000 pans.”
* * * *
“Stripes’’: Why does the Defence Depart-
ment persist in the lift bit of wasting, postage-
stamps on notices forwarded to trainees and
citizen-force cadets? Probably it is nothing
more than a clumsy effort to show a profit in
departments. The Postal Dept, sells (say l
240,000 penny stumps at face value (£1000) ;
the Defence Department enters this on the,
debit side of its pay-books. But the money
is only shifted from one pocket to another :
and an army of officials is employed to help
with the shifting. Some day, perhaps pot in
this generation, a Prime Minister with some
business knowledge will lift the axe to this
foolishness.
“Seeseed” : “Arthur II.” says (B. 7/3/’18)
that the huge aerial masts in wireless will
soon disappear, and that he heard Sydney
from Melbourne with a door-bell circuit only
four feet from the ground. Quite likely. But
if “Arthur” can send a message that far from
a four-feet aerial his fortune is made.. He
says that Balsillie communicated from
Hobart to Melbourne with wires buried ip
the earth. But when it is added that Bal-
sillie’s experiments were carried out in the
shadow of Hobart’s big aerial, any wireless
export will know that they were almost cer-
tainly an instance of what is known as re-
radiation—the energy from the buried wires
was radiated to the aerial and thence re-
radiated to Melbourne.

“G.N.” : Wooden meat-skewers (B. 7/3/TS)
were manufactured 'some years ago in con-
siderable quantities by a mill-owner at Pyr-
liiont, Sydney, and he also invented the
machine that produced them. He turned out
a much more finished article than the im-
ported (at that time principally American),
and, moreover, was supplying them at from
2s. to 3s. per thousand cheaper. Local
butchers were tumbling over themselves to
secure the output, and the same applied to
big firms throughout Maoriland. Curiously
enough, it was lack -of enthusiasm on the
part of the manufacturer that caused the
imported article to again be resorted to.
A hustler with the same opportunity would
by this time have been skewered to a for-
tune.
“Umntu”: Homing Billjims find that the
natives of the Cape fight shy of the two-
shilling piece. This is the result of deceit
and duplicity of Germans. When the florin
returned to common use a few years ago,
Hun traders had the bright idea of palming
them off on the natives as half-crowns. The
niggers were taken in for a while, but when
they found out the truth they called the new
coin “Igelemani” (a German), and it bears
that name among them to this day, as poor
old Kropf, the Lutheran missionary, had to
chronicle in his Kaffir Dictionary.
“McNorth”; In these days of high prices
couldn’t something be done with the dugongs
and turtles so abundant on our far-northern
coasts? The dugong is a big amphibian
weighing up to half a ton, with wholesome,
nourishing flesh that tastes like a cross be-
tween pork and beef. It also contains a
valuable oil. As for turtles, the writer is
no epicure, but a turtle steak is the most
luscious meat he knows of. It is sweet,
juicy and tender. Up here at Cape York
Binghi’s method of catching the beasts is to
go out in a canoe or dinghy armed with a
long, heavy pole and a barbed spear about
a foot long, to which is attached 30 or 40
fathoms of stout lino. The spear is fitted
loosely into a hole in the end of the pole.
When a turtle or dugong is Sighted, Binglii
paddles quietly towards it, and when at a
convenient distance plunges the spear into
its back. The weight of the pole drives the
spear home, and witli the end of the line
njade fast to a thwart Black Brudder tows
his prize to the beach, where Mrs. Binglii
and the kids have the fire, going ready to
roast the lunch. Certain spots in the
waters of the Gulf of Carpentaria, Torres
Strait and the Barrier Reef have turtles
and dugongs in any quantity. There ought
to lie something in the business if it were
gone into properly, say, witli sailing boats
fitted with curing gear and Binghi spearmen,
under white direction. Even if the meat
could not be sold at lower prices than beef
and mutton, the business would at any rate
bring a very palatable change of diet within
reach of mobs of people who are paying
Zbdiae prices for very commonplace food.
*' j,,; * * *
“ Ell iven”; A friend back from Calcutta
brings the news that the record • price
obtained for wlleatbags by the largest jute
mills there is 3s. 3d. per dozen. Cocky has
to spar up anything from 9s. upwards.
“Leave it to me,” said the Prime Minister.
I can quite understand why in private com-
mercial life the Prime Minister got as far
as' mending umbrellas.
THE EASY WAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It s all as easy as can be. The land you plough, and
. sow
V'it'll all the paying - soils of seeds; then wait, and
watch them grow.
Gould anything lie easier, north, south or east or
west ? —.
\o’u merely make a start, and let the seasons do
the rest.
the cow that gives off milk and cheese has nothing
■" else to do
Except to bite the barley-grass, and chew, and chew’
and chew.
And that entails no work, for when’ the grass is short
■ ; the rain
You pray for on your bended knees will make it
grow again.
The pig’s a simple matter, too. He grunts when he
- is fed,
And grunts, too, when lie isn’t —so you let him go
ahead.
And he is happy when there's mud for him to
wallow in ;
Therefore, to clean away the mud would be a useless
sin.
The poultry, too, have nothing else to do but lay
and lay,
Except, of course, the roosters—and so roosters do
not pay.
Aiid whcii you gather in the egg’s and place them on
the shelves,
The hens need no attention —they are scratching for
themselves,
A fid oh! the way the pumpkins grow—a hundred to
the vine !
And spuds—the sort of spuds on which a king would
like to dine!
And turnips, and those other things whose labels I
forget—
All waiting for the prices that are quite the highest
) yet.
A. simple life, an easy life.... I wonder why 1 drive
A cramp-held pen toy day and night to keep myself
alive?
Some day I’ll leave the grind of it, the everlasting
care,
And ’midst, the cows and pigs and hens become a
millionaire!
JJ.S.W.
R.J.C.
THE VILLAINS STILL PURSUED HIM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The grocer .sold him sugar with considerable grit,
His tea was often willow-leaves with chemicals unfit
For humankind’s consumption, but poor Carter didn’t
know,
And something in the salmon helped his early 1
overthrow.
The sausages of beef or pork that Carter ate for tea
ere rarely what good, honest, homely sausages
should be.
The acid in them wrecks a man’s digestion, we are
told—
A trouble aggravated by the stuff the dairy sold.
The bread that Carter fed on, and the butter, and
the jam
Were all a little, hocussed ; there was something in
the lamb.
These finished him ,at thirty. Loving relatives
bespoke
A coffin made of Oregon but faked to sell as oak.

COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 14th March, 1918.
Tenders for the Supply of Condensed
Milk to the Royal Australian Navy.
TIIENDERS will be received until 12 noon
A. on Friday, . r >th April, 191 S, for the
supply of Condensed Milk to the Royal Aus-
tralian Navy until 80th June, 1919.
Tender forms, specifications and any
further particulars may be obtained on ap-
plication at the Office of the Director of Navy
Contracts, Navy Office, Melbourne; the
Victualling Store Officer, Royal Edward
Victualling Yard, Pyrmont, Sydney; and at
the Naval Staff Offices, Brisbane, Birken-
head (S.A.), Fremantle and Hobart.
JOSEPH COOK,
Minister of State for the Navy.

Applications must be addressed to —
The Secretary,
Department of Defence,
Melbourne,
and forwarded so as to reach him not later
than the 30th April, 1918.
T. TRUMBLE,
Secretary.
“RUN-DOWN'' PEOPLE.
The condition of being “run-down” is one
that doctors do not recognise as a disease;
the physician of to-day who gets his training
in a hospital where severe disorders only are
encountered, knows little about it. But
those who are run-down in health know that
it is not a fancied affliction.
The expression “run-down” comes from
the feeble action of an unwound clock, and
the comparison is a good one. Applied to
health, it means a condition in which all the
bodily functions are enfeebled. Appetite
fails, the digestion is impaired, the nerves are
impoverished, the complexion becomes pale;
there is no animation, but rather worry and
mental depression. Fatigue is a constant
symptom.
No particular organ being affected, you
must look for relief to the blood, as it circu-
lates everywhere. Improvement in the blood
is quicklv felt throughout the entire system,
and Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are one of the
best and most convenient blood-builders. As
your blood becomes rich and red, the various
organs regain their tone and the body re-
covers its vigor. If you have any or all of
the above symptoms try the tonic treatment
with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, but never buy
abstitute.

Up the Blood,” to Dr. Williams’ Medicine
, Box 594 G.P.0., Sydney.— Advt.


STOP STOMACH DRUGGING.
.NEUTRALISE THE DANGEROUS
ACID WITH A LITTLE MAGNESIA.
Stomach drugging is dangerous. Drugs
otten deaden the • nerves and render them
insensible to pain, but pain serves a good
purpose it is Nature’s method of indicat-
ing that something is interfering with the
smooth working .of the human organism.
When the fault is corrected, the pain will
cease. lain after eiitiug—heartburn, flatu-
lence, etc. usually indicates not that the
stomach is diseased, but that it is troubled
by excessive acidity. The acid irritates and
inflames the delicate lining of the stomach,
and so causes pain. Obviously it is of prime
importance that the cause of this pain should
be removed, and to accomplish this you
should obtain some bisurated magnesia from
yo , u f' chemist - and take half a teaspoonful in
a little water immediately after meals. This
is the remedy which produced such excellent
results in the case of Miss Alice M. Allen.
OJ Fletcher Street, Auburn, Victoria, who
says: “I suffered so severely from indiges-
tion that I often had to lie down, quite ex-
hausted by the pain. I became thin and
felt worn-out. Nothing seemed to do me the
least good until I tried bisurated magnesia,
but the first dose of this made me feel better,
and I have had no return of my complaint."
Equally good results are invariably accom-
plished by bisurated magnesia, because it
neutralises the harmful acid which is the
underlying cause of all the. trouble, and.
having done this, it soothes, heals and
strengthens the stomach and ensures proper
digestion of the food.— Auvt.

Great was the rally on Wednesday last
week when the War Loan got a big send-off
f y om the N.S.W. Millions Club. The A.M.P.
Society through its chairman contributed
two barrow-loads of paper-money to the
movement, and others supplied from a barrel-
tul down to half a pint, according to
their means. The total ran into millions.
Lilt all this glory shouldn’t blind us to the
■tact that it was, in the main, a case of lend-
ing ourselves our own lOTJ’s payable by
ourselves to ourselves, and that our stupen-
dous foreign debts are redeemable in gold, a
commodity that is growing rather rare. All
the local paper-money prosperity that can
be imagined makes no difference in the dire
need to leave off foreign borrowings. When
the foreign loan falls due, even £loo' of
paper, good at full face value in Geelong and
Parramatta and every other town of the
Commonwealth, including Alice Springs and
Pine Creek and Tibooburra, won’t liquidate
10s. of it.
Australia seems to have accepted the
“Lord” Forrest imposition without a kick
or a murmur. The fact that he hasn’t a
son may be partly accountable for public
indifference to Bigjohn’s bauble, but it goes
“THAT BRUTE'LL BITE ME YET.”
$
without saying that somebody with a son
and heir (like, the late William Clarke) will
get a transmittable handle presently, and
the old man’s decease will be the signal for
the press of this great country to refer to
his offspring as “Sir” or “Lord.” The son
may be an exceptionally poor specimen of
an Australian in intellect and physique, not
to mention _ morals, but the stamp of
nobility” will be on every link of him. As
a subject for snobbery and grovel he will
serve just as badly as a better man. The
girl who shrinks from marrying him will be
bribed with a titular distinction for h-rself,
and the prospect of becoming the mothe* of
a personage. Canada is now protesting
against having such detriments to manhood
thrust upon the body politic. When is the
Australian National Party going to protest?

Our party system gives some weird re-
sults. There was in the Queensland Labor
crowd m last Parliament a keen, solid busi-
ness man named T. L. Jones, who had the
respect of everybody. Pie was defeated. The
other very experienced business man in the
.Labor party was Lennon, formerly northern
manager for Burns, Pliilp and Co. He had a
hard fight too. The fact seems to be that, while
men like this get more venomous opposition
than the average from the “right-thinking
person” who fancies that a man should vote
with Ins bank account and not with his
head, they don’t arouse much enthusiasm
among what is called their own crowd, which
doesn’t think at all.

If the worst ever arrives and Pearce is
pmed from his job of Director-General of
Muddle, it will be easy for him to start in
business. The ad. in the Argus will run
like this: —
COLLEGE OF CHAOS.
Students Receive Individual Tuition.
Young men before deciding upon a career should
write for particulars of the curriculum at this insti-
tution. There are unlimited opportunities in the
world for making a mess. Why not make a bigger
mess than anyone else? Thousands of men are
turned out of our public schools and universities
who are unable to do anything except follow hum-
dium principles and strain after efficiency. Do not
be misled. Efficiency is a fetich. Look around you
and count up the number of bungling citizens who
have risen to fame and fortune. Seize the oppor-
tunity ; emulate their achievements. Take notice
of all the little men in big places. Dare to be
Little! Learn how to keep the sluicegate of waste
ajar. Consult Me. Pamphlets containing a narra-
tive of “WHAT I DID BEFORE I WAS FOUND
OUT” posted free on application.
Any citizen who refuses to give him a testi-
monial will, of course, be prosecuted under
the War Precautions Act.
Maoriland Treasurer Joe Ward has been
again explaining the justice of letting in-
come derived from war loans escape taxa-
tion. Boiled- down it comes to this:—
Maoriland will probably contract a war debt of
£100,000,000. By paying the lenders 41 per cent,
free of income tax the country will save £375,000
annually, which would be lost by paying 61 per
cent, and levying taxation. If the higher rate were
given and taxation imposed there would be no more
“cheap money for settlers.”
To assert that there is national economy in
removing £100,000,000 from the ambit of
tax is characteristic of Joe Ward. And so is
the yarn about the connection between keep-
ing money “cheap for settlers and workers”
by paying only 41 per cent, to big lenders
for war. The effect of the exemption is to
give these lenders a 6 and 7 per cent, invest-
ment and must inevitably push up the rate
for other requirements. However, Ward
now promises that next time he borrows the
little man shall be able to get in at 5 per
cent, and pay income tax ; but the heavily-
gilded citizen will still get 4i< without
deduction. Which makes tilings no better
for the general body of taxpayers, but only
a little worse, since the man who wouldn’t
pay any tax to speak of will now get a
trifle more interest, while the other person
won’t be affected at all.
The feminine note is much heard in the
S. Aus. election. It is not only that Mrs.
Jeanne Young and a less-known Mrs. Sig-
gins are standing for Parliament. Crawford
Vaughan, the ex-Premier, has left his inter-
ests in the hands of his clever wife while he
is orating in America. It seems to have
been understood that she would not re-
nominate him, and the Fusion party chose
its candidates. But nominated he is, though
he has now to be classified as an Indepen-
dent.
From an old Northern hand :
Queensland Labor-leader Ryan is to be provided
with a Federal seat very soon; most likely he will
step into the shoes of Maranoa Page. He has his
eyes on the Prime Ministership of Australia; and
now that he has so successfully led his party back
to power, the time is ripe for a step towards his
goal. Theodore will become leader in Queensland,
and though without the brilliance of Ryan he is
strong and capable, and has the confidence of his
party. The Cabinet will undergo a few changes.
Fihelly is the most certain of the old bunch. Fear-
less, unrestrained and full of faith in himself, he has
become the idol of a large section of Queenslanders.
At the same time he is the most bitterly-hated man
in the State. If he keeps his head he will achieve
much. Huxham, Ilardacre and Coyne may be re-
placed by younger men. This is a young man’s
party nothing if not vigorous. Hunter is sure of
a place if he does not immediately go to London as
Agent-General. McCormack, the ex-Speaker, will be
in the Cabinet if he pleases. If Coyne leaves the
Railways Kirwan, the ex-porter, is almost certain of
the portfolio. The team otherwise will be very
strong, with an even more pronounced tendency to
State-ownership, than hitherto. But it will sadly
miss Ryan when he goes. He is shrewd and keen,
and has the capacity for making friends and a
courtesy which nothing can ruffle. The South doesn’t
yet know this man.
THE PRISONER OF WAR.
Ryan, of Queensland, will have a brother-
in-law —Mahoney, of Pittsworth—to help
him in the new House if the contingent vote
doesn’t out him. Mahonev is a lawyer; so
is F. T. Brennan, who beat Tolmie, the ex-
Liberal leader, who had held the Toowoom-
ba seat for 17 years.
Bowser of Victoria wasn’t an inspiriting
figure, but it will at least be remembered to
his credit that he threw up his job when
the Labor party and its allies carried a
snatch vote against his plans for reducing
the deficit. An Australian Premier who
goes out on that issue is almost as rare as
the man who committed suicide bv dropping
bombs on himself from an aeroplane. 11 S
MEAN DOG.
“Mean — why, he struck a winner on Satur-
day"
“And wouldn't let you on to it?”
“Worse than that — wouldn't lend me a quid
to hack it."
The old Earl of Halsbury, 92, onetime Lord
Chancellor, tottered into the House of Peers
the other day in order to move the rejection
of the bill to admit women as solicitors. The
excited ancient implored the House to keep
ladies off the legal grass which had been ever
sacred to their lordships’ sex. Doubtless he
wept as he thought of what his grandfather
would have said to this bill. And still lie
was defeated—by 47 votes to 19.
THE STRAIGHT GO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Every pound put into the War Loan hits the
Kaiser.”
Heads or tails,
Lose or win
Somebody fails
Where the big shells spin.
Somebody fails
As all shall see;
Heads or tails—
The Kaiser or me!
Who's got the will?
Who s got the heart ?
There s many a spill
the powder-cart.
The straightest shot
Is the one to kill;
And I’m out to pot
lheir Kaiser Bill.
He or I
It’s a straight-out go.
Neither can fly
In the face of his foe.
Bullet or cash
Or work or brain,
It’s bash, bash, bash
Until one is slain.
Would I be free,
There’s a path to choose ;
And the job’s for me—
Not Pearce or Hughes.
There’s the job to do
Or the whole world fails.
It’s the Kaiser or You—
Heads or Tails?
N.S.W.
MARY McC.
THE BLAME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The question now is upon whose shoulders should
the blame [for the Defence Department’s chaos] fall?”
Wanted—a scapegoat. Is there one in sight?
Some underling whose steps remain in doubt,
Who looked a little to the left or right
Or with some bold suggestion keen and bright
Appeared the heavyweights of power to flout.
Let him be sought. The wilderness is wide
Where he may lose himself in shifting sands,
Thirst for his mate and hunger for his bride;
There let him take our sins and so abide,
For we must get the matter off our hands.
It was a fine idea old Moses found,
And still it works to match our soul’s desire:
Get some poor wight to bear the burden round
And leave our precious reputation sound.
Who plucked the chestnuts for us from the fire ?
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.
