WORMS.
ONE NINE INCHES LONG AND ANOTHER FOUR
INCHES PASSED BY CHILD AFTER TAKING
COMSTOCK’S “DEAD SHOT” WORM PELLETS.
Mrs. A. E. Bade, of 203 McKillop Street,
East Geelong, Vic., writes: —“I have great
pleasure in writing to you to let you know
the satisfactory results I had in using your
‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets for my young son,
aged 14 years. He had been ailing for some
time and complained of pain in the spine and
a very sore stomach, and often in the night
would start vomiting without any warning
whatever. Pie also had several fainting
turns. I became alarmed and took him to
the doctor, but the treatment did him no
good. I then thought I would try your
Worm Tablets, as to me he had every
appearance of worms. I procured a bottle
of them, together with a bottle of Indian
Root Pills, and commenced the treatment.
The second day after taking them he passed
a stomach worm nine inches long, and the
next day one four inches long. Since then
he has picked up wonderfully and is twice
the child he was. If he shows any signs
again I will not hesitate to try them, as I
was so pleased with the results. I am most
grateful to the proprietors of these tablets,
as my husband is away on Active. Service,
and the worry of our boy being so ill nearly
drove me mad. Thanking you again.”
STOMACH WORMS.
A MENACE TO CHILDREN.
Stomach worms are undoubtedly the most
invidious kind of all. They inhabit the
stomach and inflict great havoc upon their
victim. They deprive the victim of half the
nutriment he consumes, and when the
stomach is empty gnaw the sides of the
stomach, causing such terrible sensations as
to often cause faintness. Aperient medi-
cines have no effect upon them, for when
any purgative is taken they fasten them-
selves to the walls of the stomach by a
groove running down their side. The best
method of dislodedns stomach worms is to
starve the system and take Comstock’s
“Dead Shot’’ Worm Pellets. The worms are
weakened by the want of food, and are
induced to take whatever is taken into the
stomach. Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm
Pellets contain the pr opertics which sicken
and disable-the worms, and a dose of opening
medicine will clear therm out of the system.
Comstock’s “Dead Phot” Worm Pellets
are a purely vegetable i nedicinal preparation
for the eradication of w onus in children and
adults. For sale by all chemists and store-
keepers, price 3s. 6d. pnr packet, or will be
sent post paid at the same price by The
W. H. Comstock Co., Lttd., 23 Lang Street,
Church Hill. Sydney; or Farish Street,
Wellington. N.Z.

NOTIONS
Contemplating Saturday’s Doncaster from
the distance of a previous week, the calm certainty
with which cash had been hurried on to various
nags is mighty hard to understand. There is never
anything certain about a Doncaster, and this year
it looks just as open as usual. In spite of' his
9st. 121 b. Cetigne is entitled to great respect, and
it would be no surprise to this page if the champion
won. Hut the last furlong of a Doncaster is gruel-
ling, and lcwt. 261 b. is likely to feel terribly heavy
even to so resolute a horse as this. But make no
mistake about Cetigne having a chance. Wolaroi
may not be forward enough for the trip. Panacre
will certainly do better than at Melbourne. Of
performers during the last two or three weeks nothing-
lias done so well as Dame Acre, who, if she gets
.away, can be looked for with the best when the line
is sighted. But whether she is good enough to see
?-n Sol , im, ' lun ~ n' l° right out must remain in doubt
till she does it. At Rosehill she had the last furlong-
all to herself. Satin Bird, winner of the last Epsom,
is doing well on the track and is something more
than a possibility. This writer will be expecting to
notice him at some stage of the proceedings and to
get a glimpse of Panacre, Cetigne, Wedding Day and
lah King. Of these it is not inconceivable that the
last may be first.
* * * *
There was a doubt when this was written
whether Wallace Isinglass would be a starter in
Sydney Cup, there being indications that Old King
Coal might prefer to give Prince Viridis a fly
and let the other interview Desert Gold again. It
doesn’t matter to this page anyhow. Never having-
had much time for Wallace it wouldn’t stand him
tor the Cup in any case. He has failed too often in
handicaps to inspire confidence now. Westcourt and
Kandos have been coming on well, and if nothing
went wrong during the past week should be some-
where in the picture. The others are a. mixed lot
which, so far as staying power goes, are mostly
rumor, and the remainder are burdened with weight,
i i'i nee Bardolph is an exception to the first remark,
though not to the last. Included in the field is a
little, pinched-up looking mare called Lady Liege,
with 6st. 111 b., who won over 10 furlongs at Band
wick on New Year’s Day. On that occasion it looked
as if she could have gone on indefinitely. If the
Cup happens to be run in the wet, 6st. 11 lb. might
be very handy. Otherwise Westcourt and Kandos
ought to live up to their reputations.
* * * \ *
Satin Bird was given a go in a welter
mile at the Hawkesbury meeting on Saturday and
ran as poorly as a mule. But there, is a substantial
difference _ between 9st. 51b. and Vst. 101 b., the
weight this nag has in the Doncaster. Farivale, a
Victorian, scrambled into x third place, but the Bird
was away astern.
* * * *
Frank E, another Doricasterite, did better.
He was beaten out of place over six furlongs by an
ordinary lot at Newcastle the previous week, and at
the Hawkesbury had to argue the point with top-
class sprinters like Rosedell, La Steppe and Delin-
aere. But that didn’t matter. Starting at 12 to 1
he touched off Delinacre by a head, La Steppe four
lengths away. That Frank can run a mile right out
has been explained at Randwick twice. Jt was a
good Doncaster trial.
* * *
There was nothing about Ivennaquhair’s
perlormance m winning the Hawkesbury Handicap
on Saturday to make the punter who backed some-
thing else for Sydney Cup feel sorry. The colt had
to be ridden right out to beat a second-rater. Even
granted that he be a slug, Kennaquhair will have
to do a long way better than this to win the Cup.
tic hasn t had any favors from the weight-fixer in
that event either.
*** . *
The ■ Welkin colt Volpi, who earned dis-
tinction at Rosehill by staying- behind the barrier
in one race and rushing off in a violent hurry with
the next, earned a long string of curses at the
Hawkesbury on Saturday. He was backed like a
certainty down to 5 to 4 on, played up at the
post as if possessed by numerous devils and ran
sourly all the-wav into second place. A tote div
of 4s._6d. enabled place bettors to lose ss. 6d. mi
each investment.
* * * *
At the Hawkesbury meeting the tote paid
more than one dividend in five races. In evierv
instance backers of the second horse lost money.
How much longer is this farce going to be endured?
Meanwhile what happened in one race is worth
recording as an illustration of what is occurring all
oyer the premises.. The winner -was backed in the
ring at 7’s and 4’s. Anyone who put a pound on
at the last figure drew £5 from Ike. But anyone
who backed first, second and third horses on the
tote, each with a. pound, drew £4 ss. 6d. There
weie 11 starters in /this particular event, yet the
tot , e ,. lS ™ n on a hit-headed system which makes the
public take shorter jbdds against picking three than
could be got next dqor against picking it in one.

Tip-country battler Luckv Bean, second in

Past year, when again fancied, lie declined to start

Maori land felt as sorry to hear of Desert
Cold being- beaten at Melbourne as if something had
happened to the price of the fat lamb. And in the
midst oi their misery several writers sat down to
revise their estimates of the lady. Perhaps Ibis
may have been desirable in any case, but there is
no need lor them to worry. Desert Gold is a very
beautiful mare, with considerably more courage
stamina and pace than two of the' nags she finished
behind. Nor is it at all certain that over the dis-
tance most favorable to Cetigne the mare would be
beaten in a weight-for-age trial. That over a
series of distances from six to 16 furlongs she is
the best animal racing in Australia to-dav scarcely
admits of argument. Certainly for Maoriianders to
claim that Biplane has greater stamina than Desert
, - « preposterous. It would do no harm to wait
until Biplane has been tested in that respect next
year. Ihe most that was ever claimed for Desert Gold
was that she was an exceptionally free-going, gener-
ous prad That can still stand. In spite of her
defeats she is one of the most brilliant and versa-
tne mokes the Commonwealth has known.

"Troopering R.8.R.” : In mid-October we
rode out into the desert places near the Dead Sea, a
seemingly endless ride. With casual Australian
eonidence we went into waterless wastes, but the
engineers got the necessary moisture apparently
from nowhere. So well had Allenby kept his secret
that none knew what the end was to be. Then sud-
denly the British artillery was pounding at the
great limestone ridges that protected Beersheba, and
an A.L.H. Brigade was charging for six miles into
the rear—the climax of a great outflanking move-
ment. YV ith bayonets in their hands the Anzacs on
their splendid Australian horses carried the Turkish
trenches without a stop and in one great leap.
Jacko surrendered in thousands, and his dead littered
every road about the town. But there were horses
that baulked at the Turkish trench that day, and
among them was that of poor “Tibby” Cotter,
cricketer; a Turkish sniper had added his rider to
the roll of Australian heroes. “Tibby” lies in the
Beersheba Park, alongside the other Arizae dead, in
the shade of the sighing gums of his native land.

“Centurion”: If “Tupara” (B. 21/2/T8)
had read my par. about breech-loading rifles more
carefully lie would have seen that 1 was dealing with
breech-loaders and repeaters used in the American
< ivil War. The Spencer came later, but its peculiar
magazine, located in the butt, was a feature of sev-
eral contemporary European rifles, notably one used
lor a while by the Italian army. Like the Spencer,
they had an irremediable defect —the Joss of balance
according to whether the magazine was full or
empty. The modern military rifle has its magazine
just at the “point of balance,” whether il be Lee-
Enficld Mauser or Mannlicher. Another famous
breech-loader of the ’7o’s which I forgot to mention
was the Remington, both single shot and repeating.
1 his was lmic-h used in the Balkan wars of the time,
and the Turks did great Work with it against the
Russians. The Egyptian army, under Hicks Pasha
destroyed by the first Malidi, carried Remingtons,’
fi. . 2 ia . w * lai) y of these old weapons among the
Smai Bedouins recently. The goods were probably
picked up by barter.

“Bad Australian” (from Argentina): If
it’s weighty hares you need I can send a score each

darned little good.)
Ralph de Palma, the American motor
clack, has now pulverised the world’s record for a
ho " rs ’ The previous best, an aggregate of
566 miles 589 yards, was accomplished on the Brook-
lands track (England) in 1913 by Cliassagne, Resta
and Lee-Guiness who took turns in driving a six-
cylinder Sunbeam car. De Palma, perched on his
12-cvmder Packliard, officially covered 616 miles on
tlie Sheepshead Bay track, New York, though, as he
drove very wide in each circuit measured as two
miles, the probability is that his actual mileage was
considerably more. His average speed during the
rush was 101 m.p.h., and in the first 60 minutes lie
was 110 miles nearer home. His stops amounted to
lomin. 20sec., and in that period he changed his
tyres eight times and also fed his exhausted machinery
with petrol and oil.
are ; I believe, about
1100 species of fish recorded from the Australian
seas, but it is remarkable how rarely line-fishers haul
out any but the more common kinds. A reason is
that many species, though they may be present, will
not accept a bait. During a long rock-hopping ex-
pel lence writer has only taken (of rare fishes) the
grotesque cockatoo-fish, a member of the morwong
family, and the scaly-fin, a dark brown, chubby little
chap resembling a sweep. These two fishqs arc verv sel-
dom hooked, though they are said to be fairly mimer-
ous on rocky grounds. Among fish east ashore on the
South Coast (N.S.W.) during heavy weather I’ve
found the rifle-fish (which is sometimes taken on the
Queensland coast, but is more common in the Indian
Ocean), many nannygai, parrot fish, gurnard (kur.iu
and the ordinary kind), ling-fish, sea-hedgeh6gs, flat-
head and a few'snapper. On one occasion I caught
in a moribund state, so that it could be taken by
hand, a black trevally, which is no relation of the
common trevally. This somewhat rare fish inhabits
more tropical waters, and was evidently out of its
latitude in the southern salt-lake in which it was
taken. D. G. Stead records the capture, near Senna-
s'lll (N.S.W.), of a luvarus fish, which is rare the
world over. The stranger had probably stranded
when in chase of food. It is said to run to 6ft. in
length, and to. weigh up to about 3cwt., while the
color is described as silvery salmon-pink.

"J. Quill”: Let me dig my pen into “Gas-
bag” (B. 7/3/’18), who is inflated with the comfort-
ing idea that everything is well with the fish in the
Western (N.S.W.) rivers. It is quite true as he
states, that “there are heavy penalties for using nets
and cages,” but what’s the use of heavy penalties if
the offenders are not brought along to suffer them’
1 take the opportunity of informing “Gasbag” that
the rivers are not closed during breeding time, as he
asserts; they are declared closed, but few angler<
take any notice. They fish on with nets and cages
and cross-lines and every other sort of line from
years beginning to year’s end without so much as a
irown from the Fisheries Department. Can anvone
produce a single instance in which a fisherman' lias
been fined for fishing in the N. S. Wales Western
rivers during a closed season, or with illegal
implements? &

streams of certain Kuropean and other freshwater
hsnos which are not (like brown, rainbow and Loch
Levon trout) very particular as to bait, and there-
fore easily caught.

Things the Motorist
should Remember
9 n< r° f '/ our sma^est expenses—lubrication—is your
chief safeguard against your heaviest expense—depre-
ciation.
2. Friction is relentless.
The effects of the incorrect oil will not be apparent
during the first piston stroke or the second. But
when piston strokes run up into millions—and that
does not take long—friction begins to get its due.
You need not look for the effect of wear then. You
can hear it.
3. We have solved the problem of scientific lubrication.
4. If given intelligent care and scientific lubrication
your car should run better the 2000th mile than the
first.
5. No matter how freely a low quality or wrong-bodied
oil is used, it can never do the work of the correct
lubricant.
6. No one oil will suit all cars. This is absolute.
7. You selected a car that suits you. Now select an oil
that suits your car.
8. Though you may want correct lubrication, you will
seldom stumble on it by chance.
9- The Lubrication Chart, printed in part on the right,
was prepared only after an engineering analysis of
every car. It represents our professional advice, and
specifies the correct oil for your car.

Co-operative Film Exchange, Ltd., head
oHice in Melbourne and branches in other
States, which began with a deficit of £5439
comprising preliminary expenses * £439, and
flotation loading £5OOO :
Feb., Profit. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.
10lt” o Nil £l5Ol 3935
iqic" , 9 P- c - = £930 2198 1737
1910.. 2773 10 „ „ 1165 1608 129
19i6 Reserves.
1016.. 632 632 503
Loss.
1917.. 51 - 452
Things couldn’t look much worse than that,
ilie directors explain that the last year’s
loss is due ‘partly to the increased cost of
Aims and also to the very keen local com-
petition, which has necessitated additional
expenditure in securing business.” The four
big P icture People, West’s, Spencer’s, J. D.
W imams and Taits, formed a co. to buy or
rent films and show them ; and, as that co.
also hires out films, it has a long start in the
race with this and other competitors who
have to find hirers for all their films. Then
the bigeest film-producers got into direct
touch with Australian showmen; and that
cut away more ground. On top of that comes
the Australian producer—not very important
yet, but getting stronger every year, and deal-
ing direct with showmen. Meanwhile Europe
became far too busy about other things to
spare either time or material for much film
manufacture; and the American producer
knew what that meant. So all the competi-
tion seems to be at one end, which means,
for a co. like this, buying at the maximum
Price and selling at the minimum. Things
may improve when the guns cool and (he
European producers seek an outlet for their
goods. Meantime the shareholders, after
shivering at the results of the year’s trading,
won t get much comfort from the balance-
sheet. Share capital is £11,645, and the co.
owes £29,000. Against that the items stock-
in-trade, hire of films (paid in advance) and
estimated value of unexpired lease of hired
films run to £31.697; book debts are £7133.
and there are sundries £2376. The real
value of the bulk of the assets is very difficult
to determine; and the co. is carrying heavy
risks and getting nothing for it. Of course
if it pulls through a comparatively small
profit will pay a big dividend; but share-
holders must regard it as a gamble. It might
be better to back something for the Cup.
Present writer doesn’t know what Cup; but
there is sure to be one handy. This is Aus-
tralia.
* * * *
. The output of Zeehan (Tas.) is now
limited to clean galena and tin ore. Quan-
tities and values are not disclosed. The local
paper, in reply to a question, said that “Offi-
cial information is withheld by the Govern-
ment Departments concerned.” Yet with lead
at £29 and silver at 3s. 6d. to 45., there are,
according to Mt. Lyell Co.’s experts and the
Government geologists, about 1,200,000 tons,
containing gold and silver and lead, worth
about £6,000,000, blocked out but lying idle.
Lessees of the mines containing these values
are_ encouraged to let them so lie, till the
various hydro-electric schemes are in opera-
tion—probably in the next generation. Mean-
while we and the Allies want the gold, silver
and lead. These can be produced at Zeehan
now, without waiting for a hydro-electric
scheme to extract the zinc. In the past
they. were smelted at Zeehan, and the zinc
left in the slag dump ; zinc in said dump, by
the way, is estimated at anything up to
£700,000. Those smelters, with silver at
2s. 2d. and lead at £l2 10s., made in one
year, according to the Tas. Government
auditor, £14,000 over actual treatment costs,
though, through using that sum in improve-
ments and alterations, the balance-sheet
showed a loss. This eventually led the
Anglo-Hun proprietary into debt, and the
Tas. Government became a mortgagee
for £20,000 on security of the plant,
but allowed the Kaiser, through his
agents, the Deutsche Bank, to advance
another £22,000, and so obtain a controlling
interest in the mortgage. This has taken
three and a half years to fix up, and now the
Tas. Government is offering the smelters,
dump and mining properties "of the old Anglo-
Hun co. as a whole, or in separate portions,
by tender.
In the past the co. smelted refractory ores
containing 28 p.c. zinc at a charge of from
50s. to £3 4s. per ton, on a sliding scale
varying with the amount of silver, lead and
gold associated with the zinc. The mines of
Zeehan and Dundas supplied the fluxes,
which also contain silver and lead. The cos.
and tributors received 7s. or more per ton net
on the mines, and made good profits. Those
fluxes are at present metal prices worth two
and three times, as much as they used to be.
Costs have not risen in nearly the same
proportion. Thus, on paper, any purchaser
of these smelters with a knowledge of his
business has a reasonable proposition before
him. Meantime the mines which contain
•only fluxes and second-grade lead and silver
ore lie idle, and Zeehan’s creditors and mort-
gagees live only in hopes of the good times
promised when Lyell hydro-electric schemes
fructify—“after the war”—and the
£10,000,000 of lead, zinc, gold, copper and
silver in the Read-Rosebery and other mines
of the Zeehan, Dundas and Farrell districts
are made into bullion.
* * * *
H. E. Evenden. who has been prospecting
around Cox’s Bight and Port Davey for the
Speck Government, scratched up some
molybdenite and ruby tin near the Bight,
enough to make some of his crowd peg out
an 80-acre section in this once-rushed but
disappointing country. He swears, too, that
he found bitumen caught inside wire-netting
that he put across the mouth of a creek at
Port Davey. This bitumen figured in the
mild Port Davey oil boom, extinct for a
couple of years. Cautious people then wanted
to know if it had not drifted in from outside.
Evenden thinks his netting disposed of that
idea, anyway, but does not claim to have
thrown any other light on its origin.
Queensland gold yields still sag. During
January and February last year 26,6400 z.
worth £113,159 were got; for the past two
months, 21,9510 z., worth £93,242.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character ) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped ,
Aame and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
E. J.K.: It won’t do. Plainly those notes
would have no value outside Australia; since thev could
not be cashed for gold, which is the basis of
international business dealings. Therefore thev
would be kept in Australia, and gold and the
notes that could be cashed for gold would have a
superior value, because they could do something
which the other couldn’t. The storekeeper who would
want 10 sovereigns for an article would require
11 or perhaps 12 of the notes which he oouldn’t
( augt for gold. It looks easy to raise interest-free
loans by turning the handle of a machine and print-
ing notes; but if the notes are in excess of the
amount strictly required for circulation, somebody
pays. In the instance quoted above, somebody who
took the notes at full face value would find presently
that they had depreciated. That really is the
interest that the loan costs that individual....

“R.A.Y.”: This Win-the-War Govern-
ment doesn’t do things by halves. When the
row broke out a receiving-office in N. S. Wales
was in charge of an old German, who also
carried the mails per horse and sulky to and
from the nearest post-office. He was re-
ported for having expressed himself in favor
of the Hun war policy, and the Government,
having presumably found the charge proved,
took the postal job off his hands. But it
wasn’t taken out of his house or his family.
It was given to the Hun’s wife, who is only
half German. The old lady can’t drive a
sulky, and hubby was forbidden to carry the
mail; but he accompanies the missus and
drives, while she protects the bags from pos-
sible capture by the enemy. Presumably,
also, hubby assists hep to sort and make up
mails, as there is no one else in the house to
do it.

Longer Wear-Better Service-Less Cost
It is common knowledge in engineer-
ing practice that the edged material
conveyed by belts in mining and
smelting plants cuts the unprotected
surface and soon wears out the
cheap uncovered belt.
When the Goodyear Conveyor Belt
was designed for this service, it
was designed so as to give not only
service at a lower cost but better
service.
The reinforced cover on all Good-
year Conveyor Belt adds to the life
of the belt because it resists cutting
and abrasion.
This cover is also waterproof, and because
rubber is forced into the fibre of the canvas at
enormous pressure the belt is impervious to
moisture throughout.
Ordinary belts wear out quickly at the edges
because of the friction against the troughing
rollers.
The Goodyear Conveyor Belt is provided
with a patent cushion edge to withstand the
hardest kind of frictional and cutting wear.
And the stretch has been taken out to the utmost
limit of sound practice.
There will be no loss of efficiency—of power —be-
cause of stretch.
The price may be higher, but for every shilling
expended you will get longer service—and better
service.
\
Put a section of Goodyear Belting into a Con-
veyor equipment and mark its solid lasting qualities
under the same service conditions in comparison
with any other belting of like size and weight.

“Cockaigne”: George Alexander, just
dead, began as draper’s assistant, like Tom-
linson, known as Walter Montgomery.
Though the singer died over three years
ago thd lawyers are still worrying over Nor-
dica’s will. The latest news is that a sale of
some of the diva’s jewels has been ordered by
Chancellor Walker, of New Jersey, “to meet
expenses incidental to the administration of
the estate.” As the value of the trinkets has
been officially appraised at £44,300, the job
is likely to last for some time yet.
“First Old Man”: These are strenuous
times for play-producers in Australia. All
of them, I note, are taking turns at grease-
painting their faces and producing themselves
before the footlights. E. W. Morrison, after
“Aladdin,” at Sydney
Palace.

several years of

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: James Patrick
West, over whom North Ash spoke affec-
tionate words of farewell last week in
Waverley Cemetery, was about the last of
our old school of actors. A native of
Parramatta, born Devine, in the mid-’fifties
he was associated with an amateur club
which gave perrormances in an iron building
in Sussex-street, between Market and Druitt
streets. About the same time Andy Dono-
hoe (subsequently re-christened Shiel
Barry by W. 11. Ford and J. P. West)
appeared as an amateur, and they became
pros, almost simultaneously. J. P. West
in his nearly sixty years had played a multi-
tude of parts —most of them parts that called
for lung power. In his later years he
prided himself greatly upon his perform-
ance of the cannibal in Alfred Dampier’s
“For the Term of His Natural Life” at the
old Gaiety; but he had really done better
things than that. Latterly when opportunity
offered (which was too rarely) the old actor
coached amateurs and gave lessons in elo-
cution and the dramatic art.
“Peter”: Muriel Starr with all her
“gyurls” was never adored like Nellie Bram-
ley, one of the Fuller heroines in Melbourne.
Every evening Nellie is rushed and is not
allowed to escape after merely spluttering
her thanks. She has to kiss all the babies
and distribute sweet words among the wait-
ing mammas, besides suffering the panting
proximity of a bodyguard all the way home
to her hotel. At the pub door there is an-
other protracted farewell, more dishevelled
speech, and more unanimous decision to em-
brace Ellen all together, followed by a final
wrench from the more exuberant who want
to chase her up to bed. It is a great life for
a star, but it is too reminiscent of her
struggles with the fast Roger Ilardface in
“The Clot on the Camisole” to give her the
pleasure of a new sensation.

Laura Roberts, with her husband, Charley
Taylor, and a small parcel of Australian
natives, leaves for America in the course of
a few days. Laura was Sarah in Bert
Bailey’s “On Our Selection,” and she has
handled many parts neatly and brightly and
piquantly. She and her husband (long con-
ductor of the Bailey-Grant orchestra, but
also an experienced vaudevillain) are going
into variety in New York.
“Snell”: The East Lynne Dramatic Co.
was filling a date in the course of its tri-
umphant progress through the smalls of
Western District (Vic.). The only avail-
able accommodation within the limits of the
finances of Hannibal Nasturtium, the gifted
actor-manager, was in the home of Augustus
Dunn, mixed farmer. Dunn farmed honey,
butter, eggs, fruit, spuds and maize, and the
greatest of these was eggs. Hens were all
over everyone, and right into everything.
Mr. 11. Nasturtium, in the hope of securing
a rebate on the board bill, gallantly pre-
sented Mrs. Augustus Dunn with a “stiff”
for “self and family,” assuming that the
family was all in sight at the time of writ-
ing. Judge the managerial amazement on
surveying the house that night to find
eleven Dunns occupying the front seats. The
Augustus Dunns had raked up all their near
and remote relations, and ushered them in
on the strength of that “self and family”
clause, and in Mrs. Dunn’s lap purred the
domestic cat, and next to Augustus Dunn
was perched his collie dog Spot. Cold with
fury and regardless of consequences, Hanni-
bal stepped out before the curtain. A com-
manding hand stilled the orchestra at the
small, portable piano, and then in his frost-
iest tones the actor-manager addressed the
Dunns. “I am afraid, Mr. Dunn,” he said,
“you have misunderstood me. When I gave
you that pass I imposed no limitations.
For God’s sake go and bring the chickens.”

Abanazar Lupino, pill vendor, and assistant

“Spreader”: At a Sydney suburban ’all
lately they dished up a farce called “Dockem-
street, Wo'olloomooloo.” It was billed as a
revue, and Jack Kearns made all the middle-
aged sports in the audience hark back to the
days when Rickards first got going in the
Garrick, now the Tivoli. Kearns sang a
song that Charlie Fanning first introduced
at .the Tivoli, “Wid me dook, wid me mud-
hook, wid me claw.” Some of the old Syd-
ney push dialect is introduced and makes a
big hit. But “I’ve chucked up the Push for
the Donah” was the Carbine of the lot.
. “T. 0.1.”: Word is wandering around
among the papers about a programme, on
exhibition somewhere, of the G. V. Brooke
performance in Sydney which started the
Captain Cook statue fund. The facts are
that in 1855 Brooke and Coppin agreed to
give one-third of the receipts of two even-
ings’ performances to start this fund. The
amount realised was £lOB. On his return
in the following year Brooke found that no
additions had been made to it, so he and Cop-
pin decided that the committee should hand
the amount, with interest added, in all £ll4,
to some charity. The Destitute Children’s
Asylum was selected and the amount handed
over.
“O.B.”: Thais Lawton, whom the Taits
are showing with Post in “The Masquerader”
next month, has a Portia to her credit that
haughty Boston vows is the best it has yet
inspected. Also her Beatrice in “Much
Ado” and her Rosalind in “As You Like It”
appear to have caused hoorays in Bhaks-
pearean shrines. Thais has done, in addition,
Paula Tanqueray, Dorothy A’ernon, Nell
Gwyn, Maslova (of Tolstoi’s “Resurrec-
tion” ) and several other modern heroines;
so she ought to he fully qualified for her
comparatively easy job in the version of
Katherine Thurston’s dead novel.
The improvements at Sydney Lyceum,
recently leased to Hoyt’s for picture pur-
poses, are designed on the lines of the Strand
Theatre, New York. It will have a mezza-
nine floor, or second circle, to be used as a
foyer. Downstairs floor is to be covered
with ruberoid, in the interests of silence.
In the completely-reformed house Hoyt’s will
instal a more-or-less musical instrument to
cost £8000; but if it is anything like the
machine at the same co.’s Mosman Ivinema it
will be a shame to take the money.
“8.J.” : Maggie Moore (much more nowa-
days) will in due course be introduced to the
pictures. H. R. Roberts is at present
at work on scenarios of “Struck Oil” and
“Judy O’Trot,” which will be photographed
when his wife has finished her present
engagement with the Muriel Starr co. Mean-
while the lady is compiling her memoirs.
“Peter”: I doubt “Ancient Prompter’s”
statement (B. 34/3/T8) that Nat Goodwin
“has been married and divorced more times
than any man on the boards.” Nat recently
met in N’York another well-known mummer
whose rushes to the altar and the Divorce
Court had been more numerous and sudden.
Nat gazed at his rival with great awe, and
then said in a voice hoarse with emotion :
“My God, Greasepaint, you are a glutton for
punishment.”
“The Ancient”: In Albury, back in ’7O.
“While London Sleeps” was being staged at
the School of Arts. Mrs. Holloway,/ the
heroine, was gently and pathetically depart-
ing this life, while the snow fell to soft music
about her. A close observer might have seen
that the snow was desiccated newspaper;
but the audience remained unenlightened
and sniffling, until an unfortunate slip
allowed a whole sheet of the Albury Banner
to flutter gently down upon the dying beauty.
“Sylvia D.”: Some of our Australian films
are crude things. The other night I went
to see one replete with all the usual thrills,
including a hand-to-hand encounter with a
shark. The acting at times degenerated
into burlesque, and frequent printed explana-
tions on the screen were necessary to enable
one to follow the plot. Now, people don’t go
to theatres to read ; and if our moving-pic-
ture industry is to boom our camera acting
and production must be improved until there’s
no need for words to tell the story.
TO DAPHNE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, Daphne has a tender heart
Most loving-kind, and yet she labors
By day and night with fiendish art
To agonise the frenzied neighbors;
For thickest walls are all too thin.
When Daphne plays the violin.
Not Torquemada and his band
Could be more lacking in compassion;
No plea for mercy stays her hand,
For violins are all the fashion;
With dauntless will and courage high
She’ll learn, though every neighbor die.
She lifts it lightly from its case,
Wee dimples through her roses flying,
And tucks the chin-piece into place,
With studious care each posture trying;
Then round her tiny hand she brings
And draws the bow across the strings.
And oh! the horrid sounds that rend
The shudd’ring air like demons yowling,
Or shrill, lugubrious draughts that wend
Through crooked chimneys wildly howling!
And ugh! The sudden scrapes that grate >
Like rusty nails upon a slate!
Some day she hopes to make it speak ;
Meanwhile, alas! we wilt and languish,
Unnerved by its appalling shriek,
In bitter throes of aural anguish.
Yet all who know her loudly cry:
“Dear Daphne wouldn’t hurt a fly!”
Sweet maid, whose steely soul belies
The softness of angelic features,
Withdraw from undeserving flies
That ruth and heed your fellow creatures.
Forbearance every charm endears;
You have our hearts, oh spare our ears!
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.

When an institution, almost from its in-
ception, takes the commanding position in its
field —
And when that institution maintains the
commanding position without interruption
•over a series of years^
Then you may be assured of that institu-
tion’s integrity—both as to its product and
its dealings withf the public.
From an annual output of 465 motor cars
nine years ago The Willys-Overland Com-
pany has grown steadily, and this year its
dealers have contracted for more than 200,000
motor cars.
Its net resources have grown m that time
from a little more than £lO,OOO to more than
£13,600,000.
This year the Willys-Overland factories
are building the most comprehensive range
of models ever -produced by any. one motor
car concern.
If yours is a family of average size, you
will be particularly • interested in either the
Big Four-cylinder Overland touring car, fin-
ished in a rich brown, or the Light Four
touring car, finished in gray. These two
models exemplify the remarkable values char-
acteristic of the entire group.
When you buy any motor car in the Willys-
Overland group you are assured by our com-
manding position in the industry—by our
unequaled facilities and resources—of integ-
rity of values in your motor car throughout

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Ajusdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
Why Have Grey Hair?
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes. A
very old, home-made remedy will turn the
hai» back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
two ounces of concentrate; of tammalite and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this simple lotion to the hair for a few nights
with a small sponge and you will soon have
the pleasure of seeing the greyness disappear.
This recipe is perfectly harmless, is neither
sticky nor greasy and has given perfect satis-
faction for many generations to those in
possession of the secret.
Blackheads, Oily Pores, etc.
The new sparkling face-bath treatment rids
the; skin of blackheads, oiliness and enlarged
pores almost instantly. It is perfectly harm-
less,*, pjeasant and immediately effective. All
yod ‘have to do is to drop a stymol tablet,
obtained from the chemist, in a glass of hot
water, and after the resulting effervescence
lias subsided dab the affected portions of the
face freely with the liquid. When you dry
the face you will find that the blackheads
come right off on the towel, the large pores
contract and efface themselves and the greasi-
ness is all gone, leaving the skin smooth, soft
and cool. This treatment should be repeated
a few times at intervals of several davs in
order to make sure that the result shall be
permanent.
The Magnetism of Beautiful Hair.
Beautiful hair adds immensely to the per-
sonal magnetism of both men and women.
Actresses and,smart women are ever on the
look out for any harmless thing that will in-
crease the natural beauty of their hair. The
latest method is to use pure stallax as a sham-
poo on account of the peculiar glossy, fluffy
and wavy effect which it leaves. As stallax
has never been used much for this purpose it
comes to the chemist only in Jib. sealed origi-
nal packages, enough for 25 or 30 shampoos.
A teaspoonful of the fragrant stallax gran-
ules, dissolved in a cup of hot water, is more
than sufficient for each shampoo. It is very
beneficial and stimulating to the hair apart
from its beautifying effect.
How to Remove a Bad Complexion.
Cosmetics can never really help a poor com-
plexion; often they are positively harmful,
ihe sensible, rational way is to actually re-
move the thin veil of stifling, half-dead scarf-
skin from the face, and give the fresh, vigor-
ous and beautiful young skin underneath a
chance to show itself and to breathe.
This is best done in a very simple way, by
merely applying mercolised wax at night, like
cold cream, and washing it off in the morning
Good mercolized wax can be obtained from
any well-stocked chemist. It absorbs the dis-
figuring cuticle gradually and harmlessly,
leaving a brilliant natural complexion. Of
course, this also takes with it all such facial
blemises as red blotches, tan, moth patches,
sallowness, liver spots, pimples, etc. As a
freckle remover and general complexion beau-
tiher this old-fashioned remedy is unequalled.
The Powder Puff Passes.
Every normal woman is always interested
m any plan by which she can quicklv enhance
her beauty, provided' the plan is perfectly safe
and simple. Ever since the discovery that
ordinary cleminite, such as any chemist can
supply, is an instantaneous and harmless
beautifier, there has been a growing demand
for it from smart women everywhere. They
usually get about an ounce and add just
enough water to dissolve it. A little of this
perfectly harmless lotion gives a beautiful
complexion to any face instantly. It renders
face powder quite unnecessary. Moreover, its
use cannot be detected. The skin is instantly
beautified but appears perfectly natural
under the closest scrutiny. [3

“Opal Ring”: The operator at Stringy-
bark received a telegram which was muti-
lated, and instead of sending the formal
memo., “Yours John Jones. Please supply
second text,” lie woke up the operator at
the Titree end Avith “Yours John Jones.
W hat happened to uncle?” Titree looked up
the message, and tapped back, “Uncle died.”
The same old policy of hide and shuffle !
The military anti-drink Commission sat at
Adelaide and learned this from a doctor who
had 12 mouths’ experience in charge of a
camp:—
Since 1916, 895 men were discharged- from the
camp as unfit. Alcohol accounted for eight of the
lost soldiers. More men were lost on account of
venereal disease than by reason of alcohol.
But while the figures for the Big Disease
were given, the figures for the Biggest Dis-
ease didn’t appear. Why must not the com-
munity know the facts?
“Quidam”: Russia is large in things
besides revolutions. Going to the mining
districts on the Lena one gets about 1500
miles from a railway station : but the gold is
there when you reach it. One million cubic
yards from the Blagoveschensky cut has
yielded £3,000,000, or £3 10s. Od. per cubic
yard, while the average value up and down
the river is about 30s. per yard. Working
conditions are very primitive; also Russia,
instead of installing drastic legislation to
prevent gold-stealing, on the lines of (say)
the Victorian Gold Buyers Act, provides
that companies must buy all “God-sent”
gold from miners. “God-sent” is a pleasant
way of putting it. A large part of the cos.’
expenditure goes in paying police to watch
the workmen. As there isn’t anybody to
watch the police, it probably costs more than
if the cos. took their chances.
“Ex-Englishman”: Would you believe that
anyone as unworldly as this could live?
My wife’s father is an English country vicar
and a staunch Blue-ribbon man (he doesn’t
like the name “teetotaller”). But his daugh-
ters are. not so particular, especially where
the Christmas dinner is concerned, and last
December they put into the sauce for the
plum-pudding a generous tablespoonful of
the proper flavoring. His wife writes, in
innocent glee at the deception—evidently eat-
ing the accursed stuff with a spoon must be
less sinful than drinking it—that father,
Avith no idea of what had been done, seemed
to like the unusual taste, and “actually had
two helpings of the b —y sauce.” Now, just
try to fancy the primitive simplicity of an
old lady who (1) thinks the mere name of
brandy too awful to be written in full, and
(2) has no glimmering of an idea that the
contraction she has chosen might possibly
be read otherwise.
“K.L.H.” : Though he has more than his
share of ignorant critics, the doctor is little
different from any other craftsman ; the most
successful is he who best satisfies his cus-
tomers by completely and expeditiously cur-
ing their ailments. Most ailments become
manifest to the patient himself before any
objective symptoms develop by which the
medical man could detect them ; so prophy-
lactic treatment or early treatment of the
disease depends upon the patient himself
seeking medical assistance before the disease
grips him. “A.H.AJs” attitude towards
operations (B. 28/2/’18) is characteristic of
a large section of the public. I have been
accused of being “too fond of the knife”
because I performed seven operations in the
first month I struck a certain country prac-
tice ; the 173 other cases seen and not
operated on were not heard of by my critics.
There is, hoAvever, a very real objection to
any man doing only lodge practice, and Mel-
bourne masses will soon experience it. The
man who confines himself to lodge practice
must have many families on his list to mane
a decent living; then, being rushed, he Avill
discourage people with minor ailments, with
the result that in many cases what should
have been treated as a bad cold yesterday
will be visited as pneumonia the day after
to-morrow.
“John Ketch”: Says the M.L. Times of
February 18 : —•
Apropos of the death sentence passed on William
Eggers McMahon. An Otago Daily Times reporter
was informed that the person who will officiate as
hangman is. a prosperous South Island farmer. He
acted in a similar capacity when Bennier was hanged
recently, and when he received the fee deducted his
expenses, and handed the balance to the Patriotic
Society in the district where the execution took place.
The public-spirited conduct of the hangman-
agriculturist of the South Island—a true
sportsman and an artist for art’s sake —
gives me hope that the reasonableness of a
suggestion Ayhich I have wanted to make
for a long time may lead to its adoption in
Australia. The pleasant spectacles which
take place from time to time at Long Bay,
Melbourne, Boggo-road. Adelaide. Hobart
and Fremantle Gaols, in the ea'rly forenoons
appointed for dislocating the necks of mal-
efactors, have hitherto been wastefully ex-
clusive. None but a few bored pressmen
and a handful of officials have been per-
mitted to witness them. What would be
the matter with arranging for executions to
take place in the Town Hall, charging 55.,
2s. and Is. for admission, and donating the
money realised to, let us say, the Lord
Mayor’s Patriotic Fund? When old Dar-
linghurst Gaol was thrown open for in-
spection, at a shilling a time, in 1914, some
30,000 citizens hastened to visit the joyous
place, and the gallows was the centre of
attraction. Photographs showed its plat-
form thronged with ladies, gentlemen and
dear little children. I am sure the Lord
Mayor would haA r e to put out “House Full”
long before the Sheriff gave his cue to the
hangman. The City Organist could supply
appropriate airs whilst the ceremony was
in progress—which would doubtless greatly
stimulate the gentleman playing the star
part, and would do away with that atmo-
sphere of gloom Avhieh pervades the twilight
of “B” Hall at Long Bay when a trapdoor
performance is staged. If the picture rights
were auctioned beforehand a further sub-
stantial sum might be raised. I would like
to say, in the event of this suggestion com-
mending itself to the authorities, that I
would be willing to give my services for
nothing, and would be glad to pay my own
expenses.

Heart of Empire Series—No. 9.
The Houses of Parliament,
on the site of the old Palace of
Westminster, were opened in 1847.
Designed by Sir Charles Barry,
R.A., in the Gothic Perpendicular
Style and noted throughout the
world for their symmetry and
beauty. The Royal or Victoria
Tower, 340 feet high, is the royal
entra ce to the House of Lords,
a magnificently decorated chamber.
The House of Commons, 70 feet
long, is also very handsome. Big
Ben was set in place in 1860.
Westminster Hall was built by
William Rufus and has been skil-
fully incorporated in the design.

* * * *
“E. J.M.”: A cheery little publication,
Round tiie Map, was produced on a hos-
pital ship which brought a recent cargo of
invalided men for all Australian ports. It
was edited by Sergeant Boyd (S. M.
Herald), and the drawings, which displayed
a very promising trick of caricature, were
done by Private Cyril Samuels (Evening
News). Near the Nelson Monument, while
on leave, Boyd met the late Major Hoskins,
of the 20th Battalion, to which he also
belonged. He was getting ready to salute,
when he noticed that Hoskins was without
the crown on his shoulders, so he said
“What’s this?” Hoskins replied: “My boy,
I’m having the time of my life as a simple
private.” Boyd eyed the Major and re-
marked, “Don’t you think as you’ve elected
to be a private that you should stand to
attention when talking to a sergeant?” Hos-
kins : “Quite right, my boy, and, according
to Regulations, a sergeant shouldn’t walk
down the street with a private? But, as we
are beastly Australians, we’ll do it.” Poor
Hoskins, beloved by his men, was killed at
Polygon Wood. Boyd got his ticket for Aus-
tralia at bloody Bullecourt.
* * * *
“P.X.”: You can’t keep these newspaper
men from the ink by putting stars on their
shoulders. One of them, a Sydney young-
ster—The Bulletin knows him very well —
helped to keep things moving with a paper
on the way to Egypt in 1915, produced
another while squatting in the sand by the
Canal, a third while his lot was in the Desert,
got a supply of paper at the Cape and
livened an invalids’ ship with a fourth, and, on
the way to France for his second round with
Fritz, produced his fifth- —all, of course, with
different titles suited to the circumstances.

* * * *
After 10 years’ service as cable man, C. S.
Panton has left the Melbourne Herald, with
the blessings of the editor-in-chief and a fat
cheque from the directors. Panton learned
his business in Sydney: as sub-editor on the
old Star he specialised on cables and knew'
foreign affairs as well as the liars at the
other end allow afiy man to. Panton has now
passed back to Sydney.
* * * *
“The Wanderer”: John Woollard. killed
in France, was a newspaper man w'hen he
enlisted. Though he was then pushing a pen
on_ the Northern Champion, Taree
(N.S.W.), he knew how to swing an axe
and steer a plough—w T as just such another
as the late Bob Pittendrigh, who, before he
mounted the Methodist pulpit, snatched type
on the Molong Argus for Fitzparrot, now
Holman’s Assistant-Treasurer.
* * * * * 0
“Umntu”: The Censor has put H. E. S.
Fremantle's Cape Town paper, The Inter-
preter. on the index, thereby doing honor
to S’Africa’s most impotent politician.
Fremantle, who, bj T the w r ay, belongs to the
family from which the port of Perth gets
its name, his uncle being the Admiral, is the
English exponent of the S’African brand of
Nationalism. The upholders of this Hertzog
type of the ’ism began the w r ar with “an
armed protest” and hope to end it with a
full-fledged S’African Republic. This move
for a republic gives Fremantle the time of
his life. But there is really no reason why
Hughes should exclude the amusing little
man.
CRAYFISH AND GIRL.
(In disrespectful imitation of some modern verse.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A little moon, with wink and nod,
Was dancing on the waves.
(After three drinks, it’s really odd,
The way the moon behaves.)**
It shone upon the midnight sea,
Whose wavelets turned to pearl,
And spared a curious glance for Me,
The Crayfish and the Old
We knew we’d missed the latest tram.
We knew the sands were wet.
We did not care a little damn,
But lit a cigarette.
The pink girl smoked Abdullah brand*,
I had a common fag.
(The crayfish slumbered on the sand,
Inside a paper bag.)
And nothing happened, right or wrong;
We did not even kiss.
It starts all right, your crayfish song,
But fades away like this.
Poor puppets of a phantom race,
When canny pressmen call,
We fill so-many lines of space.
We were not real at all l
You’ve heard that story lots of times;
But, while you stand the strain,
And swallow anything that rhymes,
I’ll sing the same again.
Yes, till my brain all work has struck,
I’ll hand you what you wish,
And afterwards, as well, with luck,
The Damsel and the Fish.
I never sit on midnight sands
(Rheumatic gout forbids) ;
I never hold a damsel’s hands
(I have a wife and kids) ;
I do not care for things like these;
I loathe the taste of cray;
What matter, so my piffle please
The fancy of the day?
SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
S. TALBOT SMITH.
Yance Palmer, one of the most-travelled
of Australian inky-wayfarers, whose fine
“Easter” verses grace the Plain English
page of this issue, has enlisted in Melbourne.
He wanted to do it before; but he had a wife
and two babies to consider. The wife was
formerly Nettie Higgins, and she has written
graceful verse, too.

IMPORTANT.—LOOK TO YOUR TEETH.
There are no teeth like those furnished by Nature. For service, comfort and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

Youth’s Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE’S a Red Road, and a hard road,
Where many pilgrims throng ;
And they who made that Red Road
Have done the world a wrong.
For the young man and the brave man
There is no other way ;
They sing adown its red length,
And find the going gay.
Yet the green road, and the smooth road
We trod ourselves in youth,
Is not for them ; for the Red Road
AVas made for them, in truth.
From the long street, and the bush track,
And old familiar scenes,
They haste away to the Red Road,
And all the Red Road means.
And ’tis up hill, and ’tis down hill,
It keeps its forthright track ;
Though hands wave, and the heart sigh,
There is no turning back.
And ’tis footworn, and ’tis tear-wet,
And stretches wan and wide,
And many a young and fond heart
Is breaking by its side.
Like a dull rain and a low wind
At night on limb and leaf,
The tears sound and the sobs rise
Of women bowed by grief.
Nor the fine word nor the fair word
Of how they fought and fell
Will take the tears from the sad eyes
Of those who love so well.
Yet the bright youths, and the brave youths,
Though night and darkness fall,
When all ends on the Red Road,
They have no grief at all.
’Tis the olcL folk, and the fond folk,
That bear the grievous load—
A black curse on the black hearts
That made that cruel road !
Oh, the big men and the proud men
Who did this crying crime,
’Tis hate, sure, and a world’s hate,
They’ll bear all time !
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Song of the Sword.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[Both Irish and Scottish Gaelic traditions have
many references to lost song's of magical power.]
The great room of a small castle in Ireland.
The walls are of rough stone, broken only
by three long, narrow windows set close
together. There are settles and stools about
the room, and near the windows is a large
oaken table. At this Derniot is seated with
pens and parchments. He is engrossed in
his work and does not notice either the
movements of people in the castle nor the
noise of battle without.
Phelim and Moran come in.
Phelim : It is mad he is, surely, with bend-
ing too long over old songs, and him not
heeding the Sasenach outside.
Moran : Will we try to say a word to him
t° get him away from the books and the
writing? _,lf he would put on his sword
it is great .fighting he would make below
the gate.
Phelim : Con is there and 50 with him, hold-
ing them back to keep them from
putting fire to the walls; but if Dermot
would go there himself, it is his sword
would break them altogether.
Moran ( going doubtfully to Dermot) :
There is hard fighting at the gate and
some men killed. Will you not leave the
words you want to write for a short while,
for ’tis you have the great arm?
Dermot : I will go down surely soon. There
is a word to ket here, and a thought that
came to me in the first light of the morn-
ing to write down for singing; but it will
soon be done.
He bends again over the parchment and
Moran walks slowly away.
Phelim : ’Tis no use speaking a word
to him, for he will have his own way if we
are all killed with the place burning on
us. What noise is that?
Hour men come in bearing a dead body
under a, blood-stained cloth. They set the
body down on the ground. Dermot does
not turn his head. Croghan comes in and
stands with bent head looking down at the
body. Then he strides to Dermot.
Croghan : It is Con that is killed below the
gate and the men not able to stand against
the Sasenach.
Dermot : It is a word or two that I must
put down.
Croghan : Is it mad you are to sit with a
look on you and me going back to die
while you put ink on a foolish bit of skin?
Dermot : It will be done soon.
Croghan : It will be done soon, surely. It
will be done with long arrows and hot
fire; and this place your father built
strong broken down and the cattle driven
and us with no name at all in the whole
world.
Dermot : I will be down to the fighting
when it is done; and it will keep me
from doing it if you stay here talking all
the long day.
Croghan : Let every man follow me back to
the fighting. It is Dermot that will not
lead us, though it is his own walls they
would burn.
Theft all go out. Dermot still bends over
his work. Outside the noise increases and
shouts are heard close at hand. Dineen, a
very old man, comes in, leaning on a stick.
He pauses to look at the body of Con and
goes on to Dermot, behind whose chair he
stands. Dermot works on unheeding.
Dineen : It is a black day and men dying.
Have you no sword that you hold nothing
but a goose feather?
Dermot ( starting) : Och, Dineen, it was
your voice behind me broke the sound of
a line. It is this word I must have and
no other. Do you go away now and I
will be down very soon to the fighting.
Dineen goes out slowly and Dermot bends
again over the table. An. arrow flics in at
the window and passes over his shoulder,
but he does not heed it. The shouting out-
side increases. A great stone strikes the
wall without and shakes the building. Der-
mot leaps to his feet.
Dermot : Is it no quiet a man can have at
all? Three times was the word that came
to me on the point of my pen and three
times has it gone away.
He sits down again, his head bowed
between his hands. Dineen comes in with
Murna. She is ■a beautiful young girl, but
her face is pale and. her disordered hair falls
wildly about her shoulders. Dineen stands
back leaning on his stick while Murna runs
to Dermot.
Murna : Och, Dermot, why are you sitting
with a pen, and them going to burn the
house over us? There are men dead below
and blood on the stones and the arrows
shooting in. It is you only can save us.
Dermot : Whisht, little sister. I nearly have
the word, and it the great word in all
the world. It is the song Amergin made
in far days and that Caithbad lost in a
river valley of Antrim. It is the i great
song, surely, if I can get that word.
Murna: Och, what song would it be?
Dermot : It is the Song of the Sword that
is more than all the swords of the world ;
and it is the one word I need to say it
in the faces of the Sasenach.
Murna : It is the fire I smell and the one
word of death that is come to us all.
Dermot : I have it! I have it!
Murna: Och, look out and see. It is your
sword you must get, or it will be too
late. V
She runs to the ivindow.
Dermot (rising with the parchment in his
hand ) : I have it! I have it!
Murna : Och, Dermot! ,
She falls back from the window, an arrow
in her breast.
Dermot : The curse upon their Black hearts!
They have killed my little sister. Murna,
Murna!
He takes the dying girl in his arms, then
lays her back gently on the long settle.
Dineen : Och, the black day! .
Dermot : The curse upon their black hearts!
Where is my sword?
He rushes wildly out.
Phelim comes in wounded.
Phelim : Where is Dermot?
Dineen : He is away to get his sword.
Phelim : Bad cess to the Sasenach. They
have my arm cut, and me not able to close
my hand on the hilt. It is a grand day
surely, and him with his foolish song not
there when we needed him.
DiNeen : It is the song that he has now,
surely—the Song of the Sword that Amer-
gin made and Caithbad lost in Antrim. It
is the song that makes the foe fall down
and takes the point off the long arrows.
. He will say that word at the gate, and
there will be no more fighting.
Phelim : Is it the Song of the Sword surely ?
Dineen : It is the lost words he has found.
Phelim : Och, then it is a good day; for
they are making the fires now and the
arrows as thick as leaves blowing before
the first of the snow.
Dermot comes in armed. He goes over to
the body of Murna.
Dermot : Little sister, there will be blood for
your blood and a great killing for the thing
that is done this day. I am going out
with my sword to the gate.
Dineen : It is the song—the song! Where
are the words that are found again?
Phelim : Och, Dermot, the Song of the
Sword—where is it?
Dermot : Is it words I would go out with
to them that in black shame killed my
little sister? It is the blood on her breast
calls for a s\yord and not a song.
He goes out. The noise of the fighting in-
creases. A glow of fire is seen through the
ivindow.
Dineen : He will have his own way with all
men of every sort. It is sore wounds and
death he will be making now with the
Sasenach.
Phelim : There is a smell of burping coming
from the west side. The fighting is going
hard, and me with my arm cut.
Dineen : It is sorry I am he would not take
the Song of the Sword with him, for it is
a great thing to have the words that will
do more than all the fighting.
Phelim : I would count his own sword as
good as any old song, and it made by
Amergin himself.
Dineen : He has a great arm, and the voice
of him in battle is like the sound of the
loud sea.
Phelim : It is a great way he has and no
fear at all; but my heart is sore that he
lost the good time finding the song that he
would not say.
Dineen ( walking to the table) : Could we
find the words that he has written?
Four men bear in the body of Dermot. It
is covered in a cloth that is red with blood.
They set it on the floor beside the body of
Con, and Croghan staggers in and kneels
beside it. He is wounded in the head and
side.
Croghan : It was a great sword, surely, and
it cutting swathes of men like oats in the
full harvest.
Dineen : It is the Song of the Sword if I
could find it to say the words.
He fumbles among the narchments on the
table. The glare of the fire increases and the
smoke blows throiigh the roorp.
Croghan : It is a prayer that is better to
say, for the fire is at us on every side.
Dineen : No, but I have it! I have it!
Croghan : Well, read it to the fire. It will
maybe listen.
The flames break through. Dineen falls
coughing in the smoke.

Insolvents.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WOLSELEY AVENUE is a handsome
name, but Wolseley Avenue is in
reality a most unhandsome tho-
roughfare. It is narrow and grimy, with an
unnecessary bend in its middle, as though, at
some early stage of its career, it had been
kicked in the stomach by a mere street. One
side of the Avenue is lined with cottages
which hug one another for support. Privacy
Avas left out of the builder’s calculations, for
anyone standing on the 3xlo verandah of
No. 36 can look straight down the row to the
frayed doormat of No. 12. If so disposed,
the vendors of milk and other necessaries of
life mav walk straight up the line of veran-
dahs, for the dividing fences are less than
three feet high. Wolseley’s youngsters, in-
deed, know of no other mode of transhipment
than by vaulting these absurd hurdles from
A to Z. '
Great cracks traverse the grimy brick
faces of Dahlia Row further on. Green
patches border their foundations. Their
iron gates, set in crumbling concrete, creak
rustily at the touch of every hand. Facing
Dahlia Row is the skimpiest, meanest, ugli-
est nightmare of a terrace.ever born in the
brain of a money-grabbing jerrybuilder. Yet
it has individuality at least in name, as
witness “Floraville,” “The Elms,” “Irene,”
“The Anbhorage.” One facing a dusty right-
of-way, wherein you may always rely on
finding at least one dead rat, bears the label
“Fairview.” Another seems to bear the bar
sinister —it is nameless; it stands back a
couple of feet as if in a mutinous mood. Or
perhaps a homing drunk shoved it astern with
his latchkey whilst lunging at the Yale lock.
Anyhow, by its step back it gained a few
extra inches in front, and in that fluffy strip
of dust a double-stemmed palm grows dustily.
The twin trunks rise in crooked unity, with
long, untidy strips of dead fibre rustling
miserably about them. It bears an absurd
resemblance to a bow-legged Afghan in a
ragged petticoat and a tattered turban.
It is a brooding street which seems always
to be awaiting the Crack of Doom. Yet, as
a plain matter of fact, Doomsday is nothing
to the precarious and warlike Wolselevites.
They await much greater calamities—the bad-
debt collector, the time-payment fiend, the
coining or the van which is . to carry away
the furnishings .ere the agent can seize them.
When in a hopeful mood they dream vaguely
of “a stroke of luck,”' “a lift” or “a loan.” .
To the casual and unsympathetic eye life
in this street- must seem a tedious horror.
Yet its habitues have excitements and diver-
sions quite unknown to the solvent. Take
Monday, for instance. During the morning
hours every Wolseleyite worthy of the name
experiences the thrill the rabbit gets when lie
hears the hysterical yelps of. the dog that is
scratching madly at the entrance of its bur-
row. For Monday is the field-day of the
dun. Out of the dusty hurricane of trade
that sweeps the main street, different samples
of dun wheel into the Avenue’s mournful and
Suspicious calm. First comes one grim of
eye and ruthless of jaw. lie is ’“the Kent,”
and, if money can be drawn from an insol-
vent Wolseieyite, he is the man for the
operation. Some can evade payment no
longer: they meet him with dog-eared rent-
hooks and surrender the coin reluctantly, as
commanders of captured fortresses tender
their useless swords. But where evasion is
still being practised, “the Kent,” after bat-
tering ruthlessly oil immovable front doors,
seavchingly tours the barricaded hack regions,
where the besieged are sitting tight and hold-
ing • their breath, lueu. returning to the
front, he sometimes affixes a notice to quit to
the door and retreats in good order, leaving
the unlinaneial wretches inside to think over
and curse the situation.
Other duns are going the rounds by now,
briskly rattling the knockers, tugging the
bell-handles and sliding in horrible slips of
paper covered with final-notice threats and
pathetic appeals. When at last there comes
a lull, the beleaguered garrisons heave sighs
of relief and start to move about again like
free human beings. Some, greatly daring (or
perhaps they are throwing up the sponge),
come out to “do” the verandahs with old
brooms curled up at their business ends like
the moustaches of a dragoon, and get un
neighborly discussions with other human mice
about the neighbors who are still under lock
and key. These poor wretches, sneaking out
presently to remove the shameful “notice to
quit” proclamations from the doors, are
neatly caught by the wily “Kent” wheeling
suddenly back on his quarry from some
hiding-place round the corner. When the.
Monday enemy is expected, even the harm-
less non-combatant gets no quarter from the
Wolseieyite. And it is the bad hour of
Monday morning when a smart young man
with a leather bag and a spruce “Do It
Now” air invades the street. He is' of the
Gas Company’s staff, and his monthly col-
lecting-day happens this time to coincide
with that of the dreaded “Kent.” So his
cheerful rat-tat-tat fails to rouse anyone.
Blinds everywhere are down, windows sealed,
children muzzled. The few playing about iu
the dry gutters have but one answer to every
inquiry relating to the lady of the house :
“She ain’t in.”
“I’m not ‘the Rent,’ sonny,” says Do It
Now in his kindest manner. But the kid
only stares at him. He turns and bellows
through the keyhole, ‘'Gas!” There is no
response.
“Tell your mother that ‘Gas’ is waiting,
will you, Sis ?.... Here ! You can see for
yourself that I’m only the Gas !” He opens
and shows his bag of pence as guarantee, and
the satisfied kid hurries away to procure him
his passport. At last news of his identity
filters through the street, and he is given the
freedom of the kitchens and allowed to wipe
his hair on the grimy hall curtains. As fast
as possible, for he is a fastidious young man,
he empties the slots of their month’s pence,
relocks them, gives one horrified glance at
tlxe unwashed dishes and the grime, and bolts
for the open. But at the faded door of
Maida Vale the angel of light experiences a
setback.
The Peter who bars the portal of this
earthly paradise is an oily person whose
shirt consists mostly of button. ''She ain’t
in,” he growls, indicating the smoky interior
of the den. "An’ I’m re-sponsible and carn’t
let anybody through.”
Bo It Now mechanically gives his pass-
word, ‘ Gas!”
‘‘You might be,” agrees the oily man. “On
the other ’and, you might be c’lecting for the
time-payments, or you might be the chap
about th’ di’mond ring. ’Twasn’t with no
consent o’ mine she took it on.”
As the oilv one seems doubtful and still
disinclined to shift, he is suddenly shouldered
aside and the brisk collector reaches the
kitchen, where, as certain awful fumes have
already proclaimed, “she” is most flagrantly
“in” and scorching chops on the breakfast
cooker.
As swiftly as possible he does his duty to
his employers, replaces the slot against a
background of ragged wallpaper, and bolts for
the door. II is head appears presently over
the dividing fence of “next door.” which he
has discovered to be empty. The vanished
tenant has rifled the slot and left no clue.
Do It Now desires most earnestly to track
them. lie tries to apply the third degree to
the initiates’ of Maida Vale ; but those bandits
withdrawing swiftly one by one, it proves a
one-sided and unsatisfactory proceeding. So,
with a philosophic “Nothing doing!” he goes
his way.
But not through loyalty to the defaulters
has Maida Vale remained dumb ; the simple
explanation is that another dun with a very
square jaw is.battering at the front door. He
may be the diamond ring arrears, or possibly
he is the enlarged photograph Nemesis. So
the. ragged refugees, eat their scorched pieal
while the foe batters impotently.
“Seems an impatient kind of bloke,” mut-
ters the oily man as he gnaws. “Ain’t ’s if
we’d owed it long neither.”
“Owed wot long?” whispers his unkempt
wife.
“I dunno! Th’ di’mond ring, or th’ kid's
insurance, or me suit, or whatever ’e’s c’lect-
ing for!” And they chew in silence while
the-man outside tries his strength and pati-
ence with the knocker.
Do It Now, who has inquired tirelessly
since he left “next door,” presently gets wind
of the slot thieves from Mrs. Sam Saunders,
a vast and voluble lady of color. “It’s a
thing as I wouldn’t stoop to meself, nor
Saunders neither, though ’e is in the plumber-
ing hne,” says Mrs. Sam virtuously. “Oh.
but they was a queer lot! not the sort as I
care to mix with, though I hope not un-
neighborly.” Then the eager gas angel is
asked to take an interest in young Sam’s
horoscope. “I’ve put him to the bricklay-
ering, ”. says his mother, who- seems to have
a passion for collecting odd “so
that byu'mby ’im an’ ’is ole man can be
parteners.”
Young Sam, with a face like a black pansy
and feet like tennis racquets, indicates Mrs.
Farnan, two doors down, as one likely to
supply latest news from the front regarding
the thieves. “ ’Er ole man lent ’em ’is ’and-
cart ter shift,” he says informatively. How-
ever, it turns out that Mrs. Farnan who
up to now has been visibly framed in an
open window and surrounded with babes,
like some submerged Madonna framed in a
cloud of smutty cherubim, objects to turn
informer, and" slams down the window almost
on the chin of the inquiring collector.
Meanwhile the prowling duns harry the
shiftless and goad the insolvent. Several
meeting by chance upon a doorstep make
bets as to their chances of getting payment
from those within. The sad gig of the time-
payment undertaker stands empty while his
black collector nags for instalments over-
due upon the funeral of some unfortunate
one who has for ever escaped the day of the
earthly dun.
. By midday the strain slackens, for even
duns must dine. Then the Wolseleyites
rejoice and breathe again. Peeping from
behind dusty blinds, they see the Avenue
empty save for the time-payment sewing-
machine canvassers, who, seemingly, needing
neither food nor rest, thrust greedy faces
into any house that is opened to them, and
do their villainous best to lure their fasci-
nated listeners further into the quagmire of
debt.
Next to enter the Avenue of the hopeless
are three suburban she-saints.- They pause
at No. 20 Dahlia Row. No. 20, who looks
well nourished, has but a poor tale to tell—
“hard times,” “him” discouraged, children
“that sick.” She has scruples about admit-
ting the visitors, but they will not be denied.
No. .20 has accepted the two-pound loaf of
charity, therefore she has no right to privacy.
Such nonsense!
But No. 20 does not object to them enter-
ing the ruinous place on the score of pride.
She has all the cunning of the experienced
cadger and merely holds them up while “tli’
boss” hides,his sporting paper and the beer,
and assumes the proper careworn look of the
deserving unemployed. She talks loudly so
that fast scouts may be sent out to head off
'Myrtle and May. who are now entering the
Avenue with a basket of pickles and tinned
dainties, bought with a win at last night's
two-up, a gamble in which “th’ boss” excels.
While No. 20 is fighting her rearguard
action the tenants at Ozone are making pre-
parations for flight this very night before
the baffled agent shall have regained his wind
tor to-morrow's bombardment. When neces-
sity arises, the Wolseleyites rival the tent-
folding Arab in the quality of silence. Is
“the Rent” importunate? Is- local credit
quite Then, hey presto! up and
away! “Real” walnut sideboards- are
hoisted softly upon handcarts; familv
enlargements are lifted from the places of
honor; bedding, tied up in table covers, is
bumped along the hall, and, without beat of
drum, the owner turns the key in the lock
and is swallowed up in the unknown.

“Nerves”
Our nerves are similar to an intri-
cate network of telegraph wires.
Controlled and nourished by a
portion of the brain —known as
the nerve centres —the delicate
threadlike nerves radiate in all
directions throughout the body.
So long as the nerve centres are
capable of continually supplying
nourishment to the nerves, the
nerves will remain strong and
healthy. But directly the nerve
centres become weakened by
overwork, worry or anxiety, they
are unable to transmit the
necessary nourishment, and the
nerves become worn out and
“on edge.” Then it is that a sud-
den sound makes you “ jump ”
—you get irritable —you suffer
from neuralgia—you are rest-
less and depressed. In this con-
dition there is nothing to equal
